summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered a new role for the Notch signaling pathway in promoting cell-fate reprogramming in the C. elegans germline. Notch signaling is typically associated with maintaining undifferentiated germline stem cells/progenitors, but in this study, it was found to facilitate the reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells. This process requires chromatin perturbation, and the researchers used a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes to uncover the underlying mechanisms.

     The study found that Notch signaling activates specific genes that are silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PRC2). PRC2 is a chromatin regulator that maintains gene silencing, and the researchers identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial target of Notch signaling that facilitates reprogramming. UTX-1 is a key regulator of gene expression, and its activation by Notch signaling allows for the reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells.

     The researchers used tissue-specific expression profiling to identify genes activated by Notch signaling in the C. elegans germline. They found that many of these genes were also repressed by PRC2, suggesting an antagonistic relationship between Notch signaling and PRC2. The study also found that Notch-activated genes were disproportionately linked to the X chromosome, which is largely silenced by PRC2 in the C. elegans germline.

     The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of development and diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling. Notch signaling plays a critical role in many developmental processes, and its dysregulation has been implicated in various diseases, including cancer and neurodegenerative disorders. The discovery of its role in promoting cell-fate reprogramming in the C. elegans germline provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying these processes and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies.
Summary
     The study investigated the role of the adaptor protein SKAP2 in host defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumoniae) infection. K. pneumoniae is a pathogen that causes respiratory, blood, liver, and bladder infections. The researchers found that SKAP2 is essential for protection against K. pneumoniae pulmonary infections in mice. Mice lacking SKAP2 (Skap2-/-) had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice. SKAP2 expression in innate immune cells controlled the bacterial burden. Skap2-/- neutrophils and monocytes were present in infected lungs, but the neutrophils failed to produce reactive oxygen species (ROS) in response to K. pneumoniae infection in vitro. ROS production in response to K. pneumoniae required the activity of several proteins, including Src family kinases (SFKs), spleen tyrosine kinase (Syk), Bruton's tyrosine kinase (Btk), phospholipase C gamma 2 (PLCÎ³2), and protein kinase C (PKC). The loss of SKAP2 hindered the K. pneumoniae-induced phosphorylation of SFKs, Syk, and Pyk2, indicating that SKAP2 is proximal to their activation in pathogen-signaling pathways. The study suggests that SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils is crucial for K. pneumoniae-activated ROS production and promoting bacterial clearance during infection. Additionally, the researchers found that K. pneumoniae is resistant to neutrophil phagocytosis regardless of SKAP2 expression, but can elicit SKAP2-independent degranulation during infection. ROS is an essential mechanism for protection against K. pneumoniae, as mice lacking the gp91phox component of NADPH oxidase (Cybb-/-) were more susceptible to K. pneumoniae infection. The study demonstrates that SKAP2-expressing neutrophils contribute to the pulmonary containment of K. pneumoniae in lungs independent of inflammatory monocytes. Overall, the findings highlight the importance of SKAP2 in host defense against K. pneumoniae infection.
Researchers have developed a mathematical model to understand the role of the hypnozoite reservoir in the transmission of Plasmodium vivax malaria. The hypnozoite reservoir refers to the latent liver-stage parasites that can reactivate weeks to months after the initial infection, causing new blood-stage infections. This reservoir is distinct from the parasite reservoirs in the blood circulation and mosquito.

The model, which simulates the within-host dynamics of hypnozoites, predicts that the reservoir is over-dispersed, with many individuals having few or no hypnozoites, while others have intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypnozoites are more likely to experience relapses and contribute to the transmission of P. vivax. The model also suggests that incorporating hypnozoite-killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens can significantly reduce P. vivax transmission by targeting individuals with the most hypnozoites.

The researchers validated their model against data from tropical strains of P. vivax and embedded it in a P. vivax transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypnozoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypnozoite activation and death. The model provides valuable insights into the impact of malaria control interventions on the parasite's reservoirs in the vector, blood, and liver.

The study highlights the importance of considering the hypnozoite reservoir in P. vivax transmission models. Most existing models of malaria transmission do not account for this reservoir, but it has been incorporated into some P. vivax transmission models as a state to denote hypnozoite infection or as up to two broods of hypnozoites. The researchers' within-host model of hypnozoites in liver hepatocytes demonstrates that many epidemiological patterns of relapse infections can be explained by assuming that hypnozoites activate and die at a constant rate.

Mathematical models of malaria transmission that account for P. vivax relapses can provide valuable insights into the effectiveness of interventions that target the hypnozoite reservoir. In the absence of effective diagnostics for detecting liver-stage parasites, models will play a crucial role in estimating and predicting the effectiveness of interventions that target the hypnozoite reservoir, either indirectly via vector control and blood-stage anti-malarials or directly via primaquine treatment.

Summary
     Researchers have identified two distinct populations of neural stem cells in the adult mouse forebrain: neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) and quiescent neural stem cells (NSCs). NICs, referred to as GEPCOT cells, are highly mitotic, short-lived, and capable of forming neurospheres that undergo multilineage differentiation. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells are quiescent, long-lived, and unable to form neurospheres but express stem cell markers such as Slc1a3-CreERT, GFAP-CreERT2, Sox2CreERT2, and Gli1CreERT2.

Using flow cytometry, GEPCOT cells were identified as GlastmidEGFRhighPlexinB2highCD24â/lowO4/PSA-NCAMâ/lowTer119/CD45â cells, accounting for 3.2 Â± 0.7% of all subventricular zone (SVZ) cells. On average, 36 Â± 6% of GEPCOT cells formed neurospheres, and 74% of those neurospheres underwent multilineage differentiation. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed higher levels of Glast and lower levels of EGFR and PlexinB2.

Fate-mapping with Ascl1CreERT2 and Dlx1CreERT2 revealed that GEPCOT cells were highly mitotically active in vivo but persisted only transiently in the SVZ. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were long-lived in the SVZ. Conditional deletion of the Bmi-1 polycomb protein depleted both pre-GEPCOT and GEPCOT cells, although pre-GEPCOT cells were more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdkn2a (p16Ink4a) repression.

These findings distinguish quiescent NSCs from NICs and make it possible to study their properties in vivo. The identification of GEPCOT cells as NICs and pre-GEPCOT cells as quiescent NSCs provides new insights into the biology of neural stem cells in the adult mouse forebrain.
Biomedical researchers have investigated the properties of Piezo1 ion channels, which are responsible for sensing mechanical forces in various cell types and tissues. Using cryo-electron microscopy, the structure of Piezo1 was found to be bowl-shaped and capable of inducing membrane curvature. This suggests that Piezo1 ion channels may interact functionally and influence each other's spatial distribution. To examine this further, the researchers used cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp stimulation to study Piezo1 ion channels in cells expressing the protein at low and high levels.

The study found that the density of Piezo1 ion channels had no effect on their pressure sensitivity or open probability, even at non-physiological densities that would cause significant overlap between channels. This suggests that Piezo1 channels inherently behave as independent mechanotransducers, meaning they can function independently of each other. The researchers propose that this property is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire cell membrane.

The study's findings are significant because they show that Piezo1 channels do not have a strong tendency to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity. This property is ideal for cells to transduce mechanical forces with high spatial homogeneity. The researchers used stochastic simulations to support their findings, which showed that Piezo1 ion channels at densities likely to be physiologically relevant inherently function as independent mechanotransducers.

Cells are constantly exposed to mechanical forces, and the rapid sensing of these forces is achieved by force-gated ion channels that convert mechanical energy into electrochemical signals. Piezo1 is a mechanically gated cation channel that directly senses membrane tension with high sensitivity. The study aimed to quantify the spatial distribution and potential functional interactions of Piezo1 ion channels across multiple orders of channel densities. The researchers used electrophysiology and large numbers of independent measurements to simultaneously quantify channel number and function, including single-channel current, open probability, and pressure sensitivity.

Overall, the study provides new insights into the properties of Piezo1 ion channels and their role in sensing mechanical forces in cells. The findings suggest that Piezo1 channels can function independently of each other, which is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire cell membrane.
Biomedical researchers investigated the effect of evidence volatility on decision-making in a perceptual task. They used a novel sensory manipulation to mimic the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex, which was verified with neuronal recordings from macaque areas MT/MST. The study aimed to test the predictions of a bounded evidence accumulation model, which suggests that an increase in noise should lead to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and higher confidence.

The researchers applied the manipulation to a well-studied motion task and recorded from neurons in the visual cortex of the macaque to verify the effect of the manipulation. They found that increasing the noise induced faster decisions and greater confidence in both humans and monkeys, with minimal impairment in accuracy. The magnitude of the effects was consistent with predictions of the bounded evidence accumulation model.

The study demonstrated that a stimulus manipulation that increases the variance of the momentary evidence bearing on a decision, or volatility, increases both the speed of the decision and the confidence associated with it. The researchers used a reaction-time version of the random-dot motion discrimination task to study the relationship between volatility and decision speed. Three human participants and one macaque completed a large number of trials, and the results showed that increasing the noise led to faster decisions and greater confidence, with minimal impairment in accuracy.

The study's findings support the bounded evidence accumulation model, which provides a framework for understanding the joint effect of volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence. The results have implications for our understanding of decision-making processes in the brain and may have applications in fields such as neuroscience, psychology, and artificial intelligence. The study's use of a novel sensory manipulation and neuronal recordings from the visual cortex provides a unique insight into the neural mechanisms underlying decision-making.
summary
     The insulin-like signaling pathway in Caenorhabditis elegans maintains a relatively short lifespan by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF-16, the ortholog of the FOXO transcription factors in mammalian cells. DAF-16 is phosphorylated by AKT kinases, preventing its nuclear translocation. Calcineurin, a PP2B phosphatase, also limits the lifespan of C. elegans, but its mechanism of action was previously unknown. This study reveals that TAX-6â¢CNB-1, the C. elegans Calcineurin ortholog, and UNC-43, the C. elegans Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase type II (CAMKII) ortholog, regulate lifespan through DAF-16. UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16, promoting its nuclear localization, while TAX-6â¢CNB-1 dephosphorylates and inactivates DAF-16. The phosphorylation of DAF-16 at S286 by UNC-43 is removed by TAX-6â¢CNB-1, leading to DAF-16 inactivation. Mammalian FOXO3 is also regulated by CAMKIIA and Calcineurin. The study shows that UNC-43 and TAX-6â¢CNB-1 regulate C. elegans lifespan through the reversible phosphorylation of DAF-16. The direct, antagonistic nature of these two enzymes suggests that they would constitutively negate each other and have essentially no control of DAF-16. However, the insulin signaling pathway appears to dominate in conditions where there is a conflict between IIS and CAMKII/Calcineurin signaling toward DAF-16. The study provides new insights into the regulation of lifespan in C. elegans and highlights the complex interplay between multiple signaling pathways, including insulin/IGF-1 signaling, germline signaling, mitochondrial signaling, and the signaling pathways induced by dietary restriction.
Scientists have long been interested in understanding how the nervous system organizes motor sequences, which are formed through the serial execution of different movements. To address this question, researchers studied how fruit flies groom their bodies with sequential movements. They found that the motor programs responsible for cleaning individual body parts are organized into a suppression hierarchy, where the motor program that occurs first suppresses those that occur later. This means that cleaning one body part reduces the sensory drive to its motor program, which in turn relieves suppression of the next movement, allowing the grooming sequence to progress down the hierarchy.

Using genetically targeted activation of neural subsets, the researchers were able to drive distinct motor programs that clean individual body parts. They discovered that dust on the body elicits different independently evoked cleaning motor programs for five body parts that progress in series. By controlling each cleaning motor program separately, the researchers demonstrated that they are organized into a suppression hierarchy.

The researchers also developed a computational model that shows how a suppression hierarchy can mediate the sequential selection of the cleaning motor programs. This model suggests that the evolutionarily ancient ability to select among competing behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided the necessary infrastructure to perform motor actions in sequence. The study provides the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by the prevailing model for generating human action sequences.

The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of how the nervous system organizes serial behavior. The researchers anticipate that identifying the neural circuitry for each cleaning module and determining how these circuitries interact will reveal how the serial organization of grooming behavior is encoded in the nervous system through a hierarchical suppression mechanism. This knowledge may also shed light on the neural basis of other motor sequences, such as those involved in human actions.

The study used a combination of genetic and computational approaches to investigate the organizational principles underlying the neural control of serial action selection in an innate behavior common to most limbed animals, grooming. The results demonstrate that hierarchical suppression among many behaviors leads to the emergence of a sequence, providing new insights into the neural mechanisms that underlie motor behavior.
Summary
     Sgol2 is a protein that plays a crucial role in the coordination of essential cell cycle processes during meiosis I in oocytes. Meiosis I is a complex process that requires accurate chromosome segregation, which depends on the coordination of cohesion resolution, kinetochore-microtubule interactions, and the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC). Sgol2 has been found to protect cohesin, turn off the SAC, promote the congression and bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, facilitate the formation of kinetochore-microtubule attachments (K-fibers), and limit bivalent stretching.

     Sgol2's ability to protect cohesin depends on its interaction with protein phosphatase 2A (PP2A), and its ability to silence the SAC is mediated by direct binding to the MCC protein Mad2. In contrast, Sgol2's effect on bivalent stretching and K-fiber formation is independent of PP2A and is mediated by the recruitment of the kinesin MCAK and the inhibition of Aurora C kinase activity, respectively. Sgol2's multiple interactions link many of the processes essential for faithful chromosome segregation.

     Depletion of Sgol2 in oocytes has pleiotropic consequences, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in the dynamics of kinetochore-microtubule attachments. Sgol2 silences the SAC by binding to Mad2 using a similar motif common to Mad2's other two partners, Cdc20 and Mad1. Sgol2 deficiency also delays bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, causes increased bivalent stretching, and increases Aurora B/C kinase activity at kinetochores.

     The study demonstrates that a single protein, Sgol2, regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, the spindle assembly checkpoint, and Aurora B/C kinases. Different point mutations in Sgol2 alter the regulation of some but not other processes, indicating that the pleiotropy observed in Sgol2-deficient oocytes is not due to secondary or tertiary pathology. Sgol2's ability to protect centromeric cohesin depends on its interaction with PP2A, and its deficiency causes striking changes in chromosome and microtubule dynamics. Overall, Sgol2 plays a critical role in coordinating essential cell cycle processes during meiosis I in oocytes.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the persistence of experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity relies on the localization of RNA and protein synthesis. Local translation plays a crucial role in altering the structure and function of synapses during synapse formation and experience-dependent synaptic plasticity. A recent study investigated whether local stimulation triggers the directed trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses, in addition to promoting local translation.

     The study used imaging techniques to observe RNA localization and translation in cultured Aplysia sensory-motor neurons. The results revealed that RNAs were delivered throughout the arbor of the sensory neuron, but translation was enriched only at sites of synaptic contact and/or synaptic stimulation. This suggests that spatially restricting gene expression by regulating local translation, rather than directing the delivery of mRNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses, maximizes the readiness of the entire neuronal arbor to respond to local cues.

     The study also investigated the mechanisms that trigger local translation and found a role for calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC receptor signaling. Netrin-1 is a protein that plays a crucial role in axon guidance and synaptic plasticity. The study found that netrin-1/DCC signaling is involved in local translation at synapses, and that the release of netrin-1 from either the sensory neuron (SN) or the motor neuron (MN) during synapse formation and synaptic plasticity triggers translation at sites of synaptic but not non-synaptic contacts.

     The results of the study suggest that the spatial regulation of gene expression in Aplysia sensory-motor neurons is primarily mediated at the level of translation rather than at the level of RNA targeting from the nucleus. The study also found that mRNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors were delivered throughout the sensory neuron, but translation was significantly enriched at sites of synaptic contact and at sites of synaptic stimulation.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of local translation and RNA trafficking in neurons, and highlights the importance of netrin-1/DCC signaling in regulating gene expression at synapses. The findings have implications for our understanding of synaptic plasticity and the persistence of experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity.
Scientists have developed a new method to study how cells organize themselves into complex patterns and structures during development. This process, called morphogenesis, involves interactions between different cell types and is crucial for the formation of tissues and organs. However, current methods for studying morphogenesis lack control over the emergence of different cell types and the ability to selectively manipulate specific cell populations.

     Researchers used a technique called CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) to silence key proteins that regulate cell adhesion and cortical tension in human induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs). They created mixed populations of hiPSCs with and without the CRISPRi system and then induced mosaic knockdown (KD) of specific genes. This allowed them to study how the creation of physical asymmetries in an otherwise homogeneous population leads to multicellular organization and pattern formation.

     The study found that silencing the ROCK1 gene, which regulates cortical tension, or the CDH1 gene, which regulates cell-cell adhesion, resulted in differential patterning within hiPSC colonies. This patterning occurred through cellular self-organization, and the cells retained their pluripotent phenotype. The researchers also observed a transient wave of differential gene expression within the mixed populations that stabilized in coordination with the observed self-organization.

     The study demonstrates a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations and control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells. This approach facilitates the creation of robust models of morphogenesis, which is essential for understanding the molecular pathways that regulate symmetry-breaking during development. The findings have implications for the study of human developmental tissue patterning and formation, and may ultimately contribute to the development of new treatments for developmental disorders.

     The researchers employed an inducible CRISPRi system in hiPSCs to silence key proteins that regulate cell adhesion and cortical tension. They created mixed populations of hiPSCs with and without the CRISPRi system and then induced mosaic KD of specific genes. This allowed them to study how the creation of physical asymmetries in an otherwise homogeneous population leads to multicellular organization and pattern formation.

     The study shows that mosaic KD of ROCK1 or CDH1 results in a "bottom-up" cell-driven pattern formation of hiPSC colonies while preserving pluripotency. This approach enables genetic interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, which can facilitate a better understanding of the molecular pathways that regulate symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis. Ultimately, this study identifies a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations and control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells, and subsequently facilitates the creation of robust models of morphogenesis necessary for the mechanistic study of human developmental tissue patterning and formation.
The article proposes a normative theory, known as the resource-rational theory, to explain set size effects in human visual working memory (VWM). The theory posits that the brain minimizes a weighted sum of an error-based behavioral cost and a neural encoding cost to optimize task performance. A model constructed from this theory predicts set size effects and is supported by data from nine delayed-estimation experiments. The model accounts for the effects of both set size and probing probability on encoding precision, aligning with previous empirical findings. Notably, the theory suggests that the total amount of invested resource can vary non-monotonically with set size, which is a novel prediction that has not been directly tested before.

The theory may apply beyond VWM tasks, potentially explaining set size effects in attention tasks as well. It provides a normative formalism to test the speculation that the selectivity of attention arises from balancing performance against the costs associated with spiking. The model has relatively few parameters and accounts for a non-monotonic relationship between set size and the total amount of resource invested in item encoding. This prediction can be tested using neuroimaging experiments.

The theory also makes predictions about the effects of incentive manipulations on encoding precision, as these manipulations affect the expected behavioral cost function. The model's predictions are based on the idea that the brain optimizes task performance by minimizing a weighted sum of costs, rather than being constrained by fixed limits. This perspective raises the possibility that cognitive "limitations" arise from rational cost minimization rather than from constraints.

The resource-rational theory provides a new framework for understanding set size effects in VWM and attention tasks. It offers a principled explanation for the dependence of VWM precision on set size and provides a testable prediction about the non-monotonic relationship between set size and the total amount of resource invested in item encoding. The theory has implications for our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying VWM and attention, and its predictions can be tested using behavioral and neuroimaging experiments.
biomedical summary
     The Irish potato famine pathogen, Phytophthora infestans, secretes an effector protein called PexRD54 that interferes with the host plant's autophagy pathway. Autophagy is a cellular process that helps plants defend against infectious diseases by breaking down and recycling damaged or dysfunctional cellular components. PexRD54 binds to the autophagy protein ATG8CL, stimulating the formation of autophagosomes, which are membrane-bound structures that engulf and digest cellular material. This binding also depletes the autophagy cargo receptor Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes, which normally helps the plant defend against pathogens. By outcompeting Joka2, PexRD54 counteracts the plant's defense mechanisms and promotes disease susceptibility.

     The researchers found that PexRD54 has an ATG8 interacting motif, a specific sequence of amino acids that allows it to bind to ATG8CL with high affinity. This is different from another pathogen effector, RavZ, which inhibits autophagy. Instead, PexRD54 activates selective autophagy, which may help the pathogen eliminate defense-related compounds or reattribute cellular resources by promoting nutrient recycling.

     RXLR effectors, like PexRD54, are a family of modular proteins secreted by plant pathogens that alter various host processes. These effectors are useful tools for studying key pathways involved in pathogen invasion. In this study, the researchers show that PexRD54 binds to ATG8CL and stimulates autophagosome formation, and also depletes Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes to promote disease susceptibility.

     The findings suggest that, in addition to disrupting autophagy, pathogens can also activate it for their own benefit. The researchers propose that PexRD54 competes with the host cargo receptor Joka2 and depletes it out of ATG8CL autophagosomes to promote disease susceptibility. This study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms of defense-related autophagy and the selective nature of this process. It also highlights the complex interactions between plant pathogens and their hosts, and the ways in which pathogens can manipulate autophagy for their own advantage. Further research is needed to understand the spatio-temporal dynamics of autophagy and subcellular trafficking during host infection, and to determine whether other plant pathogens also target autophagy in similar ways.
Researchers have developed a new in vitro model to study how human cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) respond to changes in their environment, particularly in the context of cardiac injury. Cardiac fibroblasts play a crucial role in tissue adaptation and inflammation during pathological conditions such as ischemia. However, the mechanisms underlying their responses to environmental changes are not yet fully understood.

The study used a multi-stimulus microdevice to apply combined mechanical strain and controlled oxygen tension to human primary CFs. The researchers found that individual stimuli, such as mechanical strain or changes in oxygen tension, triggered different responses in CFs. For example, mechanical strain caused CFs to proliferate, while changes in oxygen tension led to an increase in collagen presence.

However, the combination of hypoxia (low oxygen levels) and simulated loss of contractility (2% strain) had a more significant impact on CFs. This combination triggered the release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases, which are associated with tissue remodeling and fibrosis.

The study's findings provide new insights into the complex interactions between environmental stimuli and CF responses. The researchers propose that their in vitro model can be used to better understand pathological mechanisms and develop reparative strategies for cardiac disease. The model recreates a pathological environment by combining oxygen changes and mechanical cues, allowing for a more comprehensive understanding of CF responses during cardiac injury.

The study builds on previous research that has investigated the effects of individual environmental stimuli on CFs, such as mechanical stress, oxygen deprivation, and biochemical stimulation with pro-fibrotic cytokines. However, this study is the first to examine the combined effects of mechanical strain and oxygen changes on human CFs.

The results of this study have implications for the development of in vitro models of cardiac disease and the understanding of pathological myocardial remodeling. The researchers suggest that their model can be used to tailor reparative strategies for cardiac fibrotic disease, which is a common complication of cardiac injury. Overall, the study provides new knowledge on the complex interactions between environmental stimuli and CF responses, and highlights the importance of considering the combined effects of multiple stimuli in the development of cardiac disease.
summary
     The DOMINO complex, a chromatin regulator in Drosophila, has been found to consist of two distinct multi-subunit complexes, DOM-A and DOM-B, which are specified by alternative isoforms of the DOMINO nucleosome remodeling ATPase. Both complexes are necessary for transcriptional regulation, but through different mechanisms. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2A.V, the fly ortholog of H2A.Z, genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. In contrast, the DOM-A complex functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4, similar to the yeast NuA4 complex. This work provides an example of how different evolutionary strategies can lead to similar functional separation, as seen in yeast and humans, where nucleosome remodeling and histone acetyltransferase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog specification.

     The DOM-A complex has a similar domain arrangement to the yeast NuA4 complex, suggesting that it may serve as the central subunit of a NuA4-type complex in Drosophila. However, the ATPase activity of DOM-A is dispensable for H4K12 acetylation by the DOM-A complex, a principle that may be conserved across metazoans. The DOM-B complex, on the other hand, incorporates H2A.V into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex.

     H2A.Z is primarily found at active promoters and enhancers, where it is thought to regulate transcription initiation and early elongation. In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, H2A.Z is introduced into chromatin by the SWR1 complex, a multi-subunit ATP-dependent chromatin remodeler with the INO80-type ATPase SWR1 at its core. In Drosophila, the DOM-B complex performs a similar function, incorporating H2A.V into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner.

     The functional differences between the two DOM isoforms were explored through RNAseq analysis, which showed that knock-down of either DOM-A, DOM-B, TIP60, or H2A.V affects transcription. The results suggest that the DOM-A and DOM-B complexes play distinct roles in transcriptional regulation, with DOM-A functioning as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex and DOM-B incorporating H2A.V into chromatin in an ATP-dependent manner. This work highlights the importance of alternative splicing in generating functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the formation of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) requires a precise interaction between motoneurons and muscle cells. A key protein, Î²-catenin (Ctnnb1), plays a crucial role in the differentiation of motoneurons in muscle cells. However, the mechanism by which muscle cells communicate with motoneurons to facilitate NMJ formation is not fully understood. This study aimed to elucidate the function of Ctnnb1 in muscle cells and its role in presynaptic differentiation.

     The researchers used an in vivo transgenic approach to investigate the functions of Ctnnb1 in muscle cells. They found that a mutant form of Ctnnb1 lacking the transactivation domain (TAD) was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation. This suggests that the transcriptional regulation function of Ctnnb1 is essential for presynaptic differentiation. In contrast, the cell-adhesion function of Ctnnb1 was found to be dispensable.

     To identify a potential retrograde signal from muscle cells to motoneurons, the researchers screened for proteins that interact with Ctnnb1. They identified Slit2, a protein known to repel or collapse neuronal axons, as a potential candidate. Overexpression of Slit2 in muscle cells was found to diminish presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation in mice. Furthermore, Slit2 immobilized on beads was able to induce synaptophysin puncta in axons of spinal cord explants.

     These findings suggest that Slit2 serves as a factor utilized by muscle Ctnnb1 to direct presynaptic differentiation. The researchers also showed that Ctnnb1 associates with Tcf/Lef cis-elements in the promoter region of the Slit2 gene, indicating that Ctnnb1 regulates Slit2 expression. Slit2 was found to be concentrated at the NMJ, and transgenic mice expressing Slit2 specifically in muscle cells were able to rescue presynaptic deficits caused by a Ctnnb1 mutation.

     Overall, this study provides new insights into the mechanism of NMJ formation and the role of Ctnnb1 in presynaptic differentiation. The identification of Slit2 as a retrograde signal from muscle cells to motoneurons highlights the importance of cell-cell communication in the development of NMJs. Further research is needed to fully understand the complex interactions between muscle cells and motoneurons during NMJ formation.
Scientists have developed a new method to study how forces are transmitted between cells in epithelial tissues, which are groups of cells that form the lining of organs and glands. Epithelial tissues play a crucial role in various biological processes, including development, maintenance, and adaptation. The researchers used a combination of computational and experimental approaches to quantify the dynamics of force transmission in cell clusters with both sub-cellular and multi-cellular resolution.

The study found that basal force fluctuations in cell clusters are coupled to the localization of E-cadherin, a protein that forms the backbone of adherens junctions in epithelial cells. At the level of individual cell-cell junctions, the researchers observed that local mechanical stresses correlated with the localization of E-cadherin molecules. Additionally, they found that force propagation through cells can be intercepted by cell anchorage to the extracellular matrix and by actomyosin contraction.

The study also revealed that force transmission across a cell cluster is short-ranged, typically on the length scale of one cell diameter, due to the anchoring and scrambling of forces by cell-matrix adhesions and basal actomyosin contractility. This is in contrast to previous observations of longer-range force transmission required for tissue morphogenesis processes, such as collective cell migration.

The researchers used a novel method to quantify force exchange profiles along cell-cell interfaces in real-time, allowing them to study the dynamics of force transmission in cell clusters. They applied this method to spontaneously-forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and found that cell-cell force exchange depended on the cell position within a cluster and was adaptive to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements.

The study provides insights into the mechanisms that control mechanical stress homeostasis in dynamic epithelial tissues and highlights the importance of coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility in force transmission through cells. The developed method can be used as a resource for the study of mechanotransduction in cell-cell adhesions, which is essential for understanding various biological processes, including tissue development, maintenance, and adaptation.
Biomedical researchers have made significant progress in understanding the structure and function of the huntingtin protein, which is associated with Huntington's disease. A polyglutamine expansion in the huntingtin protein is the primary cause of this neurodegenerative disorder. To investigate the structural and biochemical properties of huntingtin and the effect of the polyglutamine expansion, researchers employed various biophysical experiments, including circular dichroism, single-particle electron microscopy, and cross-linking mass spectrometry.

     The results indicate that huntingtin is composed of five distinct domains and adopts a spherical Î±-helical solenoid structure. The amino-terminal and carboxyl-terminal regions of the protein fold to create a central cavity. Notably, the polyglutamine expansion increases the Î±-helical properties of huntingtin and affects the intramolecular interactions among the domains. This suggests that the polyglutamine expansion may modulate the structure and function of huntingtin.

     To further explore this idea, researchers conducted systematic biophysical and biochemical analyses of highly purified human recombinant huntingtins with varied lengths of polyglutamine tracts. The results show that the impact of the polyglutamine tract on huntingtin's basic structural features suggests that the protein may undergo dramatic conformational changes upon interaction with its binding partners.

     Single-particle electron microscopy of recombinant human huntingtins with polyglutamine tract lengths of 23- and 78-residues revealed that the protein's shape may enable a structural impact of the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract. The biophysical analyses of an allelic series of native recombinant human huntingtins provide a satisfying solution to the mystery of how the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract may modulate huntingtin structure and function.

     The researchers found that the Î±-helical properties of huntingtin increase with the length of the polyglutamine tract. This suggests that the polyglutamine expansion may confer a novel property on huntingtin, contributing to the development of Huntington's disease. The study's findings provide new insights into the structural characteristics of full-length huntingtin and the effects of the polyglutamine expansion, shedding light on the molecular mechanisms underlying this devastating neurodegenerative disorder.
summary
     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of ferritinophagy, the autophagic turnover of ferritin, in regulating intracellular iron bioavailability. However, the mechanisms controlling ferritinophagy flux and the role of NCOA4, a selective cargo receptor for ferritin, in iron-dependent processes have remained poorly understood. Recent studies have shed light on the molecular nature of the NCOA4-ferritin complex and the regulation of NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy by intracellular iron levels.

     NCOA4 interacts directly with ferritin heavy chain 1 (FTH1) via a conserved NCOA4 C-terminal domain and a key conserved residue on FTH1. This interaction is essential for the delivery of ferritin to lysosomes via autophagosomes. Furthermore, NCOA4 abundance is under dual control via autophagy and the ubiquitin-proteasome system. Under high iron conditions, the HERC2 ubiquitin ligase recognizes NCOA4 and mediates its turnover via the ubiquitin-proteasome system, reducing NCOA4 levels and promoting ferritin accumulation for iron capture.

     In contrast, under low iron conditions, NCOA4 levels increase to promote ferritinophagy. The HERC2 ligase plays a crucial role in this process, using its CUL7-homology domain to recognize NCOA4 under high iron conditions and mediate its turnover. This iron-dependent regulation of NCOA4 is essential for maintaining cellular iron homeostasis.

     NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy is also critical for erythropoiesis, the process of red blood cell production. In zebrafish and cultured cells, NCOA4 plays an essential role in erythroid differentiation. The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying ferritinophagy and the regulation of NCOA4 by intracellular iron levels, highlighting the importance of this process in maintaining cellular iron homeostasis and erythropoiesis.
The lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) plays a crucial role in higher-level cognition, but its functional organization has remained poorly understood. A long-standing proposal suggests that the LPFC is organized hierarchically, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information to facilitate efficient and flexible cognition. However, previous research has provided limited support for this theory.

     Recent human functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data revealed a rostral-caudal gradient of abstraction in the LPFC, indicating that progressively rostral areas are involved in processing more abstract information. Dynamic causal modeling further revealed asymmetrical interactions between LPFC sub-regions, suggesting hierarchical processing. Contrary to prevailing assumptions, the relative strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned the mid-LPFC as the apex of the hierarchy.

     The study found that cognitive demands induced connectivity modulations towards the mid-LPFC, consistent with a role in integrating information for control operations. Moreover, the strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability. These results collectively suggest that the LPFC is hierarchically organized, with the mid-LPFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control behavior.

     The researchers confirmed that the rostral-caudal axis of the LPFC operates according to abstraction principles by examining gradients of stimulus domain-generality/specificity and temporal control over action. They also modeled interactions among LPFC sub-regions to examine whether and how cognitive control demands elicit asymmetrical influences indicative of hierarchical processing.

     The study found that both the hierarchical orientation of the mid-LPFC in fixed connectivity and the top-down influences from rostral LPFC to mid-LPFC during the engagement of cognitive control were positively related to higher-level cognitive ability. This suggests that the functional organization of the LPFC is intimately tied to cognitive capacity. Furthermore, more strongly asymmetrical hierarchical projections from mid-LPFC to other LPFC regions were related to better higher-level cognitive ability, indicating a beneficial cognitive effect of a hierarchical organization with mid-LPFC at the apex.

     The researchers examined interactions within the LPFC by varying demands on stimulus domain and cognitive control, which engaged most of the LPFC and provided a comprehensive account of its function. They found that progressively rostral areas processed progressively abstract levels of cognitive control, with demands on feature control, contextual control, and temporal control eliciting increasingly rostral activations. Dynamic causal modeling revealed that hierarchical asymmetries in connectivity were strongest in mid-LPFC, and cognitive demands elicited both top-down influences from rostral to mid-LPFC and bottom-up influences from caudal to mid-LPFC. Thus, analyses of effective connectivity and its modulations suggest that mid-LPFC forms a nexus where information converges to influence action.
Summary
     The study investigated the role of the hominoid-specific gene TBC1D3 in brain development and cortical folding. TBC1D3 is a gene that underwent segmental duplications during the evolution of hominoids, but its function in brain development was unknown. The researchers found that expressing TBC1D3 in the ventricular cortical progenitors of mice via in utero electroporation caused the delamination of ventricular radial glia cells and promoted the generation of self-renewing basal progenitors with the morphology of outer radial glia. Outer radial glia are a type of neural progenitor cell that is abundant in primates and is thought to contribute to cortical expansion and folding.

     The study also found that down-regulating TBC1D3 in cultured human brain slices decreased the generation of outer radial glia. Furthermore, localized proliferation of outer radial glia resulting from either in utero electroporation or transgenic expression of TBC1D3 was often found to underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding. This suggests that TBC1D3 plays a role in regulating cortical expansion and folding.

     The researchers propose that TBC1D3 acts as a candidate gene that controls many other genes involved in cortex expansion and folding. They also suggest that TBC1D3 may have appeared earlier than another human-specific gene, ARHGAP11B, which has been shown to promote production of basal intermediate progenitors and cause cortical folding in the electroporated mouse brain.

     The study provides evidence that the brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during hominoid evolution. The researchers found that TBC1D3 expression in neuroprogenitors by in utero electroporation at E13.5 caused a reduced expression of Trnp1, a gene involved in stemness signaling pathways, in flow cytometry-sorted cells at E15. This suggests that TBC1D3 may influence the intrinsic stemness signaling pathways in basal progenitors.

     Overall, the study identifies TBC1D3 as a hominoid gene that promotes the generation of outer radial glia and induces cortical folding in mice. The findings provide new insights into the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying cortical expansion and folding, and suggest that TBC1D3 may play a key role in the evolution of higher intelligence.
summary
     The article investigates the role of ubiquitination in the transcriptional activity of the plant immune coactivator NPR1. NPR1 is a key regulator of systemic acquired resistance in plants, and its activation is associated with a reprogrammed transcriptome. Previous studies suggested that immune-induced ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation of NPR1 facilitate the continuous delivery of active NPR1 to target promoters, maximizing gene expression. However, this process is thought to be costly due to the sacrificial turnover of NPR1.

     The study reveals that ubiquitination of NPR1 is a progressive event, where initial modification by a Cullin-RING E3 ligase (CRL3) promotes its chromatin association and expression of target genes. This is followed by the extension of ubiquitin chains by the E4 ligase UBE4, which targets NPR1 for proteasomal degradation. Conversely, the activities of UBE4 are opposed by UBP6/7, two proteasome-associated deubiquitinases that enhance NPR1 longevity.

     The study demonstrates that initial CRL3-mediated ubiquitination is required for NPR1 to attain its full transcriptional activity, while the stepwise formation of long ubiquitin chains mediated by UBE4 inactivates NPR1 and promotes its degradation by the proteasome. The findings indicate that initial ubiquitination boosts NPR1 transcriptional coactivator activity, enhancing target promoter occupancy in the short term and potentially promoting genomic mobility of NPR1 in the longer term.

     The study also explores the role of E4 ligases in the ubiquitination of NPR1. Unlike E3 ligases, E4 ligases do not contribute to the initial ubiquitination of substrates but rather extend pre-existing ubiquitin chains. The E4 ligase UBE4/MUSE3 has been implicated in the degradation of NLR immune receptors in Arabidopsis. The study suggests that prior to degradation, ubiquitination itself might act as a transcriptional signal, providing a window of opportunity for NPR1 to activate its target genes.

     The study concludes that ubiquitin chain extension and trimming activities can fine-tune transcriptional outputs of unstable eukaryotic activators without strict requirement for sacrificial turnover. The findings provide new insights into the complex regulation of NPR1 and its role in plant immune responses.
Scientists have identified the precursors of 24 nucleotide small interfering RNAs (24 nt siRNAs) that guide DNA methylation and gene silencing in Arabidopsis thaliana. These 24 nt siRNAs are crucial for silencing transposons and a subset of genes. The biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs requires nuclear RNA polymerase IV (Pol IV), RNA-dependent RNA polymerase 2 (RDR2), and DICER-like 3 (DCL3). However, the precursors of these siRNAs were previously undefined.

     Researchers found that Pol IV and RDR2-dependent RNAs (P4R2 RNAs) accumulate in dcl3 mutants and are diced into 24 nt RNAs by DCL3 in vitro. P4R2 RNAs are mostly 26-45 nucleotides long and initiate with a purine adjacent to a pyrimidine, characteristics shared by Pol IV transcripts generated in vitro. RDR2's terminal transferase activity may account for occasional non-templated nucleotides at P4R2 RNA 3' termini.

     The 24 nt siRNAs primarily correspond to the 5' or 3' ends of P4R2 RNAs, suggesting a model where siRNAs are generated from either end of P4R2 duplexes by single dicing events. This model is supported by the observation that dozens of P4R2 RNAs can overlap at a given locus, but individual P4R2 RNAs represent individual Pol IV-RDR2 transcription units.

     The study also found that P4R2 RNAs are dependent on both Pol IV and RDR2, as they accumulate in dcl2/3/4 triple mutants but not in nrpd1 (Pol IV) or rdr2 mutants. Furthermore, P4R2 RNAs that accumulate in dcl2/3/4 triple mutants are substrates for DCL3, as they are diced into 24 nt RNAs in vitro. These findings provide new insights into the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs and their role in guiding DNA methylation and gene silencing in Arabidopsis.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand how immature neural networks develop into their mature form, enabling complex computations underlying cognition. A recent study investigated the progression of large-scale synaptic and cellular activity patterns across development in immature mice using high spatiotemporal resolution in vivo electrophysiology. The findings reveal that mature cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile transition period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by relative neural quiescence, after which spatially distributed, temporally precise, and internally organized activity occurs.

The study demonstrates that the transition between immature and mature network dynamics is marked by unexpectedly decreased coordinated cellular and synaptic activity at the beginning of the second postnatal week. Following this timepoint, precise neuronal synchronization and oscillatory coupling in space and time robustly emerge. This developmental trajectory is also observed in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that could facilitate a transition in network operation.

The researchers hypothesize that this transient quiescent period is a requisite for the subsequent emergence of coordinated cortical networks. During this period, oscillations and neural spiking display a nadir, signaling the shift from local, loosely correlated, putatively sensory-driven patterns to internally organized, spatially distributed, and temporally precise activity. Cortical microcircuits are still undergoing dramatic changes in anatomical connectivity and functional connectivity between and within cortical layers during this developmental epoch.

The study shows that neural spiking becomes more efficiently recruited to oscillatory activity with consistent, strong phase-locking, and higher temporal precision. These features are characteristic of adult neural networks engaged in information processing. The findings suggest that a transient period of developmental perturbation may be an evolutionarily conserved indicator of a substantial shift in cortical network operations. Overall, the study provides new insights into the developmental trajectory of neural networks and highlights the importance of a transient quiescent period in the emergence of mature cortical dynamics.
Summary
     The coexistence of multiple strains in pathogen populations that undergo sexual recombination is a central question in epidemiology, with significant implications for disease control. A new agent-based model has been developed to address this issue, focusing on the var gene family of Plasmodium falciparum, the parasite responsible for malaria. The model considers the extensive diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression.

     The model tracks the dynamics of all unique var repertoires in a population of hosts and shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain competition mediated through cross-immunity structures the parasite population into a subset of coexisting dominant repertoires of var genes. The degree of antigenic overlap between these repertoires depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic variation at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity.

     The var gene family encodes P. falciparum erythrocyte membrane protein 1 (PfEMP1), the major surface antigen of the parasite's blood stages that binds to host endothelial cell receptors to allow the parasite to sequester within various host tissues. The model suggests that immune selection acting on multi-copy antigen genes can lead to the maintenance of discrete, non-overlapping antigenic repertoires, even when antigenic variability is driven by several unlinked genes that undergo recombination.

     The model extends earlier strain theory by considering the effects of immune selection in populations of pathogens whose antigen-encoding loci are multi-copy and recombining. It explores whether structured parasite populations resulting from the selection pressures exerted by competition among distinct var repertoires for hosts, mediated by cross-immunity, can emerge. The results have implications for malaria research and control, as they suggest that vector control strategies that reduce transmission may not always drastically reduce var gene diversity, as a reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse, highly non-overlapping var repertoires will remain.
summary
     The article describes how the deamidase OspI from the enteric bacteria Shigella flexneri disrupts the interaction between the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC13 and the RING-finger ubiquitin ligase TRAF6. OspI specifically targets a glutamine residue in UBC13, converting it to glutamate (Q100E), which silences the polyubiquitination activity of the UBC13/TRAF6 complex and inhibits the downstream NF-ÎºB inflammatory response. The precise role of deamidation in silencing the UBC13/TRAF6 complex was unknown, but the study reveals that deamidation inhibits the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6 RING-domain (TRAF6RING) by perturbing both native and transient interactions.

     Deamidation creates a new intramolecular salt-bridge in UBC13 that competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge at the native UBC13/TRAF6RING interface. This competition prevents transient interactions necessary to form a typical UBC13/RING complex. Additionally, repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged surface of RING prevents transient interactions in the UBC13/RING complex. The study highlights a mechanism wherein a post-translational modification, deamidation, perturbs the conformation and stability of transient complexes to inhibit protein-protein association.

     Further studies showed that deamidation disrupts the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6RING by three mechanisms: i) a new intramolecular R14/E100 salt-bridge appears in dUBC13, which competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge in the native complex, ii) the salt-bridge competition also perturbs the UBC13/TRAF6RING transient complexes to inhibit association, and iii) transient repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged interface of TRAF6RING reduces protein-protein association.

     The Shigella flexneri effector OspI suppresses the inflammatory response (NF-ÎºB pathway) via deamidation of UBC13 at Q100. By an unknown mechanism, deamidation of Q100 to E100 inhibits the synthesis of K63-linked polyubiquitin chains by the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, preventing the downstream activation of NF-ÎºB pathway. These chains serve as a scaffold to bring together TAK1/2 and IKK kinases, eventually leading to phosphorylation and activation of the IKK kinases, IÎºB degradation, and nuclear translocation of transcription factor Nf-ÎºB.
