     biomedical summary
Researchers investigated the role of large-scale brain synchronization in working memory (WM) performance using transcranial alternating current stimulation (tACS) to modulate oscillatory activity in a right frontoparietal network. The study aimed to provide causal evidence for the contribution of oscillatory synchronization to WM and to examine the underlying neural mechanisms by which frontoparietal phase synchronization influences verbal WM.

The study consisted of two experiments using tACS to selectively entrain endogenous brain rhythms. The researchers tested four hypotheses: (1) behavioral WM performance is enhanced by entraining synchronous activity within a right frontoparietal network activated by WM demands; (2) regional brain activity in this frontoparietal network is modulated in a phase-dependent manner; (3) changes in blood oxygenation level-dependent (BOLD) activity induced by phase-dependent network stimulation correlate with behavioral performance; and (4) functional connectivity across distant brain regions is modulated in a phase-dependent manner.

The results showed that externally induced synchronization improved performance when cognitive demands were high. Simultaneously collected functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data revealed tACS effects dependent on the relative phase of the stimulation and the internal cognitive processing state. Specifically, synchronous tACS during the verbal WM task increased parietal activity, which correlated with behavioral performance. Furthermore, functional connectivity results indicated that the relative phase of frontoparietal stimulation influences information flow within the WM network.

The study found that synchronous (in-phase) stimulation caused an improvement in performance and led to increased frontoparietal activity when cognitive demands were high. These results demonstrate a link between behavioral performance in a demanding WM task and large-scale brain synchronization. The study also showed that external manipulations of large-scale brain synchronization can be used to shape activity and connectivity within brain networks, and those modulations are detectable using fMRI.

Overall, the findings provide direct evidence for the role of phase synchronization in cognitive demanding WM processes and reveal the neural correlates associated with this process. The results suggest that large-scale brain synchronization plays a critical role in WM performance and that external manipulations of this synchronization can be used to enhance WM performance. The study's findings have implications for the development of novel treatments for WM-related cognitive impairments, such as those seen in attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) and Alzheimer's disease.
summary
     Biomedical research has long understood that embryonic polarity in invertebrates, amphibians, and fish is largely specified by maternal determinants early in development. In contrast, amniote embryos, which include mammals and birds, remain plastic and can form multiple individuals until gastrulation. The study aimed to investigate how polarity is determined in amniote embryos.

     A molecular screen was conducted to identify upstream regulators of cVg1, a transforming growth factor beta (TGFÎ²) signal expressed posteriorly before gastrulation in chick embryos. The screen uncovered the transcription factor Pitx2 as a candidate regulator of cVg1. The study found that Pitx2 is essential for axis formation and directly regulates cVg1 expression by binding to enhancers within neighboring genes.

     Pitx2, Vg1/GDF1, and Nodal are also key actors in left-right asymmetry, suggesting that the same ancient polarity determination mechanism has been co-opted to different functions during evolution. In most invertebrates and anamniote vertebrates, embryonic polarity is first established by the localization of maternal determinants in the cytoplasm and/or cortex of the fertilized egg.

     However, the ability of higher vertebrate embryos to retain a regulative model of development until a late stage suggests that localisation of maternally inherited determinants is not an essential component of the mechanisms specifying embryo polarity. The study implicates Pitx2 as the earliest gene described to date that regulates the position of the embryonic axis as well as embryonic regulation and twinning.

     Pitx2 is a transcription factor that is expressed in the same domain as cVg1 in whole embryos and in bisected embryo marginal zone, and it is expressed before cVg1. The study provides evidence that Pitx2 is a direct and essential regulator of cVg1 expression both during normal development and in embryonic regulation (induced twinning). The findings suggest that Pitx2 plays a crucial role in determining embryonic polarity in amniote embryos.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that fruit flies (Drosophila melanogaster) exhibit robust odor-guided behaviors in response to odor gradients. To investigate how early olfactory circuits process temporal variations of olfactory stimuli, researchers subjected flies to precisely defined odor concentration waveforms and examined the spike patterns of olfactory sensory neurons (OSNs) and projection neurons (PNs). The study found a significant temporal transformation between OSN and PN spike patterns, with the PN output strongly signaling the OSN spike rate and its rate of change. A simple two-dimensional model admitting the OSN spike rate and its rate of change as inputs closely predicted the PN output. This suggests that PNs primarily signal the acceleration and the rate of change of dynamic odor stimuli to higher brain centers, enabling animals to reliably respond to the onsets of odor concentrations.

The researchers tested first- and second-order neurons in the Drosophila olfactory system with a wide range of dynamically varying odor concentrations and constructed a simple 2D LN model for the temporal processing of olfactory information. The shape of the 2D nonlinearity and the relatively low prediction error of the model support the hypothesis that the PN spike rate is most strongly dependent on the rate of change of its feedforward OSN input. This encoding mechanism is crucial for flying insects to detect and respond to an encountered odor plume before the fly's position drifts relative to adjacent plumes.

The study demonstrated that Drosophila's early olfactory system signals the acceleration of odor concentrations to higher brain centers. This encoding mechanism allows an animal to detect the odor onset at a very early phase. Recent studies have investigated how dynamic olfactory stimuli are processed in insect early olfactory systems and observed significant temporal processing of odor signals. Together, the researchers hypothesize that acceleration encoding in the Drosophila antennal lobe allows higher olfactory centers to rapidly respond to onsets of slowly rising odor stimuli. The findings of this study provide new insights into the neural mechanisms underlying the processing of dynamic olfactory information in insects.
summary
     In flowering plants, seed development is initiated by the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with two paternal sperm cells, resulting in the formation of an embryo and endosperm, respectively. The fertilization products are surrounded by the maternally derived seed coat, whose development is blocked by epigenetic regulators belonging to the Polycomb Group (PcG) protein family prior to fertilization. Research has shown that fertilization of the central cell leads to the production of auxin, a plant hormone, which is likely exported to the maternal tissues, driving seed coat development by removing the PcG block. This process is essential for seed coat development, as mutants for the MADS-box transcription factor AGL62 have impaired transport of auxin from the endosperm to the integuments, resulting in seed abortion.

     The production of auxin in the developing endosperm is thought to be the trigger for seed coat development. To investigate this hypothesis, researchers examined plants mutant for the MADS-box transcription factor AGL62. The agl62 mutant seeds abort early after fertilization, correlating with early endosperm cellularization and failure to develop a seed coat. This suggests that AGL62 regulates auxin transport from the endosperm to the integuments, leading to the removal of the PcG block on seed coat development.

     Further evidence supporting this hypothesis includes the rapid accumulation of auxin in the integuments after fertilization, impaired auxin biosynthesis but not auxin signaling in the endosperm causing defects in seed coat development, and ovules of transgenic lines producing auxin ectopically in the central cell initiating seed coat development without fertilization. Additionally, the failure of seed coat development in the agl62 mutant correlates with failure to export auxin from the endosperm, indicating that AGL62 is involved in mediating both endosperm and seed coat developmental pathways.

     The researchers propose a dual role for post-fertilization auxin production, driving both endosperm and seed coat development. The fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin, which is exported to the integuments, lifting the PRC2 block on seed coat development and allowing for cell elongation and differentiation. However, this process is highly dependent on the activity of the transcription factor AGL62, as agl62 seeds fail to export auxin from the endosperm, leading to auxin accumulation and failure to develop a seed coat.
Summary
     The interaction between a bacteriophage and its host is mediated by the phage's receptor binding protein (RBP), which plays a crucial role in governing phage activity and host range. Despite its importance, the molecular rules of RBP function remain poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers developed a high-throughput method called ORACLE (Optimized Recombination, Accumulation, and Library Expression) to systematically dissect the functional role of every residue in the tip domain of T7 phage RBP.

     Using ORACLE, the researchers created a library of 1660 T7 phage variants by making all single amino acid substitutions at every site in the tip domain. They then quantified the functional role of each variant on multiple bacterial hosts, generating high-resolution functional maps that revealed regions concentrated with function-enhancing substitutions and host-specific substitutional patterns. The results showed that single amino acid substitutions alone can generate broad functional diversity, highlighting the evolutionary adaptability of the RBP.

     The study identified key functional regions determining activity and host range, many of which were previously unknown. The researchers also discovered gain-of-function variants against resistant hosts and host-constricting variants that eliminated certain hosts. Furthermore, they engineered highly active T7 variants against a urinary tract pathogen, demonstrating the therapeutic utility of their approach.

     The ORACLE method presents a generalized framework for characterizing sequence-function relationships in many phage-bacterial systems. The results of this study provide valuable insights into the molecular drivers of adaptability of the tip domain and highlight the potential of phage engineering for therapeutic applications. Further experiments will be required to assess the in vivo efficacy of the T7 variants, but the findings of this study demonstrate the potential of ORACLE to generate phage variants superior to wildtype phage that could serve as starting points for further engineering.
Individuals with congenital amusia, a lifelong condition characterized by unreliable pitch processing, have been found to rely less on pitch cues during speech perception and instead rely on other dimensions such as duration. To investigate the neural basis for this strategy, researchers used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to scan 15 individuals with amusia and 15 controls. Participants were asked to read sentences where a comma indicated a grammatical phrase boundary and then heard two sentences spoken that differed only in pitch and/or duration cues, selecting the best match for the written sentence.

The results showed that individuals with amusia exhibited reduced functional connectivity between left prefrontal language-related regions and right hemisphere pitch-related regions, which reflected the between-group differences in cue weights in the same groups of listeners. This reduction in connectivity was not present during a control task. The study suggests that the reliability of perceptual dimensions is linked with functional connectivity between frontal and perceptual regions, and that individuals with amusia may have developed a compensatory mechanism to down-weight pitch due to its unreliability.

The findings are consistent with previous research showing that individuals with amusia rely less on pitch than controls to process spoken language (Jasmin et al., 2020a). The study also found that the extent of pitch-responsive regions within auditory cortex is similar in participants with amusia and controls (Norman-Haignere et al., 2016), and that brainstem encoding of pitch in speech and musical stimuli is unimpaired in individuals with amusia (Liu et al., 2015b). The results suggest that amusics neglect pitch because they have implicitly learned that their memory for pitch is unreliable, and that this down-weighting of pitch is reflected in decreased functional connectivity between right auditory areas and downstream task-relevant areas that integrate information from perceptual regions. Overall, the study provides new insights into the neural basis of congenital amusia and highlights the importance of functional connectivity in speech perception.
summary
     Scientists have discovered a new contagious cancer in the Tasmanian devil, known as Devil Facial Tumour 2 (DFT2). This cancer is distinct from an existing contagious cancer, Devil Facial Tumour 1 (DFT1), which affects the same species. Research has shown that DFT2 cells express major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class I molecules, unlike DFT1 cells, which do not express these molecules. MHC class I molecules play a crucial role in the immune system, helping to distinguish between self and non-self cells.

     The MHC class I molecules expressed by DFT2 cells are either non-classical or shared with the host devils, making them less recognizable to the immune system. This reduced immunogenicity may contribute to the contagious nature of DFT2. In contrast, the existing contagious cancer, DFT1, has lost expression of MHC class I molecules, which may have facilitated its widespread transmission.

     The study found that DFT2 cells express both classical and non-classical MHC class I molecules in vitro and in vivo. However, the expression of these molecules varies among DFT2 tumours, suggesting that MHC class I-negative subclones may emerge as the cancer transmits more widely. The researchers propose that contagious cancers may exploit partial histocompatibility between the tumour and host, but the loss of allogeneic antigens could facilitate the widespread transmission of DFT2.

     Further analysis is needed to understand the host immune response to DFT2 and its role in shaping tumour evolution. The similarity of MHC class I alleles between DFT2 and host devils may reflect a shared geographical range and genotype pool, which could impact the ability of DFT2 to spread further. The heterogenic expression of MHC class I molecules in DFT2 biopsies suggests that MHC class I expression is not fixed and may be gradually lost in DFT2.
Summary
     The study investigated the neural circuits in the granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG), a region critical for both spatial and non-spatial behaviors. Using optogenetic circuit mapping in mice, researchers found a double dissociation between two types of principal pyramidal neurons in layer 1 of the RSG: low-rheobase (LR) and regular-spiking (RS) cells. The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, sources of spatial information, selectively recruit LR cells, while claustral and anterior cingulate inputs, sources of mostly non-spatial information, preferentially control RS cells. This parallel processing is enabled by the precise sublaminar organization of distinct principal neuronal subtypes and axonal afferents in layer 1 of the RSG.

     The study found that LR neurons receive directional and spatial inputs from the anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, and the synaptic dynamics of thalamic inputs allow for the robust encoding of angular speed by LR cell models, even though these inputs only explicitly contain information about head direction, not speed. In contrast, RS cells are controlled by claustral and anterior cingulate inputs, which provide mostly non-spatial information. The distinct morphology and intrinsic properties of LR neurons make them ideally suited to integrate head direction input from the thalamus and spatial inputs from the hippocampal formation via the dorsal subiculum.

     The study highlights the importance of precise sublaminar organization in layer 1 of the RSG for parallel processing of spatial and non-spatial information. The integration of these parallel streams of information in downstream neurons within both the ipsilateral and contralateral granular and dysgranular retrosplenial cortices is the next critical step toward a mechanistic understanding of retrosplenial computations. The findings have implications for understanding the neural basis of spatial orientation and navigation behaviors.
Summary
     Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a significant risk factor for the development of dementias, including chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE), a type of tauopathy. However, the mechanisms underlying the spread of tau aggregates after TBI are not fully understood, partly due to the lack of tractable animal models. To investigate the role of seizures in promoting the spread of tauopathy, researchers used a novel zebrafish model expressing a genetically encoded fluorescent Tau biosensor that reports the accumulation of human Tau species when seeded via intraventricular brain injections.

     The zebrafish larvae were subjected to a TBI paradigm that produced various TBI features, including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. The researchers found that bath application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued TBI-induced tauopathy and cell death. These findings suggest that seizure activity plays a role in the prion-like seeding and spreading of tauopathy following TBI.

     Zebrafish larvae have emerged as a powerful complement to rodent models of TBI and cellular models of tauopathy. The researchers engineered a novel in vivo Tau biosensor model in zebrafish that can visualize pathological Tau spreading and accumulation within the intact and vibrant central nervous system (CNS). The data revealed a substantial burst of neural activity during TBI, and zebrafish larvae exposed to TBI exhibited a significantly higher propensity for spontaneous seizures.

     The researchers found that post-traumatic seizures correlate strongly with spreading tau pathology following TBI. Manipulating this seizure activity mitigated Tau aggregation and revealed a critical role for endocytosis in the prion-like spread of Tau seeds in vivo following TBI. The results from the novel in vivo TBI model implicate seizures and dynamin-dependent endocytosis in the spread of Tau seeds, offering potential therapeutic targets.

     The study highlights the importance of post-traumatic seizures as a druggable mechanistic link between TBI and the prion-like progression of tauopathy. The findings suggest that anti-convulsants that dampen post-traumatic seizures may be a route to moderating subsequent tauopathy. Further work is warranted to explore this potential therapeutic approach.
Summary
     The relationship between the spatial configuration and functional connectivity of brain regions has been investigated in a recent study using resting state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI) data. Brain connectivity is often considered in terms of the communication between functionally distinct brain regions, with many studies examining the extent to which patterns of coupling strength between multiple neural populations relate to behavior. However, the exact spatial arrangement of cortical regions has been found to interact strongly with measures of brain connectivity, suggesting that cross-subject variations in the spatial configuration of functional brain regions may be misinterpreted as changes in functional connectivity.

     The study aimed to identify how the spatial configuration and organisation of functional regions influence derived measures of functional connectivity and how they relate to trait variability in behavior and lifestyle across individuals. The researchers found that the shape and exact location of brain regions interact with the modeling of brain connectivity, and that the spatial arrangement of functional regions is strongly predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle.

     The study highlights the importance of disambiguating the influence of spatial topography to enable the estimation of fMRI measures that uniquely reflect coupling strength between neural populations. The researchers suggest that the cross-subject information represented in commonly adopted "connectivity fingerprints" largely reflects spatial variability in the location of functional regions across individuals, rather than variability in coupling strength. This finding has significant implications for the interpretation of functional imaging data and cognitive traits.

     The study builds on previous research that has investigated the organisation of the human brain into large-scale functional networks using rfMRI. Spontaneous fluctuations in distinct brain regions show temporal correlations with each other, revealing complex patterns of functional connectivity. The researchers used partial canonical correlation analysis (CCA) to examine the relationship between network matrices and spatial topographical organisation of functional regions, and found that network matrices contain little unique trait-level cross-subject information that is not also reflected in the spatial topographical organisation of functional regions.

     The study's findings suggest that the functional organisation of the brain is conceptualised and operationally defined in a way that is directly relevant to the interpretation of these findings. The researchers conclude that a better understanding of these effects is important when interpreting the relationship between functional imaging data and cognitive traits, and that future studies should aim to disambiguate the influence of spatial topography to enable the estimation of fMRI measures that uniquely reflect coupling strength between neural populations.
summary
     Biomedical research has proposed that changes in the components of the transcription pre-initiation complex (PIC) control cell type-specific gene expression. Specifically, it was suggested that the replacement of the canonical TFIID-TBP complex with TRF3/TBP2 is required for the activation of muscle-gene expression in skeletal muscle cells. However, the role of TBP2 in adult myogenesis was unclear due to the lack of a developmental phenotype in TBP2 null mice.

     To investigate this further, researchers analyzed TBP2 null mice and found that they have an intact regeneration potential upon injury. Additionally, TBP2 was not expressed in established C2C12 muscle cells or primary mouse muscle stem cells (MuSCs). In contrast, TFIID subunits and TBP were downregulated during myoblast differentiation, but reduced amounts of these proteins formed a complex that was detectable on the promoters of muscle genes and was essential for their expression.

     The study demonstrated that TBP, but not TBP2, is the essential component of the PIC that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated skeletal muscles. TBP2 was not expressed in myotubes derived from either C2C12 myoblasts or primary mouse MuSCs. Furthermore, TBP2-deficient mice displayed an intact regeneration potential, and MuSCs isolated from TBP2-deficient muscles differentiated into myotubes with the same efficiency as wild-type MuSCs.

     The researchers also compared the RNA expression profile of Tbp and Tbpl2 genes in MuSCs isolated from skeletal muscles of wild-type mice by FACS and in the C2C12 myogenic cell line. Tbp expression was detected in both MuSC-derived myotubes and in C2C12 myotubes. The study suggests that the TFIID complex may be present in myogenic cells and may drive muscle gene transcription during myoblast differentiation into myotubes.

     Importantly, the researchers detected comparable numbers of MuSCs in muscles of wild-type and TBP2 null mice 12 days after injury, indicating an intact capacity of adult MuSCs to proliferate, self-renew, and differentiate during ongoing muscle regeneration in the absence of TBP2. These findings demonstrate that TBP2 does not replace TBP during muscle differentiation and that limiting amounts of TFIID-TBP are required to promote muscle-specific gene expression.
Researchers used optogenetics to study the effects of stimulation on large-scale networks in the brains of two macaques. They used a large-scale optogenetic interface to simultaneously stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocorticographic activity across the primary somatosensory ( S1 ) and motor ( M1 ) cortex. The study aimed to investigate how stimulation affects network dynamics and functional connectivity between these areas. Two measures of network connectivity were tracked : the network response to focal stimulation and the baseline coherence between pairs of electrodes. These measures were strongly correlated before stimulation, indicating a robust relationship between them.

     Stimulation in S1 or M1 significantly strengthened the gross functional connectivity between these areas within minutes. However, at a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous connectivity changes across the network. These changes reflected the correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity, consistent with Hebbian plasticity models. Hebbian plasticity suggests that "neurons that fire together, wire together", meaning that neurons that are activated together become more connected.

     The study found that stimulation-evoked correlations were strongly correlated with changes in baseline connectivity across the network. This relationship is well-explained by a Hebbian model of large-scale stimulation-induced plasticity. The results demonstrate that stimulation can drive large-scale network plasticity in a Hebbian manner, with significant implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation. This work extends Hebbian plasticity models to large-scale circuits, providing new insights into the mechanisms of brain reorganization following stimulation.

     The study used a large-scale optogenetic interface to simultaneously stimulate populations of excitatory neurons and record large-scale Î¼-electrocorticography ( Î¼-ECoG ) activity across S1 and M1. The researchers established and compared two measures of functional connectivity that provide complementary views of the mechanisms of plasticity. They found that both measures support a key role for Hebbian learning in sensorimotor cortical plasticity. However, a deeper understanding of the relationship between these bodies of work would require further study of intermediate time-frames and detailed cortical mapping. The large-scale interface used in this study enabled robust estimation of functional connectivity and stimulation-evoked correlations.
summary
     A rare case of a Rhesus macaque monkey, born without primary visual cortex (V1), was studied to understand the neural consequences of such damage. The monkey's in-group behavior was unremarkable, but visual task training was impaired. Magnetic resonance imaging revealed that visual structures outside of the lesion appeared normal. Visual stimulation under anesthesia with checkerboards activated the lateral geniculate nucleus, while full-field moving dots activated the pulvinar. Visual cortical activation was sparse but included face patches.

     Functional connectivity analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V5/MT activation, even without V1. Despite robust subcortical responses to visual stimulation, there was little evidence for strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT supporting residual visual function or blindsight-like phenomena. Visual structures, both cortical and subcortical, outside of the large lesion of primary visual cortex remained intact in the monkey, with little evidence of atrophy.

     The structural connectivity between the subcortical regions and area V5/MT was weak, but the microstructure was intact. A connectivity analysis was performed to understand the nature of the response in area V5/MT. Given the significantly weakened tract between V5/MT and subcortical nuclei, there is little evidence to suggest that a strengthening of these connections underlies whatever residual vision was present in the monkey.

     Residual vision can manifest in several different ways, including the ability to determine information from faces. For visual motion stimuli, foci of activation in the region of V5/MT in dorsal STS of both hemispheres were observed, suggesting any activation may be sub-threshold and difficult to detect with BOLD. Even without the strong visually-evoked V5/MT responses, it was possible to map out a functional network of cortical areas showing a similar BOLD response over time to V5/MT.

     Retinal information may reach the dorsal visual areas via the weak pathways with LGN and pulvinar, but the lack of consistent visual cortex activation, possibly due to the anesthesia, makes this question difficult to answer. The lack of significant differences between the monkey and controls in the pathways between LGN-V5/MT and pulvinar-V5/MT suggests either or both could support residual visual abilities.
summary
     The innate immune system's ability to recognize foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA in the intracellular environment is a crucial defense mechanism against pathogenic infections. However, the molecular mechanisms underlying the distinction between foreign DNA and host genomic material inside the nucleus are not well understood. A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating the role of the nuclear innate immune sensor IFI16. Using biochemical assays and single-molecule techniques, the researchers found that IFI16 scans along exposed foreign dsDNA to assemble into supramolecular signaling platforms. This one-dimensional tracking mechanism allows IFI16 to distinguish between self and non-self DNA.

     The study demonstrated that nucleosomes, which are the basic units of chromatin, act as barriers that prevent IFI16 from targeting host DNA by directly interfering with its one-dimensional movements. This unique scanning-assisted assembly mechanism enables IFI16 to efficiently and selectively assemble into oligomers on foreign DNA. The researchers used time-resolved bulk and single-molecule fluorescence assays to visualize the movement of IFI16 along dsDNA and observed that chromatinization is sufficient to inhibit IFI16 from targeting host dsDNA for assembly.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular mechanism by which innate immune sensors distinguish self from foreign dsDNA in the host nucleus. The researchers propose that the one-dimensional diffusion-mediated assembly plays a key role in regulating the overall IFI16-mediated immune responses. In vivo, this mechanism allows a limited number of IFI16 molecules to allocate each other on large genomes of invading pathogens. The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of the innate immune system's ability to recognize and respond to foreign DNA in the nucleus.
summary
     Scientists have developed a new technique called spatially targeted optical microproteomics (STOMP) to analyze the composition of small protein deposits in tissues. This method uses a combination of two-photon laser scanning microscopy, photochemical affinity labeling, and mass spectrometry to identify the proteins present in specific regions of interest (ROIs) at a resolution of 0.67 Âµm in the xy-plane and 1.48 Âµm axially. The technique does not require genetic manipulation and can be applied to various biological samples, including cell lines, primary cell cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and fixed post-mortem tissue.

To perform STOMP, specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize the ROIs, and then soaked in a solution containing a photo-tag, which is a peptide that forms covalent bonds with proteins upon excitation by laser light. The photo-tagged proteins are then isolated and identified by mass spectrometry. In a test case, STOMP was used to analyze amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse model and a post-mortem AD case, confirming known plaque constituents and discovering new ones.

The results showed that the predominant protein photo-tagged in the STOMP sample was AÎ², which is not visible in the non-plaque experiment due to its low overall abundance. Gel electrophoresis of the indiscriminately photo-tagged proteins revealed a different pattern of protein bands compared to the specimen in which amyloid plaques were specifically targeted for STOMP analysis. The technique has the potential to provide clues to the etiology of neurodegenerative diseases and advance research in cell biology by yielding the composition of small features that are not amenable to biochemical purification.

STOMP represents the first application of two-photon microscopy to affinity photolabeling, offering single-micron-scale three-dimensional resolution that is an order of magnitude better than the current state of the art. The technique has the potential to be applied to various biological samples, including cell lines, primary cell cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and fixed post-mortem tissue. The total volume of tissue that needs to be photo-tagged to obtain sufficient material for mass spectrometry analysis is a subjective matter and depends on instrument sensitivity, fixation method used, sample complexity, and other factors.

     The Frizzled3 (Fz3) gene plays a crucial role in the development of axons in cranial and spinal motor neurons. Research has shown that disruption of the Fz3 gene leads to defects in axonal growth in specific motor nerves, including the VIIth and XIIth cranial motor nerves, the phrenic nerve, and the dorsal motor nerve in fore- and hindlimbs. In Fz3-deficient limbs, dorsal axons stall at a precise location in the nerve plexus and do not reroute to alternative trajectories, unlike other axon path-finding mutants.

     Affected motor neurons undergo cell death two days prior to the normal wave of developmental cell death, which coincides with the innervation of muscle targets. This finding provides in vivo evidence for the idea that developing neurons with long-range axons are programmed to die unless their axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule. The study suggests that planar cell polarity (PCP) signaling is involved in motor axon growth and highlights the question of how PCP proteins, which form cell-cell complexes in epithelia, function in the dynamic context of axonal growth.

     In the limb, the absence of Fz3 leads to a specific stalling defect among lateral motor column (LMCL) axons that is distinct from the phenotypes produced by defects in other signaling systems active in limb axon guidance. Affected cranial and spinal motor neurons die shortly after their axons stall, indicating that the primary cause of motor axon deficiency in Fz3-deficient mice is not defective motor neuron differentiation or excessive motor neuron death, but rather defective axon growth and/or guidance.

     The study found that the majority of Fz3-deficient motor axons do not follow erroneous trajectories, suggesting that aberrant path-finding is unlikely to be the primary cause of the motor axon deficiency. Instead, the research suggests that the precocious motor neuron death in Fz3-deficient embryos may be due to the lack of survival signals from intermediate targets along the axon's trajectory. This idea is supported by in vitro co-culture experiments with spinal cord commissural axons and floor plate tissue.

     The study also found that a discrete subset of motor neurons in Fz3-deficient embryos exhibits both axon growth defects and apoptotic cell death at E11.5-E12.5. This finding suggests that controlling axonal growth may be the only function of Fz3 in motor neurons. The research has implications for our understanding of the role of PCP signaling in motor axon growth and the development of motor neurons.
biomedical researchers investigated the role of transposable elements (TEs) in gene regulation during early mouse development. TEs are repetitive DNA sequences that can jump from one location to another in the genome and are thought to have contributed to the establishment of gene regulatory networks. The study focused on two types of TEs, long terminal repeat (LTR) TE families, and their potential function as enhancers in mouse embryonic stem cells (ESCs) and trophoblast stem cells (TSCs).

The researchers used a combination of epigenomic and transcriptomic data to identify a large number of TEs that are involved in establishing tissue-specific gene expression programs in ESCs and TSCs. In contrast, a wider survey of RLTR13D6 elements in ESCs using CRISPRi revealed that only a minority play significant roles in gene regulation. The researchers also identified a number of TEs that are important for driving expression of lineage-specific genes in ESCs and TSCs.

Scientists have long been fascinated by the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, a complex trait that has independently emerged in at least 60 plant lineages. C4 photosynthesis is a more efficient way for plants to convert sunlight into energy, allowing them to thrive in environments with limited water and nutrients. However, the exact path of its evolution has remained unclear.

     To better understand how C4 photosynthesis evolved, researchers conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C4, or intermediate C3-C4 forms of photosynthesis. They developed a novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model to predict how the C4 phenotype evolved. This approach allowed them to infer the likely evolutionary trajectories connecting phenotypes from sparsely sampled, highly stochastic data.

     The results showed that the emergence of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. The researchers found that the order of C4 trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers. This means that the evolution of C4 photosynthesis was influenced by factors other than photosynthesis itself, such as environmental pressures or genetic mutations.

     The study also revealed that early evolutionary events determined the downstream phenotypes of C4 sub-types by restricting lineages to independent pathways across phenotype space. This suggests that the evolution of C4 photosynthesis was shaped by a combination of genetic and environmental factors. The researchers propose that mechanistic diversity underlies the multiple evolutionary pathways generating C4 photosynthesis, which may be a key factor in facilitating the convergent evolution of complex traits.

     The findings of this study have important implications for efforts to recapitulate the acquisition of C4 photosynthesis through genetic engineering of C3 species. The researchers suggest that including additional early traits in the analysis did not alter the predicted order of the original 16 traits, indicating that their conclusions are robust. Overall, this study provides new insights into the evolution of C4 photosynthesis and highlights the importance of considering non-photosynthetic drivers in the evolution of complex traits.
summary
     Scientists have reengineered the ATPase active site of a bacterial protein called SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity. SpoIVA is a cytoskeletal protein that hydrolyzes ATP to polymerize irreversibly during the sporulation process in Bacillus subtilis. The protein evolved from a TRAFAC class of P-loop GTPases, but the reason for the change in nucleotide specificity from GTP to ATP is unclear. The reengineered protein, SpoIVAGTPase, functioned properly as a GTPase but failed to polymerize because it did not form an NDP (nucleoside diphosphate)-bound intermediate required for polymerization. Further, incubation of SpoIVAGTPase with limiting ATP did not promote efficient polymerization. This suggests that the nucleotide base, in addition to the energy released from hydrolysis, can be critical in specific biological functions.

The study found that SpoIVAGTPase, which can use either ATP or GTP to induce a conformational change after nucleotide hydrolysis, only polymerized in the presence of ATP. This indicates a specific requirement for the nucleotide base, not just the energy released from nucleotide hydrolysis, for its function. The researchers also observed that physiological levels of intracellular ATP, but not GTP, facilitate the requisite conformational changes in SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase.

The study suggests that the switch in nucleotide specificity in SpoIVA from GTP to ATP may have been driven by increased levels of ATP relative to GTP at the end of sporulation. This is consistent with the fact that SpoIVA is actively assembling the spore coat 5-5 hours after the initiation of sporulation. The researchers also found that both SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase failed to undergo nucleotide hydrolysis-mediated structural changes in the presence of low levels of GTP but did so in the presence of high levels of ATP. However, when examining SpoIVA polymerization at different concentrations of ATP, the researchers observed that neither SpoIVA nor SpoIVAGTPase were able to polymerize in the presence of low levels of ATP.

     Arabidopsis heterotrimeric G proteins, composed of XLG2, AGB1, and AGG1/2, play a crucial role in regulating immunity by directly coupling to the FLS2 receptor. The FLS2 receptor perceives bacterial flagellin epitope flg22 to activate defenses through the central cytoplasmic kinase BIK1. In the pre-activation state, XLG2 directly interacts with FLS2 and BIK1, and functions together with AGB1 and AGG1/2 to attenuate proteasome-mediated degradation of BIK1, allowing optimum immune activation. This interaction is essential for maintaining the signaling competence of the FLS2-BIK1 complex.

    Following activation by flg22, XLG2 dissociates from AGB1 and is phosphorylated by BIK1 in the N terminus. The phosphorylated XLG2 enhances the production of reactive oxygen species ( ROS ) likely by modulating the NADPH oxidase RbohD. This post-activation mechanism is critical for the regulation of immune responses. The study demonstrates that the G proteins are directly coupled to the FLS2 receptor complex and regulate immune signaling through both pre-activation and post-activation mechanisms.

    The introduction of a transgenic copy of BIK1 largely restored flg22-triggered immune responses and disease resistance in xlg2 and agb1 mutants, indicating that the heterotrimeric G protein is essential for maintaining the signaling competence of the FLS2-BIK1 complex in the pre-activation state. The results also show that the G proteins dynamically interact with the FLS2-BIK1 receptor complex during FLS2 signaling, and the findings are consistent with the observed defects in flg22-induced responses and RbohD phosphorylation in the G protein mutants.

    The study highlights the importance of heterotrimeric G proteins in plant immunity and provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying FLS2-mediated immune responses. The findings suggest that plant receptor kinases can directly couple to heterotrimeric G proteins, similar to G protein-coupled receptors ( GPCRs ) in animals. The study also demonstrates that the G proteins coupled to the FLS2 receptor complex are subject to multiple regulations during FLS2 signaling, underscoring the complexity of plant immune signaling pathways.
Scientists have studied the Arabidopsis circadian clock, a system that regulates gene expression across the day-night cycle, to understand how it functions at the single-cell level. Previous research has identified a multiple feedback loop circuit that generates the 24-hour rhythm, but it remains unclear how robust the clock is in individual cells and how clock timing is coordinated across the plant. To address this, researchers examined clock activity in Arabidopsis seedlings at the single-cell level over several days under constant environmental conditions.

The study found that individual cells exhibit robust oscillations, but these oscillations are desynchronized. Two waves of clock activity were observed: one traveling down the root and the other up the root. The researchers also found evidence of cell-to-cell coupling, particularly in the root tip. A simple model suggested that the combination of cell-to-cell coupling and period differences between cells can generate the observed waves of clock gene expression.

The study estimated the coupling strength between cells and found evidence of coupling, especially in the root tip. A model showed that the observed period differences, combined with cell-to-cell coupling, can produce the observed waves in clock gene expression. The results reveal the spatial structure of the plant clock and suggest that the Arabidopsis clock has multiple coordination points, unlike the centralized mammalian clock.

The study's findings contradict previous hierarchical models of the plant clock, which do not explain the observed short period oscillations in the root tip or the wave of clock gene expression up from the root tip. Instead, the results support a more decentralized model of clock coordination in plants. The study's use of a reporter for the core clock gene CIRCADIAN CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1 (CCA1) allowed for the examination of the dynamics of the Arabidopsis clock across the whole plant at the single-cell level over several days.

The researchers calculated the order parameter from their single-cell data and estimated the coupling strength based on a technique developed for mammalian circadian cells. The results show that the damping of rhythms at the whole-plant level is mainly due to desynchronization of oscillating single cells with different periods, rather than noise in gene expression or damping of individual oscillations. Overall, the study provides new insights into the structure and robustness of the plant circadian clock system.

     summary
Researchers have developed a new imaging technique using adaptive optics to noninvasively measure single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels in the living retina of mice. The technique, called AOSLO imaging, uses an ultrafast camera to capture images of blood cells without the need for foreign contrast agents or surgery. This allows for the measurement of blood flow in vessels with diameters ranging from 3.2 to 45.8 micrometers, covering the full spectrum of retinal vessel sizes.

Using this technique, the researchers were able to measure the speed of hundreds of thousands of blood cells per second, providing a high-throughput velocity measurement. The imaging paradigm was paired with an automated analysis workflow, enabling the measurement of single-cell velocity and flow rate in the smallest to largest vessels of the mouse retinal circulation. The results showed that the flow rate ranged from 0.0002 to 1.55 microliters per minute across the full range of vessel sizes.

The study's findings have significant implications for the understanding of microvascular diseases that originate in capillaries, which are difficult to evaluate early on due to the limitations of current imaging techniques. The researchers suggest that the eye may serve as a window to supplement the understanding of microcirculation dynamics in the central nervous system and systemic microvascular health. Additionally, the technique may be directly applicable to clinical approaches using the same instrumentation.

The study's imaging capabilities enabled single blood cell resolution as they flowed in both tight and sparsely packed formations inside microvessels, allowing for sub-micrometer precision when measuring the lumen diameters of retinal vessels. A total of 123 vessels in 19 mice were studied using this technique, providing a comprehensive understanding of blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels of the mouse retinal circulation. The results of this study have the potential to advance our understanding of microvascular diseases and may lead to the development of new diagnostic and therapeutic approaches.
summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how plants form symbiotic relationships with other organisms, such as fungi and bacteria, to acquire essential nutrients like phosphate and nitrogen. A key receptor-like kinase called Symbiosis Receptor-like Kinase (SYMRK) plays a crucial role in the development of these symbiotic relationships. However, the mechanisms that determine whether a plant will form a symbiotic relationship with a fungus or a bacterium have remained unclear.

     In this study, researchers investigated the function of SYMRK and two other receptor-like kinases, Nod Factor Receptor 1 (NFR1) and NFR5, in the development of symbiotic relationships in the legume plant Lotus japonicus. The researchers found that overexpressing NFR1, NFR5, or SYMRK in the plant's roots led to the spontaneous formation of nodules, which are specialized organs where nitrogen-fixing bacteria live and fix nitrogen for the plant.

     The researchers also found that NFR1 and NFR5 associated with endogenous SYMRK in the plant's roots, suggesting that these receptors work together to initiate the symbiotic relationship. However, overexpressing NFR1 or NFR5 alone did not trigger the formation of nodules, while overexpressing SYMRK did. This suggests that SYMRK plays a key role in the decision between forming a symbiotic relationship with a fungus or a bacterium.

     The researchers propose that SYMRK may form complexes with other receptors, including NFR1 and NFR5, to initiate the symbiotic relationship. This complex formation may lead to the release of negative regulatory mechanisms, allowing the plant to form a symbiotic relationship with either a fungus or a bacterium. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of symbiotic relationships in plants and has important implications for our understanding of how plants interact with their environment.

     The researchers also found that overexpressing SYMRK, but not NFR1 or NFR5, triggered the expression of genes related to arbuscular mycorrhizal (AM) symbiosis, a symbiotic relationship between plants and fungi. This suggests that SYMRK plays a key role in the decision between forming a symbiotic relationship with a fungus or a bacterium.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of symbiotic relationships in plants and has important implications for our understanding of how plants interact with their environment. The discovery of the role of SYMRK in the decision between forming a symbiotic relationship with a fungus or a bacterium has the potential to improve our understanding of plant biology and may have applications in agriculture and biotechnology.
summary
     A new software toolkit called DeepPoseKit has been developed to estimate animal posture using deep learning models. The toolkit addresses the limitations of current animal pose estimation methods in terms of speed and robustness. DeepPoseKit uses an efficient multi-scale deep-learning model called Stacked DenseNet and a fast GPU-based peak-detection algorithm to estimate keypoint locations with subpixel precision. This approach improves processing speed by more than two times compared to existing methods without compromising accuracy. The toolkit is designed to be easy to use and has been demonstrated on multiple challenging animal pose estimation tasks in laboratory and field settings, including groups of interacting individuals. The code, documentation, and examples for DeepPoseKit are freely available under a permissive open-source license.

     DeepPoseKit builds on the state-of-the-art for individual pose estimation using convolutional neural networks, achieving fast inference without reducing accuracy or generalization ability. The Stacked DenseNet model offers significant improvements over existing models and can run inference at a rate of 30-110 Hz, depending on the resolution of the predicted confidence maps. In contrast to conventional computer-vision methods, deep-learning-based methods can achieve near-human-level accuracy in various scenarios by manually annotating data and training a general-purpose image-processing algorithm.

     The development of DeepPoseKit aims to reduce barriers to using advanced tools for measuring behavior and has broad applicability across the behavioral sciences. The toolkit has the potential to lead to the creation of larger, higher-resolution, and more reliable behavioral datasets, which could revolutionize our understanding of relationships between behavior, the brain, and the environment. Overall, DeepPoseKit provides a powerful tool for automatically measuring animal posture from images and videos, enabling researchers to collect more accurate and detailed data on animal behavior.
summary
     Biomedical research has identified a novel mechanism by which innate immune cells, such as macrophages, respond to environmental stresses or infection by pathogens. When these cells are challenged, they form multi-protein complexes called inflammasomes, which initiate pro-inflammatory responses and a form of cell death known as pyroptosis. A recent study has revealed that the disruption of glycolytic flux, or the breakdown of glucose for energy production, serves as an intracellular trigger for NLRP3-mediated inflammatory signaling, IL-1Î² production, and pyroptosis in primed murine bone marrow-derived macrophages. This signal is mediated by a drop in NADH levels and the induction of mitochondrial reactive oxygen species (ROS) production, which can be rescued by the addition of products that restore NADH production.

The study found that the intracellular pathogen Salmonella typhimurium impairs NADH production, leading to the formation of mitochondrial ROS, which are essential for NLRP3 inflammasome activation. This suggests that disruption of glycolytic flux is a biologically relevant trigger of NLRP3 inflammasome activation, mediated by mitochondrial redox changes. The researchers propose that innate immune cells sense perturbed metabolite production and flux through the glycolytic pathway, activating NLRP3 to initiate inflammatory responses.

Inhibitors of flux-limiting enzymes and S. typhimurium effectively limited glycolysis, resulting in NLRP3-mediated inflammasome formation and pyroptosis. The study also found that the NAD+ consumption rate increased upon pyruvate treatment, indicating induction of productive metabolism downstream of glycolysis in infected BMDMs. These data indicate that glycolytic perturbation is a mechanism by which innate immune cells sense altered homeostasis during S. typhimurium infection and induce a pro-inflammatory response via NLRP3 inflammasome formation and pyroptotic cell death.

Furthermore, the study suggests that innate immune cells sense metabolic perturbation as a danger signal, resulting in inflammasome formation, caspase-1 activation, and cytokine secretion. Disrupting glycolytic flux serves as a trigger for inflammation and cell death in macrophages, and supplementation of the glycolytic product pyruvate resulted in significant reductions in caspase-1 activation, IL-1Î² secretion, and cell death induced by GB111-NH2. These findings reveal an important inflammatory signaling network used by immune cells to sense metabolic dysfunction or infection by intracellular pathogens.
Summary
     The PALB2 protein plays a crucial role in maintaining chromosomal integrity by facilitating homology-directed repair (HDR) of DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs). PALB2 interacts with BRCA1 and BRCA2, two tumor suppressors linked to breast cancer and Fanconi anemia, to coordinate their functions during HDR. In addition to its role as a scaffold protein, PALB2 binds directly to DNA, but the functional significance of this interaction was poorly understood.

     Researchers have identified a major DNA-binding site on PALB2, mutations in which reduce RAD51 foci formation and HDR efficiency by 50% in cells. The N-terminal DNA-binding domain (N-DBD) of PALB2 stimulates the function of RAD51 recombinase, which is essential for HDR. Surprisingly, the N-DBD also exhibits strand exchange activity without the presence of RAD51, and can use both DNA and RNA substrates. This novel activity highlights the versatility of PALB2's DNA interaction properties.

     Mutations in four specific amino acids within the DNA-binding site significantly impair RAD51 foci formation and HDR efficiency. The N-DBD of PALB2 can stimulate RAD51-mediated strand exchange in vitro, even in the absence of RAD51. This suggests that PALB2's DNA-binding activity is critical for the recruitment of BRCA2 and RAD51 to DSB sites and efficient DNA repair in cells.

     The study demonstrates that PALB2's interaction with DNA is essential for its function in HDR. The PALB2 N-DBD's ability to stimulate RAD51-mediated strand exchange and use RNA as a substrate reveals a novel activity of PALB2. These findings highlight the importance of PALB2's DNA-binding activity in maintaining chromosomal integrity and suggest that mutations in PALB2's DNA-binding site may contribute to the development of cancer. Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying PALB2's role in DNA repair and its potential as a target for cancer therapy.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have made a significant discovery regarding the structure and function of MreB, a prokaryotic actin homologue. Unlike other actin-like proteins, which form filaments with parallel protofilaments, MreB from Caulobacter crescentus forms antiparallel double filaments. This unique arrangement allows each subunit to interact with the membrane and membrane-bound components of the elongasome.

     The researchers used crystal structures and electron microscopy to visualize the antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments in vitro and in vivo. They found that a protofilament doublet is essential for MreB's function in cell shape maintenance and demonstrated the antiparallel orientation of MreB protofilaments in E. coli using in vivo site-specific cross-linking. Three-dimensional cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) revealed that pairs of protofilaments of Caulobacter MreB tightly bind to membranes.

     Crystal structures of different nucleotide and polymerization states of Caulobacter MreB showed conserved conformational changes accompanying antiparallel filament formation. The researchers also found that the antimicrobial agents A22/MP265 bind close to the bound nucleotide of MreB, presumably preventing nucleotide hydrolysis and destabilizing double protofilaments.

     The antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments ensures that the amphipathic helix of both protofilaments can bind to the membrane and that both protofilaments can interact with bitopic proteins, such as RodZ. Deleting the N-terminal amphipathic helix and mutating the residues in the hydrophobic loop made the protein (ÎCcMreBdh) less prone to aggregation. The crystal structure of Caulobacter MreB (ÎCcMreBdh) revealed the typical actin fold, containing two domains on either side of a nucleotide binding cleft.

     These findings provide new insights into the structure and function of MreB and its role in cell shape maintenance. The discovery of the antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments expands our understanding of the actin family of proteins and has implications for the development of new antimicrobial agents.
(summary)
     Biologists have long recognized the importance of invariant floral forms in the reproductive success of plants. In Arabidopsis thaliana, flowers consistently have four petals, whereas in the closely related species Cardamine hirsuta, petal number varies. To understand the genetic basis for this difference, researchers investigated the role of the pleiotropic floral regulator APETALA1 (AP1) in petal number robustness. They found that divergence in AP1 between the two species can account for the species-specific difference in petal number robustness. Specifically, Arabidopsis thaliana AP1 masks the phenotypic expression of quantitative trait loci (QTL) controlling petal number in Cardamine hirsuta, thereby conferring robustness. In contrast, Cardamine hirsuta AP1 fails to complement this function of Arabidopsis thaliana AP1, resulting in variable petal number.

     The researchers also discovered that upstream regulatory regions of AP1 contribute to the divergence in petal number robustness between the two species. Furthermore, they found that variable petal number is maintained in Cardamine hirsuta despite sufficient standing genetic variation in natural accessions to produce plants with four-petalled flowers. This suggests that AP1 divergence likely exposed cryptic genetic variation in Cardamine hirsuta that contributes directly to maintaining variable petal number within natural accessions of the species.

     The study identified AP1 as a gene of major effect in the evolutionary transition from a robust phenotype of four petals in Arabidopsis thaliana to a variable petal number in Cardamine hirsuta. However, AP1 polymorphisms do not contribute directly to within-species variation in Cardamine hirsuta petal number. Instead, the decanalization of petal number in Arabidopsis thaliana reflects its robustness to genetic variation. The researchers were able to change Arabidopsis thaliana petal number to variable by complementing ap1 mutants with the AP1 genomic locus from Cardamine hirsuta. These flowers mimicked the variable petal number found in Cardamine hirsuta, suggesting that there may be variability in the gene regulatory network controlling petal number in Arabidopsis thaliana that is hidden beneath the uniformity of wild-type development.
(summary)
     Biomedical research has identified proinsulin misfolding as an early event in the progression to type 2 diabetes. Proinsulin is a precursor to insulin, a hormone critical for metabolic homeostasis, and its proper folding is essential for its function. In normal pancreatic islets, a subset of proinsulin molecules contains at least one free cysteine thiol. However, in islets with a perturbed endoplasmic reticulum (ER) folding environment, non-native proinsulin forms intermolecular disulfide-linked complexes. These complexes are more abundant in genetically obese mice with otherwise wild-type islets and in leptin receptor-deficient mice, where they track with the onset of islet insulin deficiency and diabetes.

     The study found that proinsulin-Cys (B19) and Cys (A20) are necessary and sufficient for the formation of proinsulin disulfide-linked complexes. Proinsulin Cys (B19)-Cys (B19) covalent homodimers resist reductive dissociation, highlighting a structural basis for aberrant proinsulin complex formation. The research also established that proinsulin misfolding includes the inability to successfully complete its three internal disulfide bonds, with a subfraction of proinsulin molecules in the ER bearing unpaired cysteine residues. These improperly folded proinsulin molecules enter into aberrant disulfide-linked partnerships with other proinsulin molecules in the ER, resulting in misfolded proinsulin complexes.

     The study used several independent lines of evidence to establish the presence of aberrant disulfide-linked proinsulin complexes in the Î²-cells of human islets and model systems, in states that alter the ER folding environment, and in T2D progression prior to onset of Î²-cell dedifferentiation or death. In genetically obese mice with otherwise wild-type islets, disulfide-linked complexes of proinsulin are more abundant, and in leptin receptor-deficient mice, the further increase of such complexes tracks with the onset of islet insulin deficiency and diabetes. The research suggests that increased proinsulin misfolding via disulfide-linked complexes is an early event associated with prediabetes that worsens with Î²-cell dysfunction in type 2 diabetes.
summary
     The article investigates the role of Suv39h1, a histone H3 lysine 9 (H3K9)-specific methyltransferase, in assembling silent heterochromatin. Suv39h1 recognizes trimethylated H3K9 (H3K9me3) via its chromodomain (CD) and targets specific chromosomal regions. However, the detailed targeting mechanisms, especially for naÃ¯ve chromatin without preexisting H3K9me3, are poorly understood.

     The study reveals that Suv39h1's CD (Suv39h1-CD) binds nucleic acids, with a higher affinity for RNA than DNA, and this binding is independent of its H3K9me3 recognition. Suv39h1 binds major satellite RNAs in vivo, and knockdown of major satellite RNAs lowers Suv39h1 retention on pericentromere. Mutational studies indicate that both the nucleic acid-binding and H3K9me-binding activities of Suv39h1-CD are crucial for its pericentric heterochromatin assembly.

     The results suggest that chromatin-bound RNAs contribute to creating Suv39H's target specificity. Suv39h1's nucleic acid-binding activity integrates and cooperates with its recognition and binding of methylated H3K9 to establish/maintain Suv39h1-mediated pericentric heterochromatin, including H3K9me3 formation, HP1 accumulation, and transcriptionally repressive status.

     The study also shows that Suv39h1's RNA-binding activity contributes to its stable association with H3K9me3-rich pericentromeric regions. Knockdown of major satellite repeat transcripts further supports the notion that RNA binding of Suv39h1 contributes to its stability on chromatin. The results demonstrate that the four different activities of Suv39h1, including nucleic acid-binding, H3K9me-binding, HP1-binding, and chromatin-binding, cooperatively regulate Suv39h1-mediated pericentric heterochromatin assembly.
Summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the entorhinal cortex, a region of the brain involved in spatial navigation, contains grid cells that allow for precise decoding of position in space. These grid cells have been hypothesized to contribute to mental operations beyond navigation, but this requires that their activity does not depend on physical movement. A recent study investigated whether grid cell activity in the entorhinal cortex is modulated by spatial attention, even in the absence of movement.

     The study used a task where monkeys were trained to maintain central fixation while covertly attending to a stimulus moving in the periphery. The researchers recorded the activity of 141 neurons in the entorhinal cortex of two rhesus macaque monkeys performing this task. The results showed that a significant population of entorhinal cells (20/141, 14%) exhibited spatially structured receptive fields with a triangular tiling of space, similar to grid cells.

     This finding contrasts with recordings obtained in the hippocampus, where grid-like representations were not observed. The study's results provide evidence that neurons in the macaque entorhinal cortex do not rely on physical movement to activate grid cells. Instead, the activation of these cells is modulated by spatial attention, even in the absence of movement.

     The study's findings support the hypothesis that grid cells in the entorhinal cortex participate in a range of cognitive functions, including attentional processes. The results also suggest that the neural substrate for overt and covert attention is largely overlapping, and that grid cells may represent not only gaze location but also the locus of attention in general.

     The study's methodology involved training monkeys to perform a covert attention tracking task, where they maintained central fixation while attending to a small dot moving around a computer monitor. The monkeys performed the task at >70% accuracy, with the majority of errors due to saccades to outside of the fixation window. The researchers evaluated the standard gridness measure to examine spatial representations among entorhinal neurons.

     Overall, the study's results provide strong evidence that the activation of cells in the entorhinal cortex with spatially structured firing fields does not require physical movement through an environment. Instead, the activation of these cells is modulated by spatial attention, even in the absence of movement. This finding has implications for our understanding of the neural basis of spatial cognition and attentional processes.
Summary
     Researchers have developed a standardized task to measure decision-making in mice, which can be reproduced across multiple laboratories. The task, designed for head-fixed mice, assesses perceptual and value-based decision-making. To ensure reproducibility, the researchers standardized the training protocol, experimental hardware, software, and procedures across seven laboratories in three countries. A total of 140 mice were trained, and over 5 million mouse choices were collected into a publicly available database.

     The results show that while learning speed varied across mice and laboratories, once training was complete, there were no significant differences in behavior across laboratories. Mice from different laboratories relied similarly on visual stimuli, past successes and failures, and estimates of stimulus prior probability to guide their choices. These findings demonstrate that a complex mouse behavior can be reproduced across multiple laboratories, establishing a standard for reproducible rodent behavior.

     To facilitate reuse and reproducibility, the researchers adopted an open science approach, sharing the hardware and software components, as well as the experimental protocols. This approach allows the broader community to access and build upon the data and techniques used in the study. The results provide an unprecedented dataset and open-access tools for studying decision-making in mice, which can be leveraged by researchers to explore the neural basis of decision-making.

     The study's findings have implications for the field of neuroscience, where reproducibility has been a concern. By establishing a standardized task and protocol, researchers can now compare results across laboratories, knowing that any differences are unlikely to be due to differences in behavior. The study also aims to catalyze the development of new adaptations and variations of the approach, accelerating the use of mice in high-quality, reproducible studies of neural correlates of decision-making. Overall, the study provides a path toward achieving reproducibility in neuroscience through collaborative open-science approaches.
Researchers have created an atlas of gene expression and cell types in the intestine of Schmidtea mediterranea, a planarian flatworm capable of regenerating all organs, including the gut. Using laser-capture microdissection (LCM) and RNA sequencing (RNA-seq), they identified hundreds of genes with intestinal expression that were previously undetected. The study revealed extensive conservation of digestive physiology and cell types with other animals, including humans.

The researchers found that the planarian intestine expresses genes involved in metabolism, nutrient storage and transport, innate immunity, and other physiological roles, demonstrating functional homology with digestive systems of other animals. They also identified 22 intestine-enriched transcription factors, including several required for the production and/or maintenance of goblet cells. Goblet cells play a crucial role in the production of mucus, which is essential for the proper functioning of the digestive system.

Using LCM, the researchers were able to uncover previously unappreciated regionalization of gene expression in the planarian intestine along the medio-lateral axis, particularly among intestinal goblet cells. They identified two intestine-enriched transcription factors, gli-1 and RREB2, which play distinct roles in goblet cell differentiation and maintenance. Depletion of goblet cells was found to reduce the planarians' willingness to feed and viability, with only negligible effects on the regeneration of other intestinal cell types or non-intestinal tissues.

The study provides a foundational resource for investigating mechanisms involved in gastrointestinal function, repair, and regeneration. The characterization of the planarian intestinal transcriptome enables the identification of genes required for regeneration and functions of distinct intestinal cell types. The findings have implications for understanding the evolutionary conservation of intestinal gene expression and the consolidation of physiological roles performed by multiple human digestive organs in the planarian intestine. The study sets the stage for further investigation of mechanisms involved in gastrointestinal function, repair, and regeneration.
summary
     Biomedical research has revealed a novel mechanism of peripheral sensory coding in weakly electric fish, where sensory receptors with spontaneous oscillatory activity respond to stimuli by resetting their phase, resulting in transient synchrony among receptors. This synchrony encodes signal timing and location, but not waveform. The receptors are most sensitive to frequencies found in the collective signals of groups of conspecifics, and this sensitivity is correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies.

     The study investigated how evolutionary divergence in sensory perception relates to differences in peripheral sensory coding. Mormyrid fish communicate by producing a species-specific electric organ discharge (EOD) at variable interpulse intervals (IPIs). However, oscillating receptors are tuned to much lower frequencies than the peak power frequencies of conspecific EODs. These oscillation frequencies confer enhanced sensitivity to IPIs that can only be produced by groups of fish. In contrast, spiking receptors are specialized for detecting signals from individuals.

     Behavioral playback experiments demonstrated that a species with oscillating receptors responded strongly to submillisecond IPIs within the range of enhanced receptor sensitivity. This suggests that the novel peripheral coding mechanism may represent an adaptation for detecting communication signals from large groups of fish. The study also found that oscillatory electrical activity is widespread in neural networks, and that species with oscillating receptors exhibit selective behavioral responses to submillisecond IPIs, while species with spiking receptors do not.

     The findings provide evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony and suggest that different perceptual capabilities correspond to different receptor physiologies. The study hypothesizes that these divergent mechanisms represent adaptations for different social environments. The research provides new insights into the neural basis of sensory perception and communication in weakly electric fish, and has implications for our understanding of the evolution of sensory systems in response to environmental pressures.
