Scientists have studied how the dengue virus (DENV) adapts to its two main hosts: humans and mosquitoes. To understand this process, researchers used a technique called ultra-deep sequencing to analyze the genetic changes that occur in DENV populations when they are grown in human and mosquito cells. This allowed them to identify the effects of individual genetic mutations on the virus's fitness, or ability to survive and reproduce.

The study found that the DENV population is constantly accumulating deleterious mutations, which are genetic changes that harm the virus's ability to function. However, these deleterious mutations are balanced by rare beneficial mutations that help the virus adapt to its host environment. The researchers discovered that beneficial mutations tend to occur in specific regions of the DENV genome and are often found in areas of the viral proteome that are structurally flexible.

The study also identified "hotspots" of adaptation, which are specific locations in the genome where beneficial mutations are more likely to occur. These hotspots were found to be enriched in regions of the DENV genome that are involved in interactions with the host cell. The researchers used a three-dimensional model of the dengue protein E to visualize these hotspots and understand how they contribute to the virus's ability to adapt to its host.

The findings of this study suggest that the evolution of DENV is shaped by a balance between the accumulation of deleterious mutations and the occurrence of beneficial mutations that help the virus adapt to its host environment. The study also highlights the importance of understanding the structural and functional features of the DENV genome and proteome in shaping the virus's ability to evolve and adapt.

The researchers used a technique called CirSeq to analyze the fitness landscapes of DENV populations adapting to human and mosquito cells. This technique allowed them to trace the trajectories of individual allele trajectories for almost all possible single nucleotide variants across the DENV genome. The study found that adaptation relies on host-specific beneficial mutations that are clustered in specific regions of the DENV genome and enriched in regions of the proteome that exhibit structural flexibility.

The study's findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of DENV and other viruses that cycle between different host environments. The results suggest that the evolutionary constraints observed in this study may be relevant to other DENV and Zika strains, highlighting the importance of understanding the biophysical and biological constraints that shape long-term viral evolution.
Using single-particle tracking with photoactivated localization microscopy (spt-PALM) and detailed trajectory analysis, researchers have identified distinct membrane domains that dictate the diffusion and trafficking of KRasG12D, an active mutant of the KRas protein, in U2OS cells. The study reveals that KRasG12D exhibits an immobile state in approximately 70-nanometer domains, which are embedded in larger domains of around 200 nanometers that confer intermediate mobility. The remaining membrane supports fast diffusion of KRasG12D.

The researchers found that KRasG12D is continuously removed from the membrane via the immobile state and replenished to the fast state, a process reminiscent of Ras internalization and recycling. Importantly, the partitioning of KRasG12D into the three states remains invariant over a wide range of protein expression levels, demonstrating that KRasG12D diffusion and trafficking through the three mobility states and associated nanodomains is in a maintained, homeostatic condition.

Further analysis revealed that the intermediate and immobile states of KRasG12D are each associated with distinct membrane domains. The measured sizes of the immobilization and intermediate domains of KRasG12D were approximately 70 nanometers and 200 nanometers, respectively, consistent with previous beliefs that nanoscopic membrane domains regulate Ras organization on the membrane.

Based on these results, the researchers propose a new model to describe the membrane dynamics of KRasG12D, where nested membrane nanodomains dictate the diffusion and trafficking, with implications in Ras multimer formation and signaling. The study sheds new light on how complex nanodomains organize on the membrane to dictate Ras diffusion and trafficking, offering useful guidance for future experiments that aim to determine the molecular and structural identities of the Ras-associated membrane nanodomains and define the mechanisms of Ras multimer formation and signaling. The findings suggest that the various membrane nanodomains directly influence the mobility, trafficking, and potentially multimer formation and signaling of KRasG12D, although details of the trafficking and multimer formation processes are yet to be defined.
Summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how the Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) signaling gradient forms during embryonic development, particularly in patterning the dorsoventral (DV) axis of vertebrates and invertebrates. The prevailing view in vertebrates suggests that a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists, along with ligand shuttling, generates peak signaling levels. However, a recent study in zebrafish has shed new light on this mechanism.

     The researchers developed a robust quantitative imaging method to directly measure the BMP signaling gradient in wild-type and mutant zebrafish embryos. They combined these data with a mathematical model-based computational screen to test hypotheses for gradient formation. The analysis ruled out a BMP shuttling mechanism and a BMP transcriptionally-informed gradient mechanism. Instead, the study supports a source-sink mechanism, where a restricted BMP antagonist distribution acts as a sink that drives BMP flux dorsally and gradient formation.

     The researchers measured Bmp2 diffusion and found that it supports the source-sink model, suggesting a new mechanism to shape BMP gradients during development. This finding contradicts the prevailing view that a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists generates the BMP signaling gradient. The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of BMP signaling and its role in patterning the DV axis during embryonic development.

     Bone Morphogenetic Proteins (BMPs) act as morphogens repeatedly during development, including in patterning the embryonic DV axis, the neural tube, and the Drosophila wing disc. BMP morphogen systems are established by a network of extracellular regulators. In vertebrates, the mechanism by which the BMP ligands and antagonists shape this gradient is unclear. Several potential mechanisms have been proposed, including an inverse gradient of BMP antagonists and BMP antagonists generating peak BMP signaling levels by shuttling BMP ligand to these regions.

     The study's findings suggest that the BMP signaling gradient in zebrafish is formed through a source-sink mechanism, where a restricted BMP antagonist distribution acts as a sink that drives BMP flux dorsally and gradient formation. This new understanding of BMP gradient formation has significant implications for our understanding of BMP signaling and its role in patterning the DV axis during embryonic development.
Summary
     The lysosomal transporter MFSD1 plays a crucial role in maintaining liver homeostasis. MFSD1 is a member of the major facilitator superfamily of transporters, which mediate secondary active or passive transport processes across cellular membranes. Research has shown that MFSD1 is localized in lysosomes, where it facilitates the export of low-molecular-weight catabolic end-products. However, the function and regulation of MFSD1 remained poorly understood until recently.

     A detailed analysis of MFSD1 revealed that it is not N-glycosylated and contains a dileucine-based sorting motif necessary for its transport to lysosomes. Mice lacking MFSD1 (Mfsd1 knockout mice) developed splenomegaly and severe liver disease, indicating the importance of MFSD1 in maintaining normal liver function. Proteomics analysis of isolated lysosomes from Mfsd1 knockout mice identified GLMP as a critical accessory subunit for MFSD1. GLMP is a highly glycosylated lysosomal protein of unknown function prior to this study.

     The interaction between MFSD1 and GLMP is essential for maintaining normal levels of MFSD1 in lysosomes, and vice versa. Mice lacking GLMP (Glmp knockout mice) exhibited a similar phenotype to Mfsd1 knockout mice, further supporting the importance of the MFSD1/GLMP complex in liver homeostasis. These findings suggest that MFSD1 and GLMP form a tightly linked lysosomal membrane protein transporter complex.

     The study also investigated the role of MFSD1 in lysosomal function by analyzing the composition of lysosomes from wildtype and Mfsd1 knockout mice. The results showed that MFSD1 is endogenously localized in lysosomes under steady-state conditions and is ubiquitously expressed in murine tissues. The transport of MFSD1 to lysosomes is mediated by a dileucine-based sorting motif in the N-terminus.

     Overall, the research highlights the importance of MFSD1 and its accessory subunit GLMP in maintaining liver homeostasis and suggests that the MFSD1/GLMP complex plays a critical role in lysosomal function. The findings provide new insights into the regulation and function of lysosomal transporters and have implications for understanding the pathogenesis of liver disease.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have made significant progress in understanding the mechanism of kinesin motors, which are essential for various cellular processes in eukaryotes. Kinesin motors use ATP hydrolysis to transport cargo along microtubules. To gain insight into this process, the researchers developed atomic models of the monomeric kinesin motor domain on microtubules using cryo-EM reconstructions at 5-6 Ã resolution. These models were compared with existing X-ray structures of ADP-bound kinesin to infer a mechanistic scheme.

     The researchers found that microtubule attachment, mediated by a conserved 'linchpin' residue in kinesin (N255), triggers a clamshell opening of the nucleotide cleft and release of ADP. Binding of ATP re-closes the cleft, tightly coupling to translocation of cargo via kinesin's 'neck linker' element. This structural transition is similar to the nucleotide-exchange steps in myosin and F1-ATPase motors.

     The researchers also identified sites in the kinesin fold that uncouple the motor's nucleotide affinity from its microtubule affinity, providing a structural basis for kinesin's nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules. To clarify the mechanism of kinesin's microtubule-attached cycle, the researchers solved high-resolution cryo-EM reconstructions of the human monomeric kinesin motor domain attached to the microtubule in two different chemical states, representing the motor before and after ATP binding.

     The no-nucleotide and ATP-bound models of the kinesin-microtubule complex revealed a clamshell-like closure of kinesin's nucleotide cleft triggered by ATP binding on microtubules. Cryo-EM analysis of the ADP and ATP analog-bound states of the N255K point mutant of the construct supported this hypothesis, revealing two previously unidentified binding modes of kinesin in which the motor domain attaches tightly to the microtubule but is rotationally mobile. The corresponding 3D maps did not refine to high resolution due to mobility of the kinesin heads.
(summary)
     Maintaining the proper functioning of mitochondria requires mechanisms to identify and remove misfolded proteins. However, the quality control (QC) pathways for misfolded mitochondrial proteins are not well understood. Researchers have identified two temperature-sensitive (ts-) peripheral mitochondrial outer membrane (MOM) proteins, sen2-1HAts and sam35-2HAts, as novel model QC substrates in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae. These proteins are degraded from the MOM by the ubiquitin-proteasome system.

The ubiquitination of sen2-1HAts is mediated by the ubiquitin ligase (E3) Ubr1, while sam35-2HAts is primarily ubiquitinated by San1. The degradation of both substrates requires the SSA family of Hsp70s and the Hsp40 Sis1, providing the first evidence for chaperone involvement in mitochondrial-associated degradation (MAD). In addition to the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 AAA-ATPase complex, Doa1 and a mitochondrial pool of the transmembrane Cdc48 adaptor, Ubx2, are also implicated in their degradation.

This study reveals a unique QC pathway that involves a combination of cytosolic and mitochondrial factors, distinguishing it from other cellular QC pathways. The researchers found that ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation play a crucial role in the turnover of sam35-2HAts and sen2-1HAts at the MOM. The Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex is essential for the efficient proteasomal degradation of ubiquitinated mitochondrial QC substrates, and it can be recruited to substrates via Cdc48's interaction with co-factors.

Mitochondrial proteins are subject to ongoing oxidative insults that can result in damage, misfolding, and dysfunction. The researchers further assessed whether ubiquitin-modified forms of the ts- proteins were localized to mitochondria under conditions of proteasome impairment. They found that ubiquitinated sam35-2HAts and sen2-1HAts accumulated largely in the mitochondrial pellet fraction and to a lesser extent in the soluble fraction, correlating with the relative distributions of unmodified species.

Deletion of the proteasome shuttling factor Ddi1 did not affect the degradation of sam35-2HAts or sen2-1HAts, further distinguishing MAD from ERAD. These results indicate that ubiquitinated mitochondrial QC substrates require the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex for efficient proteasomal degradation. Overall, this study provides new insights into the QC pathways for misfolded mitochondrial proteins and highlights the importance of the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex in their degradation.
Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how growth signals are generated and propagated throughout tissues, particularly in the epidermis, the outermost layer of the skin. Extracellular signal-regulated kinase (ERK) plays a crucial role in this process, acting as a key effector of many growth signaling pathways. To visualize ERK activity in living mice, scientists developed an ERK FÃ¶rster resonance energy transfer (FRET) biosensor, which allowed them to monitor ERK activity in epidermal cells at single-cell resolution.

    Using this biosensor, the researchers observed that ERK activity propagated radially from cell to cell in the epidermis, a phenomenon they termed "Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity Distribution" (SPREAD). This propagation occurred in bursts, with ERK activity originating from a few cells and spreading up to a radius of approximately 50 micrometers over 30 minutes before disappearing. The frequency of SPREADs correlated with the rate of epidermal cell division, suggesting a link between ERK activity and cell proliferation.

    The researchers also found that SPREADs were stimulated by 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol 13-acetate (TPA), a compound that activates epidermal growth factor receptors (EGFRs) and their cognate ligands. This suggests that EGFR signaling is involved in the propagation of ERK activity. Furthermore, the researchers observed that ERK activation propagated as a "trigger wave" in parallel to the wound edge in wounded skin, indicating that ERK activation can be superimposed.

    By visualizing the cell cycle, the researchers found that SPREADs were associated with G2/M cell cycle progression, providing new insights into how cell proliferation and transient ERK activity are synchronized in a living tissue. These findings contribute to our understanding of how growth signals are generated and propagated in the epidermis and have implications for our understanding of tissue homeostasis and wound healing. The development of the ERK FRET biosensor and the observation of SPREADs provide new tools and insights for studying the regulation of ERK activity in living tissues.
Scientists have studied the circadian clock in cyanobacteria, a type of bacteria that responds to daily light-dark cycles, to understand how it adapts to changing day lengths throughout the year. The circadian clock is a biological process that regulates daily changes in physiology, but its alignment with external cycles is not well understood. In this study, researchers developed experimental platforms to drive the cyanobacterial circadian clock both in living cells (in vivo) and in a laboratory setting (in vitro). They found that the phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in response to light-dark cycles, tracking midday across conditions with variable day lengths.

The core biochemical oscillator, composed of the Kai proteins, behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating that these dynamics are intrinsic to the proteins. This suggests that the ability to track midday is an inherent property of the core oscillator, rather than requiring additional factors present in the cell. The researchers also developed a general mathematical framework based on instantaneous transformation of the clock cycle by external cues, which successfully predicts clock behavior under many cycling environments.

This framework can be used to guide experiments that probe the molecular origins of key mathematical features of the circadian clock. The precisely defined nature of the cyanobacterial clock facilitates such experiments, and the model is expected to be useful for other organisms as well. By reconstituting the core oscillator in vitro from purified Kai proteins, the researchers simulated seasonal effects by driving the Kai proteins with pulses of nucleotides on a 24-hour cycle, simulating in vivo metabolic conditions during day and night. The in vitro system showed a similar linear scaling of clock phase with simulated day length, indicating that core mechanisms for seasonal adaptation are intrinsic to the Kai proteins.

The data reduction achieved in the linear model holds the promise of predicting the behavior of the circadian rhythms in many time-varying environments from a minimal data set that characterizes oscillator response. This may be applicable to clocks in many organisms. The analysis suggests that a key determinant of entrained behavior is how the clock limit cycle is deformed by coupling to the environment. Overall, this study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms of the circadian clock and its adaptation to changing day lengths throughout the year.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the causes of sudden cardiac death (SCD), a leading cause of premature death in the general population. Genome-wide association studies have identified the transcription factor Meis1 as a risk factor for SCD. A recent study investigated the role of Meis1 in the development and function of sympathetic neurons, which play a crucial role in regulating heart rate and rhythm.

The study found that inactivating Meis1 in the neural crest of mice led to altered sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac rhythmicity in adults, characterized by chronotropic incompetence and cardiac conduction defects. This resulted in an increased susceptibility to SCD. Further investigation revealed that Meis1 is a major regulator of sympathetic target-field innervation and that Meis1-deficient sympathetic neurons die by apoptosis from early embryonic stages to perinatal stages.

The study also showed that Meis1 regulates the transcription of key molecules necessary for the endosomal machinery, and that the traffic of Rab5+ endosomes is severely altered in Meis1-inactivated sympathetic neurons. This suggests that Meis1 interacts with various trophic factors signaling pathways during postmitotic neurons differentiation.

Meis1 was found to regulate the transcription of proteins necessary for retrograde transport, endosomes formation, functioning, and traffic. The study demonstrated that genetic inactivation of Meis1 in rodents results in an imbalanced sympatho-vagal regulation of heart functions, leading to conduction defects, chronotropic incompetence, and premature SCD. Meis1 was shown to act independently of the well-studied network of transcription factors governing early specification of sympathetic neurons to orchestrate later phases of neuronal differentiation.

The study aimed to investigate whether Meis1 participates in neural aspects of cardiac regulation by intervening in the differentiation of sympathetic neurons and how Meis1 mutations could mediate susceptibility to unexplained SCD. The results highlight that loss of Meis1 function in early sympathetic neurons leads to an imbalanced sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac functions in adult mice, resulting in increased susceptibility to SCD. The study also found that following specific inactivation of Meis1 in peripheral neurons, the traffic of Rab5+ early endosomes is impaired and sympathetic target-field innervation is compromised.

These findings complement a previous study demonstrating Meis1 function in the regulation of neonatal cardiomyocytes mitosis, especially following myocardial infarction. However, cardiac functions were not affected following specific Meis1 inactivation or overexpression in cardiomyocytes. The study suggests that Meis1 plays a critical role in the development and function of sympathetic neurons, and its loss may contribute to the increased susceptibility to SCD.
Summary
     Biologists have long been interested in understanding how surface-attached bacterial communities, known as biofilms, develop their diverse range of morphologies. While the structural and regulatory components necessary for biofilm formation are well understood, the mechanisms that promote biofilm surface morphology remain unclear. Using Vibrio cholerae as a model system, researchers have investigated the role of mechanical instabilities and interfacial energy in biofilm morphogenesis.

     The study reveals that mechanical instabilities, including wrinkling and delamination, play a crucial role in the development of biofilm surface morphology. These instabilities are driven by compressive stress that builds up as the biofilm grows. The researchers used a combination of mechanical measurements, theory, simulation, quantitative image analysis, surface energy characterization, and mutagenesis to demonstrate that mechanical instabilities underlie the morphogenesis program of growing biofilms.

     In addition to mechanical instabilities, the study shows that interfacial energy is a key driving force for mechanomorphogenesis. Interfacial energy dictates the creation or annihilation of new or existing interfaces, which in turn shapes the overall biofilm contour. The researchers also discovered feedback between mechanomorphogenesis and biofilm expansion, which further influences the biofilm's surface morphology.

     The findings of this study suggest that exploiting mechanical principles to drive key morphogenic events is an ancient process that has been reused in eukaryotes. Biofilms represent an intriguing model system for investigating the general role of mechanical forces in morphogenesis, and they provide a convenient system for morpho-engineering. The study's results have implications for the development of new strategies to address biofilm-related problems, such as the discovery of new genes and compounds that alter biofilm morphology by altering mechanics.

     The researchers propose a mechanomorphogenesis model, which suggests that biofilms first wrinkle and subsequently delaminate as growth builds up compressive stress. This model predicts that changing the spatial distribution of mechanical stress can alter the biofilm topography. The study also highlights the importance of considering the connections between a biofilm's surface morphology and its mechanical and material properties in the development of new strategies to address biofilm-related problems.
summary
     The study investigated the role of the transcription factor NRSF (neuron-restrictive silencer factor) in the development of epilepsy. The researchers found that NRSF levels increased in the hippocampus following seizures, but surprisingly, only a small subset of genes regulated by NRSF were repressed. This subset of genes included those involved in ion channels, receptors, and calcium-related molecules, which are crucial for neuronal function and plasticity. The repression of these genes by NRSF may contribute to the pathogenesis of epilepsy.

     The study used large-scale transcriptome arrays, qPCR validation, chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP), and gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) to examine the basis of NRSF-dependent gene expression changes during epileptogenesis. The results showed that a twofold to threefold increase in NRSF levels, induced by epilepsy-provoking seizures, led to the repression of only a subset of potentially regulated genes. These genes were rescued when NRSF function was blocked, indicating that NRSF is responsible for their repression.

     The researchers also found that the subset of genes repressed by NRSF had mid-range binding frequencies to the repressor, making them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NRSF levels. This selective regulation of NRSF target genes may play a role in influencing neuronal properties in both pathological and physiological contexts. The study suggests that NRSF contributes to epileptogenesis by regulating a group of genes that critically influence neuronal function.

     The key findings of the study are: (1) NRSF contributes to epileptogenesis by regulating a group of genes that critically influence neuronal function; (2) only a few dozen of the hundreds of hippocampal genes that are potential NRSF targets are actually repressed by the transcription factor in the context of seizure-induced epilepsy; and (3) the basis of the selective regulation of a subset of NRSF target genes is their mid-range binding frequencies to the repressor, making them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NRSF levels.
Summary
     The mammalian olfactory system processes sensory information through parallel pathways formed by mitral and tufted cells in the olfactory bulb. These parallel pathways are a common feature of many sensory systems, but how local circuit activity creates stimulus selectivity in parallel pathways remains poorly understood. Researchers investigated the parallel pathways formed by mitral and tufted cells in mice and characterized the emergence of feature selectivity in these cell types via distinct lateral inhibitory circuits.

     The study found differences in activity-dependent lateral inhibition between mitral and tufted cells, which likely reflect newly described differences in the activation of deep and superficial granule cells. Simulations showed that these circuit-level differences allow mitral and tufted cells to best discriminate odors in separate concentration ranges. This suggests that segregating information about different ranges of stimulus intensity may be an important function of these parallel sensory pathways.

     The olfactory system is similar to the visual system in that distinct outputs are generated from initially homogeneous sources. In the retina, feature selectivity in each ganglion cell type emerges due to its connectivity to specific bipolar and amacrine cell types. Similarly, differences in odor-evoked responses in mitral and tufted cells emerge due to differences in connectivity to olfactory sensory neurons and external tufted cells.

     The combination of activity-dependent lateral inhibition at both low and intermediate rates enables tufted and mitral cells to collectively encode odors better than either population alone. This supports novel computations that are unlikely to occur with a single neuron type. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms behind parallel processing in sensory systems and highlights the importance of local circuit activity in creating stimulus selectivity in parallel pathways.
Summary
     The M2 muscarinic receptor, a prototypic model of allostery in G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), has been studied to understand the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allosteric effects. Allosteric interactions were monitored using a FRET-based sensor of conformation at the allosteric site and pharmacological assays involving mutants engineered to preclude intramolecular effects. The results showed that allosteric effects in monomers were exclusively negative and derived primarily from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the allosteric and orthosteric ligands.

     In contrast, allosteric effects in oligomers could be positive or negative, depending on the allosteric-orthosteric pair, and arose from interactions within and between the constituent protomers. The complex behavior of oligomers is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations. The study aimed to understand how a multimeric complex might account for allosteric behavior that resists explanation in terms of monomers.

     The M2 muscarinic receptor has been a prototype for allosteric effects within the broader family of GPCRs. Most studies of the interaction of an allosteric ligand with the allosteric site have been based on the modulation of events at the orthosteric site, typically on receptors in native membranes or detergent-solubilized extracts. Allosteric effects were examined in molecular dynamics simulations at the atomic level, starting from the crystal structure of the monomeric M2 receptor occupied by the agonist iperoxo (4MQS). The ligand and accessory proteins were removed, and conformations of the receptor were sampled from a canonical ensemble accessible within a period of 30 ns.

     The study compared positive and negative allosteric modulators for their effects on oligomers and purified monomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor. The results showed that the oligomeric nature of the M2 receptor is evident in the multiphasic effects of allosteric ligands on the dissociation rate of orthosteric antagonists and their binding at equilibrium. The chemical structures of allosteric and orthosteric ligands to the M2 muscarinic receptor were also examined, including N-methylscopolamine, quinuclidinylbenzilate, strychnine, and gallamine. Different liganded states of the receptor were identified as X_R_Y, where X and Y are the allosteric and orthosteric ligands, respectively.
Summary
     The study investigated the role of SETD2, a histone-modifying enzyme, in the cellular response to DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs). SETD2 is responsible for trimethylating histone H3 lysine 36 (H3K36me3), a modification that plays a crucial role in establishing chromatin states that coordinate the DNA damage response. The researchers found that SETD2 is necessary for the activation of the ATM kinase, a key player in the DNA damage response, following DNA damage. Additionally, SETD2 is required for the homologous recombination repair of DSBs by promoting the formation of RAD51 presynaptic filaments.

The study used reporter assays to demonstrate that depletion of SETD2 impairs the DNA damage response (DDR) and leads to delayed recruitment of 53BP1 to sites of DSBs. Furthermore, SETD2-depleted cells showed reduced homology-directed repair of DSBs. The researchers also found that SETD2 mutations are present in several cancers, including clear cell renal cell carcinoma (ccRCC), which is characterized by a high rate of genomic alterations and inactivating mutations in histone modifiers, such as SETD2.

The study provides evidence that SETD2 plays a novel role in priming intact chromatin for the response to DNA damage. Unlike other histone modifications that promote the DDR, SETD2 does not increase H3K36me3 levels at DNA damage sites or recruit SETD2 to DSBs. Instead, SETD2 is required for the recruitment of RAD51 to resected DNA 5' ends. The researchers propose that SETD2 acts as a chromatin bookmarking instrument that facilitates signaling and repair of DSBs.

The study highlights the importance of SETD2 in maintaining genome integrity and provides a functional link between altered chromatin states and the mechanisms that maintain genome integrity. The findings suggest that loss of SETD2 may afford an alternative mechanism for the inactivation of the p53-mediated checkpoint in cancers, such as ccRCC, without the need for additional genetic mutations in TP53. Further studies are needed to fully elucidate the mechanistic basis of the SETD2 impact on the DDR.
summary
     Scientists have identified a new mutation in the Rasgrp1 gene, known as Rasgrp1Anaef, which leads to the production of anti-nuclear autoantibodies and the accumulation of a specific type of T cell in mice. This mutation affects the EF-hand domain of the Rasgrp1 protein, a guanine nucleotide exchange factor that plays a crucial role in cell signaling pathways.

     In mice with the Rasgrp1Anaef mutation, a distinct population of Helios+ PD-1+ T-helper cells gradually accumulates in the periphery, accompanied by the production of anti-nuclear autoantibodies. This is in contrast to previously described autoimmune mutations in Rasgrp1, Zap70, or Lat, which affect thymocyte development. The Rasgrp1Anaef mutation, however, has no detectable effect on thymocyte development in mice with normal T-cell receptor repertoires.

     Further investigation revealed that the Rasgrp1Anaef mutation leads to increased tonic mTOR signaling in naive CD4+ T cells. mTOR (mechanistic target of rapamycin) is a key regulator of cell growth and metabolism. The increased mTOR signaling in Rasgrp1Anaef mice results in the constitutive overexpression of CD44 on naive CD4+ T cells and the gradual accumulation of Helios+ CD44hi CD4+ T cells and autoantibodies.

     Genetic reduction of mTOR function in Rasgrp1Anaef mice normalizes CD44 expression on naive CD4+ T cells and abolishes the excessive accumulation of effector T cells and autoantibodies. This demonstrates a central role for increased mTOR activity in driving immune dysregulation in Rasgrp1Anaef mice. The findings of this study reveal a new role for Rasgrp1 in the cell-intrinsic regulation of peripheral CD4+ T cells, dissociating this function from its well-known role in thymic positive selection.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that mutations in the SWI/SNF chromatin remodeling family are common in cancer, but the mechanisms by which these mutations contribute to tumorigenesis are not well understood. A recent study investigated the role of Arid1a, a subunit of the SWI/SNF complex, in pancreatic ductal adenocarcinoma (PDAC) initiation. The study used a mouse model to suppress Arid1a in the pancreas, either in adult mice or during embryogenesis, in the presence of oncogenic Kras mutations.

     The results showed that suppressing Arid1a in adult mice with Kras mutations led to rapid and irreversible reprogramming of acinar cells into mucinous PDAC precursor lesions. In contrast, suppressing Arid1a during embryogenesis, concurrent with Kras activation, resulted in retention of acinar cell fate. These findings suggest that Arid1a is a critical modulator of Kras-dependent changes in acinar cell identity and that the timing and genetic context of Arid1a inactivation can influence the effects of SWI/SNF complex alterations in epithelial tumorigenesis.

     Further analysis revealed that Arid1a loss enables a non-reversible reprogramming of cell fate that is dependent on cellular and genetic context. The study also found that Arid1a disruption reduces the chromatin accessibility of key transcription factors to enhancers of genes needed to sustain acinar cell fate. This suggests that Arid1a stabilizes acinar cell identity and acts as a barrier to pancreatic cancer initiation. However, other factors are likely necessary to promote the transition from pancreatic intraepithelial neoplasia (PanIN) to PDAC.

     The study used a powerful mouse-modeling platform to demonstrate that Arid1a depletion can modulate the early stages of pancreatic neoplasia in a context-specific manner that depends on the timing of Arid1a inactivation and the presence of oncogenic Kras. The findings provide new insights into the role of Arid1a in pancreatic cancer initiation and highlight the importance of considering the timing and genetic context of SWI/SNF complex alterations in epithelial tumorigenesis.
Biomedical research has shown that social networks play a crucial role in buffering the effects of stress in many species. However, the absence of a social network can also be stressful. A recent study examined the effects of social isolation on neuroendocrine responses in mice, specifically focusing on the hypothalamic paraventricular nucleus (PVN) corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons.

The study found that isolating preadolescent female mice from their littermates for less than 24 hours altered the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in a sexually dimorphic fashion. Specifically, the first spike latency (FSL) and excitability of CRH neurons increased in isolated females, but not in age-matched males. In contrast, subjecting either males or group-housed females to acute physical stress, such as swimming, increased FSL in both groups.

The study also found that the increase in FSL following social isolation or acute physical stress was blocked by the glucocorticoid synthesis inhibitor, metyrapone, and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone. This suggests that the increase in FSL results in a decrease in the excitability of CRH neurons. Furthermore, the study demonstrated that social isolation, but not acute physical stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN CRH neurons.

The researchers prepared hypothalamic brain slices and examined synaptic and intrinsic properties of identified CRH neurons in the PVN. They found that social isolation altered the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in female, but not male, mice. The study also showed that female mice, but not male mice, may interpret social isolation as a stress. When single-housed male mice were subjected to an acute swim stress, FSL increased, but in females, the prior isolation occluded the effects of an acute stress.

These findings demonstrate that males and females react differently to some stressors, but not others. Specifically, social isolation has definitive, CORT-dependent effects on CRH neurons in female, but not male mice. By contrast, both males and females show indistinguishable responses to an acute physical swim stress. Additionally, a prior social isolation occludes the effects of subsequent physical stress on FSL in females, but has no effect on FSL in males. Overall, the study provides new insights into the sex-specific effects of social isolation on neuroendocrine responses in mice.
(summary)
     Biologists have long been interested in understanding how the protein hemoglobin (Hb) adapts to meet the unique needs of different mammalian tissues for oxygen delivery. Hemoglobin is a model protein for studying molecular adaptation in vertebrates, and its ability to bind oxygen is crucial for tissue oxygen delivery. While previous research has focused on the affinity of Hb for oxygen, a new study suggests that both affinity and cooperativity of oxygen binding play a role in molecular adaptations of Hb.

     The study used a mathematical model to analyze the relationship between the Hill coefficient, which measures cooperativity, and the relative affinity and conformational changes of Hb. The researchers found that mammalian Hb cooperativity values all reside on a "biophysical Hill landscape" that allows for both gross- and fine-tuning of tissue oxygen unloading to meet the distinct metabolic requirements of mammalian tissues for oxygen. This suggests that the maximum cooperativity point of Hb has been tuned during evolution to satisfy the particular physiological requirements of different tissues for oxygen supply.

     The study also found that Hb in small-sized animals with high metabolic rates has lower affinity for oxygen than Hb in large-sized animals, reflecting the adaptive adjustment of tissue oxygen unloading by Hb. Furthermore, the study found a positive correlation between an animal's metabolic rate and the observed maximum Hill cooperativity value of oxygen binding by Hb. In contrast, an inverse linear correlation was found between the metabolic rate and Hb affinity.

     These findings provide evolutionary and functional insights into the mechanism underlying body-size-related adaptation of mammalian Hb. The study suggests that changes in mammalian Hb binding and conformation have been tuned during evolution to give rise to close to maximum cooperativity in oxygen binding, yielding a different maximum Hill value in each case. The generality and implications of these findings are discussed, highlighting the importance of considering both affinity and cooperativity in understanding molecular adaptations of Hb. The study's results have significant implications for our understanding of how Hb adapts to meet the unique needs of different mammalian tissues for oxygen delivery.

     summary
Scientists have studied how cells adapt to the loss of a crucial component, BEM1, which is essential for cell polarization in yeast. Cell polarization is the process by which cells become organized into functional modules, and BEM1 plays a key role in this process. To understand how cells adapt to the loss of BEM1, researchers deleted the gene from yeast cells and allowed them to evolve over 1000 generations. The results showed that the cells were initially able to rapidly increase in fitness, but then slowed down and eventually reached a fitness level of over 90% of their BEM1-containing ancestors.

     Genome sequencing and monitoring of cell polarization revealed a common evolutionary trajectory, with a fixed sequence of adaptive mutations that improved cell polarization by inactivating proteins. This suggests that cells can evolve to perform the same biological functions with different proteins, and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions.

     The researchers found that the cells adapted to the loss of BEM1 by following a reproducible evolutionary trajectory, which could be largely explained by the interactions amongst the mutated genes. This adaptation led to a substantial recovery in the speed and accuracy of cell polarization. The study also found that bem1â cells often contained more than one polarity site, suggesting that the positive feedback that ensures a single site of polarization is weaker.

     The results of this study demonstrate that cells can be evolutionarily robust to physiologically destructive perturbations, and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions. This suggests that the multiple layers of regulation that allow cells to polarize rapidly and accurately also make it possible for them to adapt to very strong perturbations. Under strong selective pressure, the larger target size for mutations that inactivate proteins favors evolutionary trajectories that remove components from modules over those that quantitatively alter their properties.
Researchers investigated the neural mechanisms of economic commitment in the human brain using a decision-making task where participants chose whether to accept or reject prospects that provided sustained financial returns. The study aimed to understand how the brain computes value and makes economic commitments. Participants were presented with a series of choices between goods or gambles with lasting consequences, such as investing in a house or choosing a partner. The researchers found that the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) encoded stimulus value only when acceptance or rejection was deferred into the future, suggesting a role in integrating value signals over time. In contrast, the dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) encoded stimulus value only when participants rejected or deferred accepting a prospect. The dACC BOLD signals reflected two decision biases: deferring commitments to later and weighting potential losses more heavily than gains. These biases paradoxically maximized reward in the task. The findings provide new insights into the pressures that shape economic decisions and the computation of value in the medial prefrontal cortex. The study used a multi-alternative choice task where decisions had financial ramifications that persisted over prolonged episodes and could not be reversed. In half of the blocks, participants had to choose between accepting and deferring acceptance of a prospect. The results showed that the dACC encoded value when a prospect was excluded, while the rmPFC encoded value only during deferral. Joint consideration of the behavioral data and dACC activity allowed the researchers to pinpoint two pressures that shaped decisions in the task: a bias to defer until a later date and a bias to weight unfavorable options more heavily. These findings contribute to our understanding of the neural mechanisms underlying economic decision-making and the role of the medial prefrontal cortex in integrating value signals over time. The study's results have implications for understanding the pressures that shape economic decisions and the computation of value in the brain.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered that paraspeckles, a type of nuclear body found in cells, display a circadian expression pattern in pituitary cells. Paraspeckles are composed of long non-coding RNA, Neat1, and RNA-binding proteins, and are believed to control gene expression at a post-transcriptional level by retaining mRNA in the nucleus. The researchers found that the insertion of inverted repeats of Alu sequences (IRAlu) at the 3'-untranslated region (3'-UTR) of the EGFP gene led to a rhythmic circadian nuclear retention of the EGFP mRNA. This effect was lost when paraspeckles were disrupted. In contrast, the insertion of a single antisense Alu had only a weak effect. Using real-time video-microscopy, the researchers showed that IRAlu drove a circadian expression of EGFP protein.

     The study suggests that paraspeckles control circadian gene expression at a post-transcriptional level by rhythmically retaining RNAs in the nucleus. The researchers used a rat pituitary cell line, the GH4C1 cells, to study the role of paraspeckles in circadian gene expression. They found that paraspeckles were rhythmically expressed in these cells and that the insertion of IRAlu at the 3'-UTR of the EGFP gene led to a circadian expression of EGFP protein.

     The study builds on previous research that has shown that paraspeckles are involved in post-transcriptional regulation of gene expression and that they display a circadian expression pattern in pituitary cells. The researchers suggest that paraspeckle bodies can rhythmically retain RNAs in the nucleus, leading to a rhythmic expression of the corresponding gene. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of circadian gene expression and highlights the importance of post-transcriptional regulation in determining oscillations at the mRNA level.

     The researchers identified several RNA-binding proteins that target antisense Alu elements within the 3'-UTR of mRNAs, including HNRNPC, which displays a circadian expression pattern in several tissues, including the mouse pituitary. The study suggests that these RNA-binding proteins may play a role in the circadian regulation of gene expression. Overall, the study provides new evidence for the role of paraspeckles in controlling circadian gene expression at a post-transcriptional level.
Summary
     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channels (VGCCs) in converting electrical signals into chemical communication. However, the molecules within the active zone that regulate this function have remained poorly understood. A recent study has shed light on the role of Munc13, an active-zone protein essential for exocytosis, in controlling VGCC function.

     The study demonstrates that Munc13 interacts with VGCCs via a specific pair of basic residues in its C2B domain. This interaction dictates the behavior of VGCCs during various forms of activity, including high-frequency action potential bursts and single action potential stimuli. The researchers found that eliminating this interaction by removing Munc13 or replacing it with a Munc13 C2B mutant altered the synaptic VGCC's response to and recovery from high-frequency action potential bursts, as well as calcium influx from single action potential stimuli.

     The study's findings illustrate a novel form of synaptic modulation, where Munc13 controls both vesicle priming and the trigger for exocytosis. This suggests that Munc13 is poised to profoundly impact information transfer at nerve terminals by regulating VGCC function. The researchers pinpointed a critical interaction site to two basic residues within the C2B domain of Munc13, which is orthogonal to the potential membrane-interacting loops of this domain.

     The study's results have broad implications for understanding how synaptic transmission is controlled. The observation that Munc13 enhances Ca2+ influx and modulates kinetic properties of VGCCs across a range of stimulation conditions implies that this modulation is mediated by a direct VGCC-Munc13 interaction. The researchers tested the significance of this interaction by examining the impact of removing Munc13 on action potential-driven calcium influx in nerve terminals. They found that VGCC failed to reactivate in 1.2 mM extracellular Ca2+ in cells lacking Munc13, suggesting that the increase in exocytosis during 2 ms ISI paired pulses in Munc13-KR/AA is a direct effect of the lack of Munc13-VGCC interaction.

     Overall, the study provides compelling evidence that Munc13 interacts with VGCCs in a way that controls calcium channel use-dependence on millisecond to second time scales at nerve terminals. This novel form of synaptic modulation has significant implications for understanding how synaptic transmission is controlled, and highlights the critical role of Munc13 in regulating VGCC function.
summary
     The shape and function of plant cells are intricately linked, and the puzzle-shaped cells found in the epidermis of many plants are a striking example of this relationship. However, the functional benefit of these complex cell shapes has remained unclear. Researchers propose that these intricate forms provide an effective strategy to reduce mechanical stress in the cell wall of the epidermis. They suggest that when tissue-level growth is isotropic, lobes emerge at the cellular level to prevent the formation of large isodiametric cells that would bulge under the stress produced by turgor pressure.

     The researchers used data from various plant organs and species to support the relationship between lobes and growth isotropy. They also tested this relationship with mutants where growth direction was perturbed. Using simulation models, they showed that a mechanism actively regulating cellular stress plausibly reproduces the development of epidermal cell shape. The results suggest that mechanical stress is a key driver of cell-shape morphogenesis.

     The researchers propose that the puzzle-shaped cells seen in the epidermis of many plant species emerge from a mechanism that evolved to limit mechanical stress in tissues that grow isotropically, such as the epidermis of leaves and cotyledons. They used finite element method (FEM) analysis of 3D pressurized cells to show that cell shape influences the direction and magnitude of mechanical stress exerted on the cell wall. When an epidermal cell becomes large in two directions, stress is greatly increased.

     To test their hypothesis, the researchers analyzed the stress in a mechanical model of the cotyledon epidermis of Arabidopsis thaliana. They found that in stems, roots, and siliques, growth is strongly anisotropic, and cells can simply elongate. However, this is not possible for isodiametric organs such as broad leaves, cotyledons, and silicle fruit pods. The researchers propose that puzzle-shaped cells in the epidermis of more isodiametric plant organs provide a means to avoid large open areas in the cell and the high stresses that they induce.

     The researchers also propose a different function for the puzzle shape, that it is an adaptation to a developmental constraint related to the mechanical forces that act on turgid plant cells that reside in the epidermis. They suggest that mechanically, plant cells are like small balloons inflated with considerable turgor pressure, up to 10 bar in Arabidopsis leaf cells, reaching values up to 50 bar in specialized cells such as stomata.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have investigated the process of pre-spliceosome assembly in human cells to understand how introns and exons are properly connected during gene splicing. Gene splicing is a crucial step in gene expression, where introns (non-coding regions) are removed and exons (coding regions) are joined together to form a mature messenger RNA molecule. The process involves the assembly of a complex called the spliceosome, which is composed of multiple subcomplexes that interact with the pre-mRNA molecule.

     To study this process, the researchers used human nuclear extracts and fluorescently tagged spliceosomal subcomplexes to observe the dynamics of pre-spliceosome assembly on individual pre-mRNA molecules. They found that the U1 subcomplex, which binds to the 5' splice site of an intron, can interact with another U1 subcomplex bound to the 5' splice site of the next intron. This interaction significantly increases the rate and efficiency of U2 subcomplex recruitment to the branch site/3' splice site of the upstream intron.

     The researchers also discovered that the flanking 5' splice sites have a greater than additive effect on U2 recruitment, suggesting distinct mechanisms facilitating U2 recruitment. This synergy of 5' splice sites across introns and exons is likely important in promoting correct and efficient splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs. The study suggests an ordered human pre-spliceosome assembly pathway, where stable U2 association requires prior U1 binding, in contrast to the branched pathway observed in yeast.

     The researchers established methods for observing the dynamics of spliceosomal subcomplexes on and the splicing of individual pre-mRNA molecules in human cell extracts. They found that the dynamics of U1 binding to the 5' splice site are largely unaffected by the presence of U2 binding sites positioned either downstream or upstream of the 5' splice site. Pre-spliceosomes can form on multi-intron pre-mRNAs through at least two different pathways: cross-intron interactions leading directly to a catalytically active spliceosome, or cross-exon interactions where the exons flanking an intron are first defined, after which cross-intron interactions between adjacent cross-exon complexes lead to spliceosome assembly.

     The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms contributing to accurate and efficient gene splicing, highlighting the importance of collaboration between flanking 5' splice sites in promoting U2 recruitment during pre-spliceosome formation. The findings have implications for understanding the regulation of gene expression and the development of therapies for genetic disorders related to splicing defects.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a novel folding assistance system that operates on membrane proteins from the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER). This system relies on a specific type of protein modification called ubiquitination, where a small protein called ubiquitin is attached to a lysine residue on the target protein. In the case of the Wnt signaling coreceptor LRP6, ubiquitination on a specific lysine residue (Lys-1403) promotes its folding by retaining it in the ER while preventing degradation. A deubiquitinating enzyme called USP19 then removes the ubiquitin from LRP6, allowing it to exit the ER.

     This ubiquitination-deubiquitination cycle is conceptually similar to the glucosylation-deglucosylation process that occurs in the ER lumen during the calnexin/calreticulin folding cycle. However, to avoid infinite futile cycles, successfully folded LRP6 molecules undergo palmitoylation and are exported from the ER, while those that fail to fold are polyubiquitinated on other lysines and targeted for degradation.

     The researchers also found that this ubiquitin-dependent folding system controls the proteostasis of other membrane proteins, including the cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) and the anthrax toxin receptor 2 (also known as Capillary Morphogenesis gene 2, CMG2). These proteins are involved in severe human diseases, such as cystic fibrosis and Hyaline Fibromatosis Syndrome. The study suggests that this ubiquitin-dependent folding system may be a general mechanism for assisting the folding of membrane proteins in the ER.

     The researchers propose that the ubiquitination of LRP6 on Lys-1403 allows it to interact with an ubiquitin-binding protein that acts as a chaperone, providing the protein with time to fold. If folding is successful, LRP6 is transported out of the ER, a process that is favored by palmitoylation. If folding is impaired or delayed, polyubiquitination of other cytosolic lysine residues takes over and targets LRP6 to the ER-associated degradation (ERAD) pathway.
Biomedical research has shown that sex differences in cognitive processes can contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders. A key area of interest is value-based decision making, where sex differences have been proposed to influence decision-making processes. However, overall performance in decision-making tasks often shows minimal differences between sexes. To uncover latent variables that define different decision-making approaches, researchers used computational tools to analyze behavior in a classic value-based decision-making task, the restless two-armed bandit, in mice.

The study found that male and female mice had similar accuracy in the task, but they achieved this performance via different patterns of exploration. Male mice tended to make more exploratory choices overall, often getting "stuck" in exploration once they started. In contrast, female mice explored less but learned more quickly during exploration. These results suggest that sex exerts a stronger influence on decision-making during periods of learning and exploration than during stable choices.

The study used a combination of computational modeling approaches to characterize sex differences in a canonical explore/exploit task. While males and females had similar performance, they used different latent explore-exploit strategies to learn about the dynamic environment. The researchers found that female mice displayed more win-stay behaviors, indicating that they were more likely to repeat behaviors that produced reward on the previous trial.

The study's findings have implications for understanding sex differences in neuropsychiatric disorders. Exploration during decision-making is altered in individuals diagnosed with addictions, depression, and neurodevelopmental disabilities. The neural mechanisms of exploration are a highly translational avenue for conferring sex-modulated vulnerability to these diagnoses. The study's results suggest that sex differences in exploration and learning may contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to these disorders. Further research is needed to understand how sex, explore-exploit states, and reward outcomes interact to influence animal choices.

The study's use of computational tools allowed researchers to uncover latent variables that define different decision-making approaches, even in animals with similar correct performance. This approach has the potential to reveal new insights into the neural mechanisms underlying sex differences in decision-making and neuropsychiatric disorders. The study's findings highlight the importance of considering sex differences in research on decision-making and neuropsychiatric disorders.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the process of endoplasmic reticulum-associated protein degradation (ERAD), where membrane proteins are ubiquitinated, extracted from the membrane, and degraded by the proteasome. A crucial step in this process is the movement of hydrophobic transmembrane (TM) segments from the phospholipid bilayer into the cytosol, often accompanied by hydrophilic and folded ER luminal protein parts. However, the mechanism by which this occurs has remained unclear.

     Using a reconstituted system with purified proteins from Saccharomyces cerevisiae, scientists have identified the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 (Teb-4/MARCH6 in animals) as a retrotranslocase that facilitates membrane protein extraction. The study reveals that a substrate's TM segment interacts with the membrane-embedded domain of Doa10 and then passively moves into the aqueous phase. In contrast, luminal substrate segments cross the membrane in an unfolded state, with their unfolding occurring on the luminal side of the membrane by cytoplasmic Cdc48 action.

     This research demonstrates how a membrane-bound retrotranslocase, Doa10, cooperates with the Cdc48 ATPase in the extraction of proteins with folded luminal domains. The findings provide new insights into the ERAD process and shed light on the long-standing question of how luminal domains are moved across the membrane during protein extraction. The study's results have implications for our understanding of protein quality control pathways in various organelles, including the ER, Golgi, mitochondria, and chloroplasts, where membrane proteins are removed from the organelle for proteasomal degradation in the cytosol.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that Hedgehog (Hh) proteins play a crucial role in guiding development and maintaining adult tissue homeostasis in both invertebrates and vertebrates. Aberrant Hh protein production, distribution, and responses are common causes of developmental birth defects and cancer. In Drosophila, Hh signaling proteins induce transcriptional changes in target cells by inhibiting the proteolytic processing of full-length Drosophila Ci or mammalian Gli proteins to nuclear transcriptional repressors and by activating the full-length Ci or Gli proteins.

     To investigate the contributions of these mechanisms to dose-dependent Hh signaling, researchers used Ci variants expressed at physiological levels in Drosophila wing imaginal discs. The results showed that Ci variants that cannot be processed supported a normal pattern of graded target gene activation and the development of adults with normal wing morphology, when supplemented by constitutive Ci repressor. This suggests that Hh can signal normally in the absence of regulated processing.

     Furthermore, processing-resistant Ci variants were also significantly activated in the absence of Hh by elimination of Cos2, likely acting through binding the CORD domain of Ci, or PKA, revealing separate inhibitory roles of these two components in addition to their well-established roles in promoting Ci processing. The study also found that Ci can mediate normal Hh morphogen action in wing discs in the complete absence of regulated processing, provided a constitutive source of Ci repressor was present to suppress ectopic dpp expression in anterior cells.

     The results of this study demonstrate that wing patterning by Hh does not require regulation of the level of either repressor or full-length Ci protein by processing. Animals lacking both Fu kinase and Su(fu) also develop normal wings, although late third instar wing discs do lack anterior En induction. These findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of Hh signaling and suggest that Hh can act as a morphogen in the absence of regulated Ci processing.
