summary
     Biomedical research has identified a crucial role for the RAB23 gene in regulating the development of bones in the skull. Mutations in RAB23 have been linked to Carpenter Syndrome, a condition characterized by abnormalities in bone development, including extra fingers and toes, and premature fusion of skull bones. To understand how RAB23 regulates bone development, researchers generated mice lacking the RAB23 gene and studied their skeletal development.

     The results showed that RAB23-deficient mice exhibited premature fusion of skull bones, similar to humans with Carpenter Syndrome. Further investigation revealed that RAB23 regulates the activity of two key signaling pathways: FGF10-pERK1/2 and Hh-GLI1. The FGF10-pERK1/2 pathway is involved in the proliferation and differentiation of bone cells, while the Hh-GLI1 pathway regulates the development of the mesenchymal stem cell niche.

     The researchers found that RAB23 acts as a negative regulator of these pathways, meaning that it suppresses their activity. In the absence of RAB23, the FGF10-pERK1/2 pathway is overactive, leading to excessive bone cell proliferation and premature fusion of skull bones. Similarly, the Hh-GLI1 pathway is also overactive, leading to an imbalance in the development of the mesenchymal stem cell niche.

     Inhibition of the FGF10-pERK1/2 pathway in RAB23-deficient mice normalized bone cell proliferation and prevented premature fusion of skull bones. Additionally, inhibition of the Hh-GLI1 pathway corrected the imbalance in the mesenchymal stem cell niche. These findings suggest that RAB23 plays a critical role in regulating the development of bones in the skull by coordinating the activity of the FGF10-pERK1/2 and Hh-GLI1 pathways.

     The researchers also found that RAB23 regulates the expression of the GLI1 gene, which is involved in the development of the mesenchymal stem cell niche. RAB23 acts as a negative regulator of GLI1, meaning that it suppresses its expression. In the absence of RAB23, GLI1 is overexpressed, leading to an imbalance in the development of the mesenchymal stem cell niche.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of RAB23 in regulating bone development and highlights the importance of this gene in preventing premature fusion of skull bones. The findings also suggest that RAB23 may be a potential therapeutic target for treating conditions characterized by abnormal bone development.
Scientists have long sought to understand how cells "remember" past events, such as environmental or developmental cues, and maintain gene expression states over time. A key player in this process is the Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2), which maintains epigenetic repression of many genes in animals and plants. PRC2 modifies chromatin at its targets, and these modifications can be inherited through cell division. However, it remains unclear whether this inheritance directs long-term memory of individual gene expression states (cis memory) or if local chromatin states are dictated by diffusible factors (trans memory).

To address this question, researchers studied the Polycomb target gene FLOWERING LOCUS C (FLC) in Arabidopsis plants. They found that one copy of the FLC gene can be repressed while the other is active, and this mixed expression state is inherited through many cell divisions as the plants develop. This suggests that epigenetic memory of FLC expression is stored locally in the chromatin environment of the gene itself, rather than in the concentrations of diffusible factors.

The researchers exploited the classic epigenetic process of vernalization in Arabidopsis, which is the acceleration of flowering following prolonged cold exposure. Vernalization is mediated by cold-induced epigenetic repression of the Polycomb target gene and floral repressor FLC. The study used an assay to visualize mitotic heritability of FLC expression states at the single-cell level and found that FLC repression is independent of DNA methylation, making it a useful system for studying the cis-memory storage capability of Polycomb at a single gene.

In vernalized plants, FLC expression is bistable, with the number of cells in which FLC is stably repressed increasing quantitatively with the duration of prior cold exposure. The results demonstrate that epigenetic memory of FLC expression is stored not in trans, but in cis, meaning that the local chromatin environment of the gene itself instructs its own epigenetic inheritance. This finding has implications for our understanding of how cells maintain gene expression states over time and how epigenetic memory is stored and inherited.
BiP, an endoplasmic reticulum (ER) Hsp70 chaperone, forms oligomers that have a poorly understood structure and functional significance. Using site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking, researchers have probed the architecture of BiP oligomers and found that they consist of adjacent protomers engaging the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate binding site of another. This interaction attenuates the chaperone function of oligomeric BiP.

Native gel electrophoresis revealed a rapidly modulated reciprocal relationship between the burden of unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers, as well as slower equilibration between oligomers and inactive, covalently modified BiP. Lumenal ER calcium depletion caused rapid oligomerization of mammalian BiP and a coincidental diminution in substrate binding, indicating that the oligomers are relatively inert. This suggests that equilibration between inactive oligomers and active monomeric BiP is poised to buffer fluctuations in ER unfolded protein load on a rapid timescale.

BiP oligomerization is rapidly tuned to the physiological state of the cell, and given that oligomerization inactivates participating BiP protomers, the oligomeric form is a relatively inactive storage form of the chaperone that is accessible on a short timescale. The findings are consistent with a model where competition between oligomerization and substrate binding buffers the ER against rapid fluctuations in unfolded protein load in both directions.

The researchers propose that the affinity of Hsp70 proteins for their substrates is governed by J-protein driven non-equilibrium mechanisms, which could account for BiP favoring itself as a client (oligomerizing) over other clients as ER calcium levels decline. This would shift the equilibrium between unfolded proteins and active chaperones in favor of the former. The inverse correlation between unfolded protein burden and BiP oligomerization appears to break down in the context of ER calcium depletion, which is both a strong inducer of the unfolded protein response (UPR) and promotes rapid oligomerization of BiP.

The study suggests that enhanced production of unfolded proteins rapidly draws on the pool of inactive, oligomeric BiP to service its growing clientele. Reports of enhanced presence of oligomers in longer-term stressed cells may have reflected the partitioning of excess chaperone produced during the activated UPR, but made redundant following resolution of the insult. The relationship between unfolded protein load and the oligomeric state of Hsp70 chaperones is confounded by powerful feedback that dominates the unfolded protein responses and couples chaperone production to the levels of unfolded protein stress.
(summary)
     Scientists have determined the near-atomic structure of a flexible filamentous plant virus called Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV) using a technique called electron cryomicroscopy. PepMV is a representative of the genus Potexvirus, which includes economically important plant pathogens. The structure of PepMV reveals that its coat protein (CP) is made up of several hundred copies arranged in a helical fashion, protecting a single-stranded RNA (ssRNA) genome.

     The researchers found that the CP of PepMV has a similar structure to the nucleoproteins (NPs) of the genus Phlebovirus, a group of enveloped viruses with a segmented ssRNA genome. This similarity suggests that there may have been gene transfer between eukaryotic viruses with positive-sense ssRNA and those with negative-sense ssRNA. The main difference between the CP of PepMV and the NP of Phlebovirus is in their C-terminal extensions, which determine the characteristics of their multimeric assemblies.

     The C-terminal extensions of the CP of PepMV form a flexuous, helical rod, while those of the NP of Phlebovirus form a loose ribonucleoprotein (RNP). The RNPs of Phleboviruses are associated with the viral genome and are protected by an envelope with inserted glycoproteins that form an icosahedral shell.

     Flexible filamentous viruses, including PepMV, are non-enveloped and rod-shaped, containing hundreds of copies of the CP arranged in a helical fashion to protect the ssRNA genome. The high-resolution structures of these viruses have only been determined for representatives of the genus Potexvirus, including PepMV, PapMV, and BaMV. It is unclear whether other families of flexuous filamentous viruses, such as Betaflexiviridae, Closteroviridae, and Potyviridae, have a similar fold in their CPs.

     The discovery of structural homology between the CP of PepMV and the NP of Phlebovirus has significant implications for our understanding of the evolution and diversity of viruses. It suggests that there may have been gene transfer between eukaryotic viruses with positive-sense ssRNA and those with negative-sense ssRNA, and highlights the importance of further research into the relationships between different virus families.
Scientists have developed a new method, called SiteLoc, to precisely locate the binding sites of antibodies on the surface of cells. This is important because the location of an antibody's binding site can determine the outcome of an immune response. Current techniques for identifying binding sites are time-consuming, expensive, and not well-suited for high-throughput analysis.

The new method uses topological image averaging to achieve nanometer-scale resolution, which is the ability to measure distances as small as one billionth of a meter. This is done by calculating the average distance between a bound ligand (such as an antibody) and a reference surface on the cell. The method can be applied to both spherical and non-spherical cell surfaces, which is a significant improvement over existing techniques that are only suitable for identical or spherical particles.

SiteLoc uses high-resolution images to determine the location of binding sites with nanometer-scale precision. The method involves reducing the dimensionality of the surface data within an image plane, allowing for accurate measurements of molecular heights on the cell surface. The researchers demonstrated the effectiveness of SiteLoc by using it to locate the binding site of an antibody on the surface of bacteria.

The results show that SiteLoc can accurately estimate molecular heights on both spherical and non-spherical cellular surfaces. The method was also able to accurately locate the binding site of a non-specific monoclonal IgG antibody on the surface of bacteria. The binding site of this antibody was previously known to be located at the S region of the M protein, and SiteLoc was able to confirm this location.

Overall, the SiteLoc method provides a fast and efficient way to screen epitope locations on the surface of cells, which can be useful for the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines. The method has the potential to improve our understanding of immune responses and facilitate the development of new treatments.
Summary
     The regulation of systemic vascular pressure in vertebrates is a complex process involving multiple factors, including peripheral resistance in tissue capillary beds. One key aspect of this regulation is the relationship between central control of vascular flow and peripheral resistance, particularly in response to hypoxia. Hypoxia, or low oxygen levels, triggers a response in individual tissues, which in turn affects systemic cardiovascular adaptation to oxygen deprivation. This study investigates the role of peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin, a large vascular bed, in modulating cardiovascular response to hypoxia in mice.

     The researchers found that the skin's response to hypoxia, mediated by the HIF (Hypoxia-Inducible Factor) transcription factors, plays a crucial role in influencing cardiovascular parameters, including heart rate, arterial pressures, and body temperature. The study demonstrates that peripheral tissues, such as the skin, can play a fundamental role in cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia. To investigate this further, the researchers analyzed the effect of losing hypoxia-responsive genes in the skin on the cardiovascular response to hypoxia.

     The results show that mice lacking HIF-1Î±, HIF-2Î±, NOS2, and Arg-1 expression in the epidermis exhibit significant changes in systemic blood pressures and cutaneous temperatures compared to littermate control animals. The study also reveals that keratinocyte HIF-1Î± and HIF-2Î± expression affects the immediate, acute, and chronic phases of the cardiovascular response to hypoxia. The researchers propose a model for hypoxic responses involving HIF isoform activity and nitric oxide homeostasis in the skin, which suggests that hypoxia leads to an initial increase in vascular diameter and perfusion, followed by a reduction of intracellular L-arginine.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the dynamic role of oxygen sensing in peripheral tissues, such as the skin, in modulating cardiovascular response to hypoxia. The results demonstrate that peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin play a critical role in systemic cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia, and highlight the importance of considering peripheral tissues in the regulation of cardiovascular function.
Scientists have developed a new method for mapping the connections between individual neurons in the brain with high precision. This method uses a technique called optogenetics, which involves using light to activate specific neurons and observe their responses. The researchers used a protein called channelrhodopsin (ChR2) that is sensitive to light and can generate electrical signals in neurons when activated. By combining ChR2 with a technique called temporal focusing, which expands the light beam in the imaging plane, the researchers were able to selectively activate individual neurons and observe their responses with high accuracy.

The new method allows researchers to determine the presence or absence of synaptic connections between individual neurons with single-neuron resolution. This is a significant improvement over previous methods, which often left an indeterminate region of the circuit map coinciding with the dendritic arbors of the recorded neuron. The new method also reduces the risk of unintended activation of fibers of passage or local axonal boutons, which can respond to temporal focusing of two-photon excitation.

To achieve this level of precision, the researchers restricted ChR2 expression to the soma and proximal dendrites of neurons, rather than allowing it to be expressed throughout the dendritic and axon terminal fields. This allowed them to selectively activate individual neurons and observe their responses without generating confounding light-induced electrical responses.

The synaptic organization of individual neurons into circuits is the physiological basis for the interpretation of sensory input and production of behavioral responses. Understanding the precise patterns of connectivity among the distinct types of neurons that comprise neural circuits is critical for elucidating circuit function. The new method developed by the researchers provides a powerful tool for mapping functional connectivity with single-cell resolution, which is essential for advancing our understanding of neural circuits and their role in behavior. This method has the potential to revolutionize the field of neuroscience and provide new insights into the workings of the brain.
Summary
     The interaction of transcription factors (TFs) on eukaryotic genomes is a complex process that has been the subject of ongoing debate. Recent single-molecule data on the TFs Sox2 and Oct4 suggested an ordered assembly model, where Sox2 assists Oct4 in binding to DNA. However, a rigorous biophysical analysis of the data reveals that the interaction between Sox2 and Oct4 is actually characterized by negative reciprocity. This means that the expression of Sox2 increases Oct4's genomic binding, while the expression of Oct4 decreases Sox2's binding.

     The negative reciprocity between Sox2 and Oct4 can arise from two possible mechanisms: energy expenditure away from thermodynamic equilibrium or a mixture of positive and negative cooperativity at distinct genomic loci. The genomic-diversity model, which assumes that Sox2 and Oct4 sometimes help and sometimes hinder each other across the genome, can account for the single-molecule data. This finding is unexpected and suggests that TFs like Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit different cooperativities at different loci.

     The implications of this finding are significant, as it highlights the complexity of TF interactions on DNA. Single-molecule methods, such as those used by Chen et al., offer novel detection capabilities for unraveling these mechanisms. The variation in sign of cooperativity across the genome has important ramifications, suggesting that single-molecule data can provide more information than previously expected. In fact, a single number, Î=-0.22, can indicate that Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit both positive and negative cooperativity across genomic loci in vivo.

     The genomic-diversity model suggests that either energy expenditure maintains loci at which Sox2 and Oct4 bind away from thermodynamic equilibrium or there is mixed cooperativity across the genome, with Sox2 and Oct4 helping each other through positive cooperativity at some loci and hindering each other through negative cooperativity at other loci. Further studies are needed to address the implications of this finding and to understand the mechanisms underlying the interaction of Sox2 and Oct4 on DNA.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that detecting pathogens and mounting an immune response is crucial for animal health, but these responses come at a high metabolic cost. To avoid pathogens before infection may be advantageous. Bacterial endotoxins, such as lipopolysaccharides (LPS), are important immune system infection cues, but it remains unknown whether animals possess sensory mechanisms to detect them prior to infection. This study investigated whether Drosophila melanogaster (fruit flies) possess a sensory mechanism to detect LPS.

     The study found that Drosophila melanogaster display strong aversive responses to LPS and that gustatory neurons expressing Gr66a bitter receptors mediate avoidance of LPS in feeding and egg-laying assays. The expression of the chemosensory cation channel dTRPA1 in these cells was found to be necessary and sufficient for LPS avoidance. Furthermore, LPS stimulates Drosophila neurons in a TRPA1-dependent manner and activates exogenous dTRPA1 channels in human cells. These findings demonstrate that flies detect bacterial endotoxins via a gustatory pathway through TRPA1 activation as a conserved molecular mechanism.

     The study showed that LPS is avoided during feeding and oviposition, and that dTrpA1 expression in bitter-sensing gustatory neurons is necessary and sufficient for LPS avoidance. These data indicate that a TRPA1-dependent mechanism of avoidance of LPS may serve flies to prevent infection with Gram-negative bacteria. The study also found that control animals, but not dTrpA1-/- mutants, preferred laying eggs on control food, rather than on food contaminated with LPS.

     The study suggests that TRPA1 mediates gustatory avoidance of LPS in flies. Blocking neurotransmission in these neurons by expressing the light chain of tetanus toxin (TNT) abolished avoidance of LPS, indicating that flies can detect LPS through a gustatory mechanism. The findings demonstrate that fruit flies possess a gustatory mechanism underlying the detection and avoidance of LPS. The avoidance of LPS-contaminated food during feeding and oviposition may serve to prevent Gram-negative bacterial infections, potentially compensating for the lack of adaptive immunity in these animals. The fact that this sensory mechanism exploits dTRPA1 suggests a broadly conserved principle whereby these channels play a crucial role in LPS detection by sensory neurons in flies and mammals, regardless of the particular sensory modality involved.

     The generation of naÃ¯ve T lymphocytes is crucial for immune function, but the mechanisms governing their maturation are not yet fully understood. Researchers have identified a mouse mutant, bloto, which harbors a hypomorphic mutation in the zinc finger protein Zfp335. Mice with this mutation exhibit a deficiency in naÃ¯ve T cells due to an intrinsic developmental defect that begins in the thymus and continues into the periphery, affecting T cells that have recently undergone thymic egress. The effects of Zfp335bloto are multigenic and cannot be attributed to altered thymic selection, proliferation, or Bcl2-dependent survival. Zfp335 binds to promoter regions via a consensus motif, and its target genes are enriched in categories related to protein metabolism, mitochondrial function, and transcriptional regulation. Restoring the expression of one target, Ankle2, partially rescues T cell maturation. These findings identify Zfp335 as a transcription factor and essential regulator of late-stage intrathymic and post-thymic T cell maturation.

     The study reveals a novel role for Zfp335 as an essential regulator of T cell maturation. By analyzing mice with a hypomorphic missense mutation in a C2H2 zinc finger of Zfp335, researchers show a selective defect in the accumulation of naÃ¯ve T cells resulting from a maturation block in SP thymocytes and recent thymic emigrants. Zfp335 regulates transcription by binding to promoters of target genes. A set of Zfp335 target genes in thymocytes is identified, and evidence is presented that decreased Zfp335 occupancy at a subset of these targets alters gene expression in mutant thymocytes. The findings provide evidence that Zfp335 functions as a transcription factor and key regulator of a transcriptional program required for T cell maturation.

     Ankle2 is identified as a functional target gene of Zfp335, and its dysregulation by Zfp335R1092W contributes to the maturation defect in blt/blt T cells. Quantitative RT-PCR analysis shows reduced Ankle2 transcript levels in thymocyte and naÃ¯ve T cell populations sorted from blt/blt mice. Western blot analysis confirms reduced Ankle2 protein levels in mutant thymocytes. The study demonstrates that the maturation defect in blt/blt mice is independent of thymic selection or effects on proliferation, but is associated with reduced viability. Restoring Ankle2 expression partially rescues T cell maturation, indicating its functional relevance to the T cell maturation defect in blt/blt mice.

     Biologists have long assumed that the amount of messenger RNA (mRNA) in a cell directly correlates with the amount of protein produced. However, research has shown that this is not always the case, and other factors can influence protein production. A new study has identified a previously unknown factor, called mRNA:ncRNA avoidance, which plays a significant role in controlling protein expression levels in bacteria and archaea.

     The study found that mRNA:ncRNA avoidance, which refers to the ability of mRNA to avoid interacting with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs), is a critical factor in determining protein abundance. The researchers generated synonymously variant green fluorescent protein (GFP) mRNAs with different potential for mRNA:ncRNA interactions and demonstrated that GFP levels correlate well with interaction avoidance. This suggests that taking stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions into account can enable precise modulation of protein abundance.

     The study analyzed data from 97% of species and found a reduced capacity for interaction between native RNAs relative to controls. By generating variant GFP mRNAs that differ in their potential to interact with core ncRNAs, the researchers demonstrated that GFP expression levels can be both predicted and controlled. The results indicate that there is strong selection for the avoidance of stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions across prokaryotes.

     The study also found that measuring only extrinsic avoidance, using shuffled mRNAs as negative controls, revealed that stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions are significantly underrepresented in most prokaryotic phyla. The results clearly establish a signature of selection that acts to minimize stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions. However, the complete avoidance of stochastic interactions is combinatorially unlikely, suggesting a tradeoff between avoidance and optimal expression.

     The study's findings suggest that crosstalk between mRNAs and ncRNAs can impact protein expression levels, and that taking crosstalk into account will enable the design of constructs where protein expression levels can be precisely controlled. The researchers predict that mRNAs with low avoidance values will produce fewer proteins for each mRNA transcript than those with high avoidance. A meta-analysis of proteomics and transcriptomics data supports this prediction, indicating that mRNA:ncRNA avoidance is at least as good an explanation of variation in protein abundance as codon usage and internal mRNA secondary structure.
summary
     Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a fatal neurodegenerative disease characterized by the loss of upper and lower motor neurons (MN) and neuromuscular junctions (NMJs). A key pathological event in ALS is the denervation of NMJs, which occurs prior to symptom onset and is observed in both patients and animal models of the disease. However, the underlying mechanisms of NMJ denervation in ALS remain unclear.

     Researchers used in vivo imaging to study the dynamics of NMJ denervation in a mouse model of ALS (SOD1G37R mice) over a three-month period. They found that single motor-units (MUs) are dismantled asynchronously, with the dismantling of axonal branches accompanied by contemporaneous new axonal sprouting and synapse formation onto nearby NMJs. This suggests that NMJ denervation in ALS is a complex and dynamic process of continuous denervation and new innervation, rather than a manifestation of sudden global motor neuron degeneration.

     The study revealed that denervation events tend to propagate from the first lost NMJ, consistent with a contribution of neuromuscular factors extrinsic to motor neurons. Distal branches were found to be more susceptible to denervation. These findings highlight a long temporal window between early branch-specific dismantling and global motor axon degeneration, which could be therapeutically exploited in ALS.

     The results also suggest that local branch-specific events may play a key role in NMJ denervation, as the rescue of neuronal loss in SOD1 mice does not necessarily improve NMJ denervation. Local alterations of synaptic function and molecular signaling occur early at the NMJ in ALS, and the study's findings support the "dying-back hypothesis," which proposes that local branch-specific degeneration contributes to NMJ denervation.

     The study's use of longitudinal repeated in vivo imaging of single motor neuron axonal arbors and their NMJs provides new insights into the time course and dynamics of structural changes during ALS progression. The findings have implications for the development of therapeutic strategies to target NMJ denervation in ALS.

summary
     ISG15 is a protein that plays a crucial role in the innate immune response against viral infections. Recent research has shown that ISG15 can also counteract bacterial infections, specifically Listeria monocytogenes. The study found that ISG15 expression is dramatically induced in non-phagocytic cells upon Listeria infection, and this induction is not dependent on type I interferon. Instead, the induction of ISG15 is mediated by the cytosolic surveillance pathway, which senses bacterial DNA and signals through STING, TBK1, IRF3, and IRF7.

     The researchers used stable isotope labeling in tissue culture (SILAC) to identify proteins that are modified by ISG15 during Listeria infection. They found that ISG15 modification of ER and Golgi proteins leads to increased secretion of cytokines that counteract infection. This suggests that ISG15 plays a key role in restricting Listeria infection by modulating the host cell's response to the pathogen.

     The study demonstrated that ISG15 expression restricts Listeria infection both in vitro and in vivo. The researchers also found that ISG15's role in host defense against Listeria requires ISGylation, a process by which ISG15 is covalently linked to target proteins. The study's findings provide new insights into the mechanisms by which ISG15 counteracts bacterial infections and highlight the importance of ISG15 as a key component of the innate immune response.

     The study's results show that ISG15 can be induced by Listeria in an interferon-independent manner in human non-phagocytic cells. The researchers used a viral protein, B18R, to block signaling from the interferon receptor and confirmed that ISG15 induction is not dependent on type I interferon. The study's findings suggest that ISG15 plays a previously uncharacterized role in restricting Listeria infection and reinforce the view that ISG15 is a key component of the innate immune response. Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which ISG15 counteracts bacterial infections and highlights the importance of ISG15 in host defense against pathogens.
Summary
     Scientists have developed a new software tool called DeepFRET to analyze data from single-molecule FÃ¶rster Resonance energy transfer (smFRET) experiments. smFRET is a technique used to study the structure and dynamics of biomolecules. However, analyzing the large amounts of data generated by smFRET experiments can be time-consuming and subjective.

     DeepFRET uses deep learning, a type of artificial intelligence, to automate the analysis of smFRET data. The software takes raw microscope images as input and outputs detailed information about the behavior of biomolecules. The only human intervention required is setting a quality threshold for the data. DeepFRET has been shown to accurately classify smFRET data with an accuracy of over 95%, outperforming human operators and other commonly used methods.

     The software is based on a pre-trained neural network that can recognize patterns in the data and learn useful features directly from the raw input. This allows DeepFRET to operate without extensive data preprocessing or empirically defined rules, making it a reproducible and less opinionated evaluation of single-molecule data. Additionally, DeepFRET is significantly faster than human annotation for large single-molecule datasets.

     The developers of DeepFRET have made the software available as a free-to-use standalone executable for both Windows and Mac users. This allows end-users with limited programming skills to easily operate the software. DeepFRET has the potential to contribute to benchmarking smFRET for dynamic structural biology and enable new fully automated analysis methodologies related to biomolecular recognition, protein folding, and dynamics.

     The software has been tested on real data and has demonstrated its capacity to objectively quantify biomolecular dynamics. The developers expect DeepFRET to be a paradigm shift in the field of single-molecule studies, enabling new fully automated analysis methodologies and increasing the breadth and impact of structural biology.
Summary
     The Polycomb repressive complex-1 (PRC1) plays a crucial role in epigenetic regulation of gene expression. SCML2, a mammalian homolog of Drosophila SCM, is a Polycomb-group protein that associates with PRC1. Research has shown that SCML2A, an isoform of SCML2 tightly associated with chromatin, contributes to PRC1 localization and directly enforces repression of certain Polycomb target genes.

     SCML2A binds to PRC1 via its SPM domain and interacts with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) through a novel RNA-binding region (RBR). The targeting of SCML2A to chromatin involves the coordinated action of the MBT domains, RNA binding, and interaction with PRC1 through the SPM domain. Deletion of the RBR reduces the occupancy of SCML2A at target genes, and overexpression of a mutant SCML2A lacking the RBR causes defects in PRC1 recruitment.

     The study also found that SCML2 appears to enforce repression directly, independent of PRC1 recruitment, at a subset of genes. The identification of SCML2 as a PcG protein that binds RNA and contributes to repression of Polycomb targets sheds new light on the interplay between ncRNAs and epigenetic regulation. The results suggest that RNA binding plays a role in SCML2 function, as it is required for its localization to chromatin.

     The study proposes a model in which the RBR contributes to the localization of SCML2A to chromatin, where it interacts with PRC1 and creates a chromatin environment that is refractory to transcription. The RBR is required to recruit or stabilize SCML2 on chromatin, and SCML2A is then retained at target genes through the interaction with PRC1. The study concludes that SCML2 is recruited to a subset of target genes through a process that requires its RNA-binding activity and that the presence of mutant SCML2A lacking the RBR causes a partial release of BMI1 and PRC1 from chromatin.
summary
     Biologists have long understood that the regulation of translating ribosomes is a crucial component of the gene expression control network. In the bacterium Escherichia coli, the C-terminal arrest sequence of the SecM protein plays a significant role in regulating the SecA-dependent secretion pathway by causing ribosome stalling. However, the underlying molecular mechanism of this stalling process has remained unclear at the atomic level.

     To address this knowledge gap, researchers used a single-particle cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) technique to analyze the structures of SecM-stalled ribosome nascent chain complexes (RNCs) at near-atomic resolution. Their analysis revealed two distinct mechanisms of stalling at different elongation steps of the translation cycle. The first mechanism involves the inactivation of the ribosomal peptidyl-transferase center (PTC), which prevents the formation of a peptide bond with the incoming prolyl-tRNA. The second mechanism involves the prolonged residence of the peptidyl-RNA at the hybrid A/P site, which inhibits the full-scale translocation of the tRNA.

     The researchers found that the interactions between SecM and the ribosome's exit tunnel induce conformational changes in the PTC, leading to translation arrest at distinct elongation steps. Specifically, the position of the tRNA-nascent peptide linkage of SecM-tRNA is similar to that seen in the structure of the ribosome with an active PTC. This suggests an alternative mechanism of SecM-induced stalling, where the PTC is locked in an inactive state, preventing the stable binding and accommodation of the incoming A-site tRNA.

     These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanism of SecM-dependent translational stalling and demonstrate how regulatory peptides can control the translation cycle through a continuous, dynamic reshaping of the ribosome's functional center. The results also shed light on the complex interactions between SecM and the ribosome, highlighting the importance of conformational changes in the PTC for translation regulation. Overall, this study contributes to our understanding of the intricate mechanisms underlying gene expression control in bacteria.
Using a new generation Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope, researchers have made the first measurements of foveal cone density as a function of axial length in living eyes. The study involved imaging cones at and near the fovea in 28 eyes of 16 subjects. Cone density and other metrics were calculated in both visual angle and linear retinal units. The results show that the foveal cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fovea, but not in proportion to eye length.

The study found that despite retinal stretching, myopes generally have a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes. This suggests that myopes may have better visual acuity due to a higher concentration of cones in the foveal region. The findings contradict reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes compared to emmetropes, which have been attributed to increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during myopic progression.

Previous studies have implicated retinal stretching as a possible cause of myopic visual deficits, but the structural changes at the foveal center during myopic progression were not well understood. The current study aimed to investigate how eye length affects cone density at and near the foveal center. The researchers used adaptive optics to image the foveal photoreceptor mosaic, which offers the highest contrast en face images of the foveal photoreceptor mosaic ever recorded in vivo.

The study found that if the enlargement of the retinal image exactly matched the stretching of the cone mosaic, eyes of different lengths would sample the visual field similarly. However, the results show that this is not the case, and myopes generally have a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes. This suggests that myopes may have better visual acuity due to a higher concentration of cones in the foveal region, contradicting previous reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes.

The study's findings have implications for our understanding of the relationship between eye length and visual acuity. The results suggest that myopes may have an advantage in terms of visual acuity due to a higher concentration of cones in the foveal region, despite retinal stretching. Further research is needed to fully understand the relationship between eye length and visual acuity, but the current study provides new insights into the structural changes at the foveal center during myopic progression.

Summary
     The slow delayed rectifier current (IKs) plays a crucial role in stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration in the heart. This current is mediated by the tetrameric voltage-gated ion channel KCNQ1 and its Î²-subunit KCNE1. However, the stoichiometry between Î± and Î²-subunits has been a subject of controversy, with some studies suggesting a strict 2:4 ratio and others proposing a variable ratio up to 4:4.

     To clarify this issue, researchers created fusion proteins linking KCNE1 to one, two, or four KCNQ1 subunits, mimicking 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries, respectively. Whole-cell and single-channel recordings showed that fusion proteins with two or four KCNQ1 subunits (EQQ and EQQQQ) exhibited increasingly hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter first latency of opening compared to the fusion protein with one KCNQ1 subunit (EQ). The addition of KCNE1 abolished these differences, indicating that KCNE1 is essential for the observed effects.

     Furthermore, using a UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid in KCNE1, the researchers found that the crosslinking rates of EQQQQ and EQQ were progressively slowed compared to KCNQ1, suggesting that there is no intrinsic mechanism limiting the association of up to four Î²-subunits within the IKs complex. This finding supports the idea that the stoichiometry between KCNQ1 and KCNE1 can be regulated based on availability, allowing for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression.

     The results of this study provide evidence for a 4:4 stoichiometry of KCNE1:KCNQ1 and highlight the flexibility in KCNQ1's Î²-subunit association, both in number and type, which can modulate repolarization in the heart. This flexibility allows for changes in expression to alter the distribution of channel composition, shifting the overall activation kinetics of the current. The study's findings have significant implications for our understanding of the IKs channel complex and its role in cardiac function and disease.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the underlying processes of tissue regeneration, particularly in response to injury. A recent study on zebrafish fin fold regeneration has shed light on the role of interleukin-1 beta (Il1b) in this process. The study found that Il1b, a pro-inflammatory cytokine, is expressed by epithelial cells in response to tissue injury and plays a crucial role in initiating the inflammatory response. However, prolonged expression of Il1b leads to apoptosis of regenerative cells, highlighting the double-edged sword nature of Il1b signaling in tissue regeneration.

     Further investigation revealed that macrophages, a type of immune cell, are responsible for attenuating Il1b expression and resolving inflammation during tissue regeneration. The study demonstrated that macrophages play a critical role in tuning the level of Il1b expression and inflammation, which is necessary for the progression of tissue regeneration. In fact, genetic ablation of macrophages in adult zebrafish was shown to affect fin ray patterning and fin growth, underscoring the importance of macrophages in tissue regeneration.

     The study also found that Il1b signaling is essential for the activation of regeneration-induced genes, which are necessary for tissue regeneration. This suggests that a proper level of Il1b expression and inflammatory response, tuned by macrophages, is necessary for the progression of tissue regeneration. In contrast, excessive Il1b signaling and inflammation can impair tissue regeneration, leading to apoptosis of regenerative cells.

     The findings of this study provide new insights into the complex interplay between Il1b signaling, inflammation, and tissue regeneration. The study highlights the critical role of macrophages in regulating the level of Il1b expression and inflammation, and underscores the importance of a balanced inflammatory response in tissue regeneration. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of tissue regeneration and may inform the development of new therapeutic strategies for tissue repair and regeneration.

     The study investigated the relationship between protein mobility and surface properties in the cytoplasm of three bacterial species: Escherichia coli, Lactococcus lactis, and Haloferax volcanii. The researchers used surface-modified fluorescent proteins (FPs) to determine their translational diffusion coefficients (D) in the cytoplasm of these bacteria. The results showed that the diffusion coefficient of proteins in E. coli depends on their net charge and the distribution of charge over the protein surface, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones.

In contrast, the slowdown in diffusion with increasing positive surface charge was less pronounced in L. lactis and Hfx. volcanii due to electrostatic screening. The study found that the decrease in diffusion coefficient is mainly caused by the binding of positive proteins to ribosomes, with dissociation constants (KD) on the order of micromolar. This suggests that non-selective interactions between proteins and ribosomes can have a significant impact on protein mobility.

The researchers also found that the diffusion coefficients of proteins in the cytoplasm of E. coli become even slower when cells are exposed to an osmotic upshift. In addition, positive proteins not only diffuse slowly but their binding to ribosomes might also inhibit protein synthesis by affecting the assembly or activity of ribosomes. The study suggests that ribosome surface properties may limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome.

To determine the consequences of these findings, the researchers analyzed the proteomes of E. coli, L. lactis, and Hfx. volcanii, as well as four endosymbiotic bacteria. They determined how a protein will diffuse in light of the composition of the proteome, taking into account both the net charges and abundances of all proteome components. The study also investigated how ribosomes affect the diffusion of proteome constituents, considering the net charge of the proteins.

The findings of this study have significant implications for our understanding of the functioning of prokaryotic cells, particularly in the context of endosymbiotic relationships. The study highlights the importance of considering the interactions between proteins and ribosomes in the cytoplasm, and how these interactions can limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome. The results also suggest that non-selective interactions between proteins and ribosomes can have a significant impact on protein mobility and protein synthesis.
Scientists have long been fascinated by the ability of certain animals, such as planarians, to regenerate lost body parts. However, the mechanisms that trigger this regenerative process are not yet fully understood. A recent study has shed light on this mystery by identifying a key protein called Smed-follistatin, which plays a crucial role in initiating regeneration in planarians.

The researchers found that Smed-follistatin is required for planarians to respond to injuries that result in the absence of tissue, but not for normal tissue turnover. They also discovered that two other proteins, Smed-activin-1 and Smed-activin-2, are necessary for Smed-follistatin to function properly. Furthermore, the study showed that Smed-follistatin is expressed at higher levels in response to injuries that cause tissue absence, suggesting that it is specifically activated in response to these types of wounds.

The researchers propose that Smed-follistatin inhibits the activity of Smed-activin proteins, which are part of the TGF-Î² signaling pathway. This inhibition is thought to trigger the regenerative response in planarians. The study also found that Smed-follistatin is wound-induced, meaning that it is produced in response to injury, and that its expression persists at high levels for a longer period after a major injury compared to a simple injury.

The discovery of Smed-follistatin and its role in regeneration has important implications for our understanding of regenerative mechanisms across the animal kingdom. Regeneration is a complex process that involves the coordinated action of multiple cellular and molecular pathways. The identification of Smed-follistatin as a key player in this process provides new insights into how animals distinguish between different types of injuries and regulate distinct wound response programs.

The study suggests that Smed-follistatin allows planarians to initiate a regenerative response specifically in response to injuries involving tissue absence. This is in contrast to injuries that do not result in tissue loss, which do not trigger a regenerative response. The researchers propose that wound-induced expression of Smed-follistatin is a critical mechanism for triggering regeneration in planarians.

Overall, the discovery of Smed-follistatin and its role in regeneration provides a new understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying this complex process. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the role of Smed-follistatin in regeneration and to explore its potential applications in regenerative medicine.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a unique mutation signature in tumors with a deficiency in mismatch repair (MMR), a process that corrects errors in DNA replication. Using whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing, they found that MMR-deficient tumors exhibit a distinct pattern of mutations, characterized by the accumulation of indel mutations in mono- and di-nucleotide repeats, known as microsatellite instability (MSI). This mutation signature is distinct from other tumor genomes but surprisingly similar to germ-line DNA, suggesting that a significant portion of human genetic variation arises from mutations that escape MMR.

     The researchers also identified a set of recurrent indels that can be used to detect MSI, which is a hallmark of MMR-deficient tumors. They developed a novel marker set that can detect MSI with greater specificity and selectivity than standard MSI tests. Furthermore, they found that recurrent indels are enriched for the "DNA double-strand break repair by homologous recombination" pathway, indicating that MMR-deficient tumors have reduced ability to repair DNA double-strand breaks.

     As a result, MMR-deficient tumors are sensitive to DNA double-strand break inducers, making them a potential target for therapy. The researchers demonstrated that MMR-deficient tumor cultures are sensitive to DNA double-strand break inducers in a dose-dependent manner. This finding has significant implications for the treatment of MMR-deficient tumors, which are often resistant to standard chemotherapy.

     The study used whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing to analyze 33 tumor-normal pairs, of which 25 were MMR-deficient. The results showed that MMR-deficient tumors contain a significantly higher number of somatic mutations, with an average of 2015 mutations per tumor, compared to 39 in MMR-proficient tumors. The validation rates for substitutions and indels were 87% and 86%, respectively. The study provides new insights into the molecular characteristics of MMR-deficient tumors and identifies potential therapeutic targets for these tumors.
Summary
     Scientists have developed an improved version of a tool called Magnets, which allows for precise control over biological processes in living cells using light. Magnets are small protein modules derived from the Vivid photoreceptor found in the fungus Neurospora crassa. They consist of two complementary components, nMag (negative Magnet) and pMag (positive Magnet), which require simultaneous photoactivation to interact and form a dimer.

     The original Magnets pair has limitations, including the need for concatemerization (a process where multiple copies of the same protein are linked together) and preincubation at a low temperature (28Â°C) to function efficiently. To overcome these limitations, researchers engineered an optimized Magnets pair, called eMags, which do not require concatemerization or low-temperature preincubation.

     The eMags were tested in various applications, including recruiting proteins to subcellular organelles, inducing organelle contacts, and reconstituting a protein complex involved in lipid transport between the endoplasmic reticulum and the Golgi apparatus. The results showed that eMags are a highly effective tool for optogenetically manipulating physiological processes in mammalian cells.

     The eMags have several advantages over the original Magnets pair, including greater thermal stability, higher dimerization efficiency, and faster association and dissociation kinetics. The researchers used a robust screen to identify the optimal placement of charge-complementing amino acids in the Vivid dimer interface to achieve these improvements. The eMags are well-suited for optogenetic modulation of small volumes and subcellular organelles due to their rapid association and dissociation kinetics and low background activation.

     The eMags have the potential to be used in a variety of applications, including the study of cellular processes and the development of new therapeutic strategies. They offer a powerful tool for precise control over biological processes in living cells, allowing researchers to manipulate specific proteins and organelles with high spatiotemporal resolution.
summary
     Biomedical research has shown that ribosome stalling during translation can lead to neurodegeneration, but the underlying signaling pathways triggered by stalled elongation complexes were unknown. To investigate these pathways, scientists analyzed the brains of C57BL/6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice, in which neuronal elongation complexes are stalled at AGA codons due to deficiencies in a tRNAArgUCU tRNA and GTPBP2, a mammalian ribosome rescue factor. The results revealed increased levels of phosphorylation of eIF2Î± (Ser51) prior to neurodegeneration in these mice. Transcriptome analysis demonstrated activation of ATF4, a key transcription factor in the integrated stress response (ISR) pathway. Genetic experiments showed that this pathway was activated by the eIF2Î± kinase, GCN2, in an apparent deacylated tRNA-independent fashion. Furthermore, the ISR was found to attenuate neurodegeneration in C57BL/6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice, underscoring the importance of cellular and stress context on the outcome of activation of this pathway. These results demonstrate the critical interplay between translation elongation and initiation in regulating neuron survival during cellular stress.

     The study used unique mouse models with the Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mutation to assess the signaling events triggered by ribosome stalling. The results showed that GCN2 is activated in the B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mutant brain in a tRNA-deacyl-independent manner prior to the onset of neurodegeneration. Activation of this kinase results in eIF2Î± phosphorylation and induction of ATF4-target genes in the cerebellum and hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice. Genetic studies demonstrated that GCN2 activation functions to attenuate neurodegeneration in the B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- brain. These results suggest that ribosome stalling activates GCN2 as a previously unknown regulatory mechanism that links the elongation and initiation steps of translation.

     Failure to resolve stalled elongation complexes in mammalian neurons results in degeneration of these cells, but the signaling pathways evoked by these complexes are unknown. The study shows that GCN2 activates the integrated stress response in the brain of mice with stalled ribosomes, demonstrating an important feedback loop between elongation defects, in particular ribosome stalling, and translation initiation. In the hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/-; Gcn2-/- mice, degeneration of CA1 pyramidal cells was observed, suggesting the eIF2Î±/ATF4 pathway is vital for survival of these neurons during ribosome stalling. ATF4 activation also occurs in the brains of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice in which the n-Tr20 is entirely deleted and thus no unprocessed tRNA is produced. These results raise the possibility that ribosome stalling may also contribute to GCN2 activation in other conditions, such as amino acid deprivation, in which like B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- neurons, lower levels of acylated tRNA are present. The exact mechanism for GCN2 activation by ribosome stalling remains unclear.
Over the past century, adult human height has undergone significant changes across the world's countries, with varying degrees of increase and stability. A comprehensive analysis of 1472 population-based studies, involving over 18.6 million participants, was conducted to estimate mean height for individuals born between 1896 and 1996 in 200 countries. The study found that being taller is associated with enhanced longevity, higher education, and earnings.

The largest gains in adult height over the past century were observed in South Korean women and Iranian men, who became 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm taller, respectively. In contrast, there was little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan African countries and in South Asia over the century of analysis. The tallest individuals over the past 100 years were men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century, with an average height of 182.5 cm, while the shortest were women born in Guatemala in 1896, with an average height of 140.3 cm.

The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations was approximately 19 cm for men and 20 cm for women a century ago, and has remained relatively stable for women but increased for men over the past century. The study also found that the ranking of countries in terms of adult height has undergone significant changes, with some countries experiencing substantial increases in height while others have shown little change.

Notably, the largest gains in height since the 1896 birth cohort were observed in South Korean women and Iranian men, who surpassed the heights of women in Norway, Iceland, the USA, and American Samoa. In contrast, the shortest women were found in El Salvador, Peru, Bangladesh, South Korea, and Japan. The tallest populations a century ago lived in Central and Northern Europe, North America, and some Pacific islands, while the shortest populations were found in sub-Saharan Africa and South Asia.

The study's findings highlight the significant variations in adult height across the world's countries over the past century, with no indication of convergence across countries. The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations has remained relatively stable for women but increased for men, despite substantial changes in the ranking of countries in terms of adult height.

Summary
     Hepatitis B virus (HBV) and its satellite virus, hepatitis D virus (HDV), remain significant public health concerns due to their severe impact on individuals coinfected with both viruses. The entry of both viruses into host cells is mediated by HBV envelope proteins, specifically the pre-S1 domain of the large envelope protein. However, the identity of the receptor(s) responsible for binding to this domain has remained unknown.

     Researchers used near-zero distance photo-cross-linking and tandem affinity purification to identify the receptor-binding region of pre-S1 and found that it specifically interacts with sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP), a multiple transmembrane transporter predominantly expressed in the liver. Silencing NTCP inhibited HBV and HDV infection, while exogenous NTCP expression rendered nonsusceptible hepatocarcinoma cells susceptible to these viral infections.

     Further analysis revealed that replacing amino acids 157-165 of nonfunctional monkey NTCP with the human counterpart restored its ability to support both viral infections. This suggests that residues between 157 and 165 of NTCP are crucial for binding to the receptor-binding region of the pre-S1 domain of the L protein of HBV and critically contribute to NTCP-mediated HBV and HDV infections.

     These findings demonstrate that NTCP is a functional receptor for both HBV and HDV. The pre-S1 domain of the L protein is a key determinant for entry of both viruses and is believed to mediate viral interaction with the cellular receptor(s) on hepatocytes. The discovery of NTCP as a receptor for HBV and HDV provides new insights into the mechanisms of viral entry and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for the treatment of HBV and HDV infections.
summary
     Sepsis, a life-threatening condition caused by an overwhelming immune response to an infection, leads to lasting changes in the phenotype and function of memory CD8 T cells, a type of immune cell that plays a crucial role in fighting off infections. Memory CD8 T cells provide long-term immunity by rapidly recognizing and killing infected cells and producing cytokines to coordinate the immune response. However, sepsis can impair the function of these cells, making individuals more susceptible to re-infection.

     Researchers investigated the effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells in patients and mice. They found that central memory CD8 T cells, a subset of memory CD8 T cells, proliferate more in septic patients and mice compared to healthy individuals. This increased proliferation leads to changes in the composition of the memory CD8 T cell compartment and altered tissue localization. The researchers also observed that memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors have an altered transcriptional profile and chromatin accessibility, indicating long-lasting changes in the cells.

     The sepsis-induced changes in the memory CD8 T cell pool and transcriptional landscape result in altered T cell function and reduced capacity to control infections. Specifically, the researchers found that memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors have a reduced ability to undergo T cell receptor-dependent adhesion and immunologic synapse formation, which is essential for effective immune responses. These findings suggest that sepsis leads to long-term alterations in memory CD8 T cell phenotype, protective function, and localization, potentially changing the host's capacity to respond to re-infection.

     The study demonstrates that pre-existing memory CD8 T cells numerically recover with time after sepsis, potentially due to increased proliferation in response to the sepsis-induced lymphopenic environment. However, this recovery is accompanied by changes in the composition and localization of pre-existing memory CD8 T cells, leading to an over-representation of central memory CD8 T cells. These findings have important implications for our understanding of the long-term consequences of sepsis on the immune system and highlight the need for further research into the mechanisms underlying sepsis-induced changes in memory CD8 T cells.
summary
     The APOBEC3A (A3A) enzyme is a potent inhibitor of Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1 or L1) retrotransposition in human cells. L1 retrotransposition poses a mutagenic threat to human genomes, and A3A has been shown to inhibit this process in cultured cell assays. However, the molecular mechanism by which A3A inhibits L1 retrotransposition has remained unclear. A3A catalyzes the deamination of cytidine nucleotides to uridine nucleotides (C-to-U) in single-strand DNA substrates, but previous studies failed to detect deamination events in L1 retrotransposition events generated in the presence of A3A.

     To elucidate the mechanism of A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition, researchers employed an in vitro assay (L1 Element Amplification Protocol or LEAP) that recapitulates the first strand cDNA synthesis step of the L1 integration. The results showed that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. This deamination leads to inexact TSDs (target site duplications) in the post-integration site.

     The researchers further demonstrated that engineered L1 retrotransposition events derived from cultured human cells that co-express A3A and the uracil DNA glycosylase inhibitor protein (UGI) contain strand-specific, A3A-mediated C-to-U deamination events. These data indicate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration.

     Previous studies had shown that over-expression of wild-type APOBEC3A cytidine deaminase protein (A3A) can potently inhibit L1 retrotransposition in cultured human cells, but deamination events were not detected in L1 retrotransposition events generated in the presence of A3A. The degradation of deaminated retrotransposition intermediates could reduce L1 retrotransposition levels in cultured cell assays and eliminate evidence of A3A-mediated deamination events in the resultant retrotransposed L1 sequences. This scenario could explain why previous studies failed to detect A3A-mediated deamination events in engineered L1 retrotransposition events.

     The findings of this study provide a mechanistic explanation of how the A3A cytidine deaminase protein can inhibit L1 retrotransposition. The results demonstrate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration, leading to inexact TSDs in the post-integration site.

     The study investigated the role of the activity-regulated, non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity. The researchers found that mice lacking Rem2 (Rem2-/-) failed to exhibit normal ocular dominance plasticity during the critical period. At the cellular level, Rem2 was shown to regulate intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. In vitro and in vivo recordings revealed increased spontaneous firing rates in the absence of Rem2. These findings demonstrate that Rem2 is a key molecule that regulates neuronal excitability and circuit function in the context of changing sensory experience.

     The study suggests that Rem2 functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in neuronal activity to preserve proper circuit function. The results raise the possibility that Rem2 may play an important role in regulating the absolute responsiveness of the cortex to visual stimulation, such as through regulation of neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling.      The study provides an important step forward in connecting molecular regulators of neuronal morphology with broader circuit function. Given that Rem2 expression is acutely regulated by visual experience, the researchers sought to determine if Rem2 plays a role in activity-dependent processes in the visual cortex following sensory deprivation by investigating ocular dominance plasticity. The activity-regulated Ras-like GTPase Rem2 is an excellent candidate to link the activity of a neural circuit with functional plasticity. Rem2 is a member of the Rad/Rem/Rem2/Gem/Kir (RGK) protein family, a Ras-related subfamily of small GTPases, whose expression and function are regulated by neuronal activity in response to calcium influx through voltage-gated calcium channels.
(summary)
     The article presents an analysis of the physical limits to magnetogenetics, a field that aims to make cells responsive to magnetic fields for controlling their activity. Recent studies have reported promising results, including the identification of a protein complex that acts like a compass needle to guide magnetic orientation in animals and the magnetic control of membrane conductance by attaching ferritin to an ion channel protein. However, the article argues that these claims conflict with basic laws of physics, as the forces or torques produced by magnetic fields are too small to have the desired effects on molecular orientation or membrane channels. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes is also found to limit their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells.

     The article suggests that if the reported phenomena do occur, they must rely on entirely different mechanisms. The proposed routes to magnetogenetics, based on either pulling or heating a ferritin/channel complex with magnetic fields, are unlikely to be successful. One study reported the design of a molecular system intended to couple magnetic fields to ionic current across the cell membrane, but the experimental results are limited and hard to interpret.

     The article also discusses the biophysical basis for magnetosensation, the ability of organisms to sense the Earth's magnetic field for navigation. Two competing proposals have been put forward: one suggests that magnetosensation relies on a magnetite mechanism, while the other proposes that the magnetic field acts on single molecules in biochemical reactions. The article highlights the need for a deeper understanding of the biophysical mechanisms underlying magnetosensation and magnetogenetics.

     The article concludes that the paramagnetic nature of protein complexes seriously limits their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells. The forces or torques produced by magnetic fields are too small by many orders of magnitude to have the desired effects on molecular orientation or membrane channels. As a result, the proposed routes to magnetogenetics have no chance of success, barring dramatic new discoveries about the structure of biological matter. The article emphasizes the need for a more fundamental understanding of the biophysical mechanisms underlying magnetosensation and magnetogenetics.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how stem cells switch between self-renewal, differentiation, and quiescence, a state of dormancy. Drosophila neural progenitors, also known as neuroblasts, have been a valuable model system for studying self-renewal and differentiation. However, the mechanisms governing quiescence in these cells have remained poorly understood. A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating the role of the Prospero differentiation factor in neuroblast quiescence.

     The study found that when neuroblasts enter quiescence, the Prospero differentiation factor is transiently detected in the neuroblast nucleus at low levels. This pulse of Prospero precedes the establishment of a unique molecular profile characterized by the absence of most progenitor and differentiation markers. Furthermore, the study demonstrated that loss of Prospero prevents entry into quiescence, while a pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero can drive proliferating larval neuroblasts into quiescence.

     The researchers used Drosophila neuroblasts as a model system to investigate the role of Prospero in quiescence. They found that nearly all neuroblasts that were quiescent at 12 hours after induction had reactivated proliferation and re-expressed progenitor markers by 24 hours. Additionally, they showed that precocious low-level nuclear Prospero could advance the timing of neuroblast quiescence.

     The study also explored the relationship between Prospero levels and neuroblast quiescence. They found that the pulse of nuclear Prospero precisely matched the timing of neuroblast quiescence, occurring earlier in mutants with advanced quiescence and later in mutants with delayed quiescence. This suggests a functional relationship between Prospero levels and neuroblast quiescence.

     The researchers propose that Prospero levels distinguish three progenitor fates: absent for self-renewal, low for quiescence, and high for differentiation. This study provides new insights into the mechanisms governing neuroblast quiescence and highlights the importance of Prospero in regulating this process. The findings have implications for our understanding of stem cell biology and may inform the development of stem cell-based therapeutics.
Scientists have developed a new method to study the process of HIV-1 capsid uncoating, which is essential for the release of the viral genetic material into the host cell. Using single-particle fluorescence microscopy, researchers were able to observe the uncoating kinetics of individual HIV capsids in real-time. The study found that the opening of the first defect in the capsid lattice is the rate-limiting step in the uncoating process, followed by a rapid and catastrophic collapse of the capsid.

The researchers also investigated the effects of different capsid-binding molecules on the uncoating process. They found that the inhibitor PF74 accelerates the opening of the capsid but stabilizes the remaining lattice, while a polyanion that binds to a conserved arginine cluster in the lattice delays the initiation of uncoating but does not prevent subsequent lattice disassembly. These findings suggest that different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently, and the interplay between different capsid-binding regulators determines the overall uncoating kinetics.

The study used a novel in vitro single-molecule fluorescence imaging assay to follow the uncoating kinetics of hundreds of individual HIV capsids in a single experiment. This approach allowed the researchers to resolve intermediates in the disassembly pathway that are averaged out in traditional ensemble assays. The results showed that the integrity and stability of capsids in a population are highly heterogeneous, with the majority containing defects and/or uncoating immediately after removal of the viral membrane.

In the absence of capsid binders, uncoating is an all-or-none process that proceeds rapidly after the first defect is created. The study also demonstrated that the half-life of the HIV capsid is sufficiently long to use PFO-permeabilized viral particles as a platform to quantify the binding of labeled proteins to authentic viral capsids at the single-particle level. The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of HIV-1 capsid uncoating and its regulation by capsid-binding molecules.

     The cJUN NH2-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway plays a crucial role in maintaining genome stability and preventing tumor initiation in breast cancer. Contrary to expectations, loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway are frequently found in breast cancer genomic DNA. These mutations were thought to be associated with reduced JNK signaling, which can promote cell proliferation by activating the AP1 transcription factor. However, research has shown that JNK deficiency in the murine breast epithelium leads to genomic instability and the development of breast cancer. Furthermore, JNK deficiency accelerates tumor formation in a murine model of breast cancer, characterized by widespread early neoplasia and genomic instability.

     The study found that JNK plays a key role in the initiation of tumor development, rather than the growth of established tumors. This suggests that JNK pathway defects are 'driver' mutations that promote genome instability and tumor initiation. The frequent loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway in human breast cancer may therefore represent 'driver' mutations that promote tumor development. The study demonstrates that JNK loss plays a key role at the early stages of breast cancer development by promoting mammary gland neoplasia.

     The results of this study challenge the previous understanding of the JNK signaling pathway's role in breast cancer. The association of breast cancer with reduced JNK signaling was thought to be a correlation, but the study shows that JNK signaling is actually required to prevent tumor initiation. The JNK signaling pathway may therefore be suppressed in many breast cancers. The study's findings have significant implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying breast cancer development and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies targeting the JNK pathway.

     The study's major conclusions are that JNK deficiency in the mammary epithelium can cause murine breast cancer and that JNK deficiency rapidly accelerates tumor development in a mouse model of breast cancer. These findings highlight the importance of the JNK signaling pathway in maintaining genome stability and preventing tumor initiation in breast cancer. Further research is needed to fully understand the role of the JNK pathway in breast cancer development and to explore its potential as a therapeutic target.
A recent study has identified peptidylglycine Î±-amidating monooxygenase (PAM) as a crucial enzyme required for the assembly of cilia in various cell types. Cilia are hair-like structures that protrude from the surface of cells and play a vital role in sensing the environment and transmitting signals. The researchers found that PAM is essential for the normal formation of motile and primary cilia in Chlamydomonas, planaria, and mice. In Chlamydomonas, PAM knockdown lines failed to assemble cilia beyond the transition zone, had abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia assembly components. Similarly, in planaria, decreased PAM gene expression reduced motile ciliary density on the ventral surface and resulted in the appearance of cytosolic axonemes lacking a ciliary membrane. In mice, the architecture of primary cilia on neuroepithelial cells in PAM-deficient embryos was also aberrant.

The study suggests that PAM activity and alterations in post-Golgi trafficking contribute to the observed ciliogenesis defects. The researchers propose that PAM plays a key conserved role during the early steps of ciliogenesis, revealing a novel link between amidation and cilium assembly in multiple cell types. PAM is thought to be involved in the biogenesis of the motile cilia present in Chlamydomonas and on the ventral epithelium of planaria, as well as the normal assembly of the primary cilia of neuroepithelial cells in mammals.

Interestingly, while reduction of PAM in Chlamydomonas and planaria resulted in a dramatic defect in ciliary assembly, complete loss of PAM in mice did not abolish primary cilia formation in neuroepithelial cells, although they were significantly shorter than cilia in wild-type animals and in many cases only ciliary stubs were evident. The study highlights the importance of PAM in ciliogenesis and provides new insights into the mechanisms underlying the assembly of cilia in different cell types. The findings also suggest that PAM may have a novel and highly conserved signaling or sensory function in eukaryotic cilia.
