     Biomedical research has shed light on the critical roles of class III myosins (Myo3) and actin-bundling protein Espin in regulating the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells. Defects in these proteins have been linked to hereditary hearing impairments. Myo3 interacts with Espin1 through its tail homology I motif (THDI), but the mechanism by which Myo3 acts through Espin1 to regulate actin bundle assembly and stabilization was unclear.

     Recent studies have revealed that Myo3 THDI contains a pair of repeat sequences capable of independently and strongly binding to the ankyrin repeats of Espin1, providing an unexpected Myo3-mediated cross-linking mechanism of Espin1. The structures of Myo3 in complex with Espin1 have elucidated the mechanism of binding and revealed a Myo3-induced release of Espin1 auto-inhibition mechanism. Furthermore, Myo3-mediated cross-linking has been shown to promote actin fiber bundling activity of Espin1.

     Research has also suggested that Myo3 binding can release auto-inhibition and increase the diameter of Espin1-promoted actin bundles. Myo3a and Myo3b have been found to co-localize with Espin1 at the tips of stereocilia of hair cells, and co-expression of Myo3a and Espin1 can stimulate elongation of stereocilia in cultured organ of Corti hair cells.

     The interaction between Myo3 and Espin1 is thought to promote the formation of thicker parallel actin bundle fibers in cellular structures such as stereocilia. The THDI mediates binding of Myo3 to its cargo protein Espin1, allowing Myo3 to transport Espin1 to the tips of actin bundle-based structures. Once localized, Espin1 promotes the elongation of actin protrusions.

     The study reveals a previously unrecognized direct myosin binding-induced actin bundling activity regulation mechanism of an actin filament cross-linker protein. The full-length Myo3/Espin1 complex may have even higher actin fiber bundling activity than the Myo3-ABR12/Espin1 complex studied in this work. The findings provide new insights into the molecular basis governing the Myo3 and Espin1 interaction and highlight the critical roles of Myo3 and Espin1 in stereocilia development and maintenance.
Voltage-gated calcium channels play a crucial role in various cellular functions, including synaptic transmission, muscle contraction, and gene expression. These channels translate membrane depolarization into calcium influx, enabling cells to respond to electrical activity. Researchers have sought to understand the molecular mechanisms governing the specific voltage-sensing properties of these channels.

     To investigate the structural determinants of voltage-gating properties in calcium channels, scientists combined structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition kinetics of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1. The study aimed to identify the molecular mechanisms governing specific voltage-sensing properties.

     The researchers generated advanced structure models of the activated and resting states of CaV1.1 VSDs using the Rosetta computational modeling software. These models revealed the basic features of the sliding helix model, which describes the voltage-sensing mechanism in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels. The study found that stabilization of the activated state enhanced the voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slowed the kinetics.

     The results suggest that specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels. The study's findings provide a mechanistic model explaining how distinct ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can result in the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated calcium channels. The results were supported by mutagenesis and electrophysiology analysis, which showed that alterations of kinetic properties recognized experimentally were reliably reproduced and explained by the computer model.

     The study fills an important gap in the understanding of the voltage-sensing mechanism in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels. The results provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms governing the specific voltage-sensing properties of calcium channels, which are essential for various cellular functions. The study's findings have implications for understanding the regulation of cellular excitability and the coupling of electrical activity to fundamental cell functions.

     The study investigated the role of matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) in adult visual plasticity, specifically in the primary visual cortex (V1) of healthy and stroke-affected brains. MMPs have been associated with experience-dependent plasticity in the juvenile brain, but their function in the adult brain is not well understood.      Biomedical research has made significant progress in understanding the structure and function of AMPA (Î±-amino-3-hydroxy-5-methyl-4-isoxazole propionate) receptors, which are crucial for signal transduction at vertebrate excitatory synapses. AMPA receptors are tetrameric ligand-gated ion channels composed of four subunits that assemble as a dimer-of-dimers. Recent advances in cryo-electron microscopy have provided full-length structures of AMPA receptors, both alone and in combination with auxiliary subunits. These structures suggest that the dimers may undergo substantial lateral motions during activation, resulting in the opening of the extracellular layer along the central twofold symmetry axis.

     To investigate this further, researchers used bifunctional methanethiosulfonate cross-linkers to calibrate the conformations of functional AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of the auxiliary subunit Stargazin. The results indicate that the extracellular layer of AMPA receptors can become trapped in stable, opened-up conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. However, the presence of Stargazin limits this conformational flexibility. Under synaptic conditions, where brief glutamate exposures and the presence of auxiliary proteins dominate, the extracellular domains of AMPA receptors are likely to remain compact during activation and desensitization.

     The study suggests that the extracellular domains of synaptic AMPA receptors are unlikely to undergo large structural rearrangements during synaptic transmission and instead work in a fairly compact conformational regime, unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions. The presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Stargazin, plays a crucial role in maintaining the compact conformation of AMPA receptors, which is essential for their proper functioning at synapses. The findings of this study provide new insights into the structural dynamics of AMPA receptors and highlight the importance of auxiliary subunits in regulating their conformational flexibility.
Scientists have long faced challenges in isolating immune cells from the intestines of mice infected with helminths, a type of parasitic worm, due to the strong immune response triggered by the infection. This response leads to the production of mucus, tissue remodeling, and the infiltration of immune cells, making it difficult to obtain a clear picture of the immune response. To overcome this challenge, researchers optimized a standard intestinal digestion protocol to successfully isolate millions of immune cells from the heavily infected duodenum, the first part of the small intestine.

To validate the accuracy of the isolated cells, the researchers used a high-dimensional spectral flow cytometry panel, which allowed them to analyze the cells in great detail. They also confirmed their findings using confocal microscopy, a technique that provides high-resolution images of the cells. The combination of the optimized cell isolation protocol and high-dimensional analysis enabled the researchers to identify many known hallmarks of the anti-parasite immune response throughout the entire course of helminth infection.

The researchers characterized the isolated cells using a 23-color spectral flow cytometry panel, which incorporated many of the hallmark surface and intracellular markers associated with helminth infections. They found that the cell isolation protocol and high-dimensional analysis allowed them to characterize many known hallmarks of both innate and adaptive anti-parasite immune responses. The changes in immune cell populations were validated using confocal microscopy, and the results showed that many of these changes were only observed locally, highlighting the importance of good cell isolation techniques to investigate intestinal responses against helminths directly.

The researchers were able to quantify specific immune cell populations, such as B220+, Siglec F+, CD3+ CD4+, CD3+ CD4-, and CD64+ cells, using confocal microscopy and compared their frequency to the spectral flow cytometry data. The results showed that the cell isolation protocol provided an accurate representation of intestinal immune responses. The study's findings have the potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries of local helminth immune responses that have previously been unfeasible due to the challenges associated with intestinal digestion during helminth infection.
Scientists have developed a new model to understand how the 20S proteasome, a key enzyme involved in protein degradation, regulates protein half-life and affects various cellular processes. The 20S proteasome is a dynamic enzyme that changes its activity over time due to interactions between substrates and products, as well as its structural characteristics. A team of researchers used a combination of experimental and computational modeling approaches to create a comprehensive mathematical model that describes the regulatory components of the 20S proteasome and its interactions with substrates and products.

The model suggests that the rate-limiting step of protein degradation is the transport of substrates into the proteasome, and that the efficiency of this transport varies between human standard and immuno-proteasomes. This difference in transport efficiency affects the total degradation rate and the usage of substrate cleavage sites. The researchers used carefully designed experimental assays and short fluorogenic peptides to study proteasome dynamics in real-time.

The study found that the proteasome dynamics observed in purified 20S proteasomes were similar to those observed in protein homogenates, where 20S proteasomes are surrounded by regulatory molecules. This suggests that the model describes a core set of proteasome features that can reflect proteasome dynamics in more complex cellular environments. The researchers also found that poly-ubiquitin tagging, transport, and peptide-bond hydrolysis regulate protein half-life and affect various metabolic processes in the cell.

The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of proteasomal action, particularly the causes and extent of differences in substrate-specific proteolysis between different proteasome isoforms. The findings have implications for understanding the regulation of protein degradation and its impact on cellular processes, and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for diseases related to protein misfolding and degradation. The model developed in this study can be used to predict the behavior of the 20S proteasome and its interactions with substrates and products, providing a valuable tool for further research in this area.

     The recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission. In Drosophila, histamine recycling is required for visual synaptic transmission. Synaptic histamine is rapidly taken up by laminar glia and converted to carcinine. Carcinine is then delivered back to photoreceptors, where it is hydrolyzed to release histamine and Î²-alanine. However, it was unclear how Î²-alanine is returned to the laminar glial cells.

     A new Î²-alanine transporter, named BalaT, has been identified. Null balat mutants exhibited lower levels of Î²-alanine and less Î²-alanine accumulation in the retina. BalaT is expressed and required in retinal pigment cells for maintaining visual synaptic transmission and phototaxis behavior. These results provide the first genetic evidence that retinal pigment cells play a critical role in visual neurotransmission and suggest that a BalaT-dependent Î²-alanine trafficking pathway is required for histamine homeostasis and visual neurotransmission.

     The transport of Î²-alanine seems less important, as it is present at higher concentrations in Drosophila. However, Î²-alanine recycling is essential for maintaining histamine homeostasis and photoreceptor synaptic transmission. Establishing and maintaining a Î²-alanine pool play key roles in sustaining visual synaptic transmission. The majority of pigment granules, which reduce the intensity of light exposure, are formed in retinal pigment cells of the Drosophila compound eye.

     High levels of Î²-alanine are detected in retinal pigment cells, suggesting that pigment cells may be critical for the transport and storage of Î²-alanine in this system. An efficient Î²-alanine recycling system has been proposed to compensate for the rapid loss of Î²-alanine that occurs in the conversion of histamine to carcinine. Mutations in balat disrupted transport and storage of Î²-alanine in the retina, resulting in dramatically reduced photoreceptor synaptic activity and visual behavior.

     This suggests a conserved function for Î²-alanine trafficking in mammals and Drosophila. Our current work, together with previous reports, provides evidence for the formulation of a more complete histamine recycling pathway, which is critical for sustaining photoreceptor synaptic transmission. In this pathway, carcinine is synthesized in epithelial glial cells within the optic lamina and is transported back to photoreceptor cells via the synaptic cleft.
Scientists have been trying to understand the role of oxidants in aging for a long time, but the exact mechanisms are still unclear. A recent study used yeast cells to investigate the relationship between oxidants and aging. The researchers found that as yeast cells age, they undergo a sudden collapse in their redox balance, which affects over 80% of the proteins in the cell. This collapse is not caused by an increase in oxidants, as previously thought, but rather by a decrease in a molecule called NADPH, which is essential for maintaining the cell's redox balance.

The study showed that the decrease in NADPH levels occurs early in the aging process and triggers a cascade of events that ultimately leads to the oxidation of the cell's proteins. The researchers also found that caloric restriction, a well-known method for extending lifespan, increases NADPH levels and delays the onset of this cascade. This suggests that the decrease in NADPH levels is a key factor in the aging process.

The study used a type of yeast called Saccharomyces cerevisiae, which is commonly used as a model organism for studying aging. The researchers monitored the oxidation status of almost 300 different proteins in the yeast cells and found that they undergo a global redox collapse several days before cell death. This collapse is characterized by the oxidation of thiol-containing proteins, which are essential for many cellular processes.

The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of aging and suggest that the decrease in NADPH levels is a critical factor in the aging process. The study also highlights the importance of maintaining redox balance in cells and suggests that interventions that increase NADPH levels, such as caloric restriction, may be beneficial for extending lifespan. Further research is needed to confirm these findings and to understand the specific mechanisms by which NADPH levels affect aging. The study's results also raise the possibility that the oxidation of certain proteins may actually contribute to the beneficial effects of aging, such as the down-regulation of cellular processes that would otherwise shorten lifespan.
summary
     Biologists have long recognized the importance of maintaining homochirality in the biological macromolecular world, where the vast majority of molecules are composed of either L-amino acids or D-amino acids. A key mechanism for enforcing this homochirality is the removal of D-amino acids that are mistakenly attached to transfer RNAs (tRNAs) during protein synthesis. This process is carried out by an enzyme called D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD), which selectively removes D-amino acids from tRNAs to prevent them from being incorporated into proteins.

     A recent study has shed light on the mechanism by which DTD achieves its chiral specificity. The researchers used X-ray crystallography to determine the structure of DTD from the parasite Plasmodium falciparum in complex with a substrate-mimicking analog. The results show that the enzyme uses a unique "cross-subunit" Gly-cisPro dipeptide to capture the chiral center of the incoming D-aminoacyl-tRNA. This dipeptide is inserted into the active site of the dimeric enzyme, where it plays a central role in recognizing the D-amino acid.

     Notably, the recognition mechanism does not involve direct interaction with the side chain of the D-amino acid, but rather relies on weak hydrogen bonds to select for the correct chirality. The researchers found that if an L-amino acid were to bind in the active site, it would have to adopt one of three possible conformations, highlighting the strict chiral specificity of the enzyme.

     The study provides insights into a fundamental enantioselective mechanism that is essential for maintaining homochirality in proteins. By removing D-amino acids from tRNAs, DTD ensures that the cellular pool of L-aminoacyl-tRNAs is enriched, preventing the incorporation of D-amino acids into proteins. The findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of translational apparatus and the importance of chiral enforcement mechanisms in preventing D-amino acid infiltration. The study also highlights the crucial role played by weak hydrogen bonds in D-chirality selection, providing a clear explanation for the enzyme's ability to act on multiple D-amino acids.
summary
     The cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is a chloride ion channel that belongs to the ATP-binding cassette (ABC) protein superfamily. CFTR channels are gated by cytosolic ATP after phosphorylation of the cytosolic regulatory (R) domain by cAMP-dependent protein kinase (PKA). Research has shown that ATP-binding-induced dimerization of two cytosolic nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs) opens the pore, and dimer disruption following ATP hydrolysis closes it. However, in certain cystic fibrosis (CF) mutants, spontaneous openings without ATP are possible and are stimulated by ivacaftor, a clinically approved CFTR potentiator.

     A study has correlated energetic coupling between residues across the dimer interface with spontaneous pore opening/closure in single CFTR channels. The results show that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which may occur even without ATP. This means that coordinated NBD/pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, and ATP alters the stability, but not the fundamental structural architecture, of open- and closed-pore conformations.

     The study also found that the presence of bound ATP does not alter the global arrangement of conformations, but rather acts as a'molecular glue' to stabilize the transition state and the open state. This suggests that the mechanism by which binding of the natural ligand ATP promotes CFTR channel opening is through stabilizing the transition state and the open state, rather than altering the global arrangement of conformations.

     The research provides insights into the understanding of CF mutation and drug mechanisms. The strict coupling between NBD and pore movements, regardless of the absence or presence of ATP, constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent channel gating and helps explain modulatory effects of mutations, or of physiological or pharmacological gating regulators. The study also highlights the importance of understanding the underlying driving forces, which are likely conserved among ABC proteins.
Scientists have discovered a new force that drives the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers. This force, called the "orderphobic effect," arises from the pre-transition effect between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane. When a protein with a hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase is embedded in an ordered bilayer, it stabilizes a microscopic order-disorder interface. The stiffness of this interface is finite, and when two such proteins approach each other, they assemble because assembly reduces the net interfacial energy.

     The orderphobic effect is mediated by the proximity to the order-disorder phase transition and the size and hydrophobic mismatch of the protein. This phenomenon is analogous to the hydrophobic effect, where the strength and range of the force depend on the ability of the protein to induce a disordered layer in its vicinity. The strength and range of the orderphobic force are significantly larger than those that could arise from membrane elasticity for the membranes considered.

     The researchers used large-scale molecular simulation to demonstrate the orderphobic effect. They found that a transmembrane protein with a hydrophobic thickness of approximately 2.6 nm will favor the structure of the disordered phase. If the protein is large enough, it can melt the ordered phase near the protein and result in the formation of an order-disorder interface. This interface provides a force for assembly between the proteins.

     The orderphobic effect has implications for our understanding of biological membranes and transmembrane proteins. The researchers predict that the effect may be amenable to experimental tests by single molecule tracking techniques. They also note that biological membranes and transmembrane proteins are more complicated than the models considered in this paper, with multiple components allowing for more than one order-disorder transition. A dual to the orderphobic effect also exists, where a transmembrane protein in the disordered phase that favors the ordered phase can nucleate an ordered region and order-disorder interface.

     The discovery of the orderphobic effect provides new insights into the forces that drive the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers. It highlights the importance of the pre-transition effect between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane and the role of protein size and hydrophobic mismatch in mediating this effect. Further research is needed to fully understand the implications of the orderphobic effect for biological membranes and transmembrane proteins.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have long used fluorescent calcium indicators to study neural dynamics, but the relationship between these indicators and action potentials (APs) has remained unclear. To address this issue, a recent study characterized the AP-fluorescence transfer function in vivo for 48 layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in primary visual cortex of transgenic mice expressing GCaMP6s or GCaMP6f. The researchers used simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings to investigate the relationship between APs and fluorescence signals.

     The study found that under optimal conditions, most APs were detected, but under conditions typical of population imaging studies, only a minority of APs were detected, often less than 10% for single AP events and around 20-30% for double AP events. This highlights the limitations of AP detection under more realistic imaging conditions. The researchers also found that inferring the underlying AP train or firing rate from calcium imaging remains challenging due to the large field of view required for population imaging studies, which reduces the signal-to-noise ratio.

     To construct AP-calcium fluorescence response curves, the researchers identified isolated AP events and summed APs over 250 ms within each event. They found that only a minority of APs were used to construct these curves. The study also tested the performance of several spike inference models, detecting APs under optimal conditions and under less optimal conditions typical of population imaging experiments.

     The results of this study provide insight into the relationship between spiking activity in vivo and fluorescence signals and will aid the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging datasets. The findings have implications for the use of genetically encoded calcium indicators (GECIs) in population imaging studies, which are widely used to report neuronal activity within local populations in vivo. The study's results will facilitate the interpretation of datasets from mainstream transgenic mouse lines, such as the Allen Institute's Brain Observatory Visual Coding dataset.
summary
     Biologists have discovered a unique biological switching valve in the female genitalia of the Brazilian cave insect Neotrogla. This valve allows females to control the direction of seminal flow, enabling them to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males within a short time span. The valve complex is composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-and-outflow canals. Females of Neotrogla have a penis-like intromittent organ, which they use to coercively anchor males and obtain voluminous semen. The semen is packed in a capsule that is not only used for fertilization but also consumed by the female as nutrition.

     The switching valve has two slots for insemination, allowing Neotrogla females to continue mating while the first slot is occupied. This adaptation enables females to compete for seminal gifts in their nutrient-poor cave habitats through long copulation times and multiple seminal injections. The evolution of this switching valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of the intromittent organ in Neotrogla. The female penis of Neotrogla bears spines, which females use to anchor males coercively during copulation, likely to obtain more semen from a male.

     In contrast to close relatives, Neotrogla females engage in long copulation durations of 40-70 hours, compared to just 50 minutes for the formation of one seminal capsule. This is an exaggerated adaptation for competition for nuptial gifts in this group of insects inhabiting highly oligotrophic cave habitats. The presence of a spermathecal plate with a valve function to control seminal flow may further refine our understanding of the causes and consequences of the reversed sexual selection in this genus. Female-female competition for males to receive nutritious seminal substances is considered the most important factor driving the evolution of the female penis in Neotrogla.
Summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the mechanisms underlying synaptic exocytosis, the process by which neurons release neurotransmitters. A key component of this process is the assembly of soluble N-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor attachment receptor (SNARE) proteins, which couple their stage-wise folding and assembly to rapid exocytosis in a Munc18-1-dependent manner. However, the functions of the different assembly stages in exocytosis and the role of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly were not well understood.

    Using optical tweezers, researchers observed four distinct stages of assembly in SNARE N-terminal, middle, C-terminal, and linker domains (NTD, MD, CTD, and LD, respectively). They found that SNARE layer mutations differentially affect SNARE assembly, with the NTD and CTD responsible for vesicle docking and fusion, respectively, while the MD regulates SNARE assembly and fusion. Munc18-1 initiates SNARE assembly and structures the t-SNARE C-terminus independent of the syntaxin N-terminal regulatory domain (NRD) and stabilizes the half-zippered SNARE complex dependent upon the NRD.

    The researchers also found that Munc18-1 regulates every stage of SNARE assembly, from initiation to stabilization of the half-zippered SNARE complex. They propose a working model to account for the multiple roles of Munc18-1 in stage-wise SNARE zippering and membrane fusion. This model suggests that Munc18-1 mainly acts on the folding intermediates of SNAREs to chaperone their assembly.

    The study used optical tweezers to observe the forces associated with Munc18-1-mediated de novo SNARE assembly and found that Munc18-1 enhances de novo SNARE assembly by holding the SNAP-25 molecule together with the tethered syntaxin or VAMP2 as the t-SNARE unfolded. The researchers also observed three force-dependent activities of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly, consistent with previously reported multiple modes of binding between Munc18-1 and the SNARE complex.

    Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of synaptic exocytosis and the role of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly, shedding light on the complex process of neurotransmitter release. The findings have implications for our understanding of neurological disorders and the development of new therapeutic strategies.
Summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how cells create diverse adhesion sites on their surface to interact with the extracellular matrix. These sites, called focal adhesions, are crucial for cell movement, growth, and signaling. A team of researchers investigated how the integrin adhesome, a group of proteins involved in adhesion site formation, is assembled and exchanged between focal adhesions and the cytosol, the cell's internal fluid.

     Using advanced microscopy techniques, the researchers found that the integrin adhesome is already pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks. These building blocks can rapidly assemble into adhesion sites on the cell surface. Importantly, the researchers discovered that stationary focal adhesions release the same types of protein complexes that they recruit, maintaining a uniform pool of building blocks in the cytosol.

     The findings suggest that the integrin adhesome is extensively pre-assembled in the cytosol, forming multi-protein building blocks that facilitate rapid and modular assembly of adhesion sites. The building blocks are spatially uniform around stationary focal adhesions, indicating that they exchange with the cytosol without altering their composition. This symmetric exchange preserves the specifications of the building blocks and maintains the assembly logic of the system.

     The researchers propose a model in which the integrin adhesome is pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks. At the plasma membrane, these building blocks can self-assemble into adhesion sites by passing through checkpoints that induce additional protein interactions. This model ensures that the self-assembly process remains controlled and specific, preventing the formation of aberrant structures.

     The study provides new insights into the assembly logic of adhesion sites and highlights the importance of pre-assembled multi-protein building blocks in facilitating rapid and modular self-assembly. The findings have implications for our understanding of cell-matrix interactions and may contribute to the development of new therapeutic strategies for diseases related to adhesion site dysfunction.
Researchers have identified a common electrophysiological marker that can track states of reduced arousal in humans, including different sleep stages and anesthesia. This marker is based on the 1/f spectral slope of the electrophysiological power spectrum, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale-free component of neural activity. The study used intracranial and surface EEG recordings from four independent data sets to demonstrate that the spectral slope can distinguish wakefulness from propofol anesthesia, non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, and rapid eye movement (REM) sleep.

The researchers hypothesized that the spectral slope would become more negative (i.e., the power spectrum steeper) in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness. They also predicted that the spectral slope could discriminate wakefulness from NREM and REM sleep. The study found that the spectral slope differed significantly between wakefulness and propofol anesthesia, and this state-dependent modulation generalized to other forms of decreased arousal, such as sleep.

The researchers analyzed two datasets obtained during a full night of sleep and found that the spectral slope could discern wakefulness from different sleep stages, including REM and NREM sleep. They also demonstrated that the non-oscillatory, aperiodic part of the power spectrum, which is devoid of prominent low-frequency oscillatory components, reliably differentiates wakefulness from all three states of reduced arousal level, namely REM, N3 sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol.

The study's findings provide empirical evidence for the notion that the slope in the range from 30 to 45 Hz correlates well with all stages of arousal. The spectral slope tracked changes in arousal levels in both propofol anesthesia and sleep in humans with high temporal precision and is observable on a wide range of timescales from sub-second epochs to full night recordings. The study's results provide a common electrophysiological marker that can be used to track states of reduced arousal in humans, including different sleep stages and anesthesia.
summary
     Biologists have discovered that chloroplasts in plant cells are connected to the nucleus by dynamic tubular extensions called stromules, which play a crucial role in the plant's innate immune response. These stromules extend along microtubules (MTs) in the cell and are stabilized by actin filaments (AFs), which provide anchor points for the stromules. Research has shown that the organization of MTs directly affects the dynamics of stromules, with stabilization of MTs increasing the number and length of stromules.

     During an innate immune response, stromules extend along MTs towards the nucleus and attach to actin anchor points surrounding the nucleus. These perinuclear stromule attachments guide chloroplasts to the nucleus, facilitating their movement. This process is not limited to immune responses, as stromule-directed chloroplast movement was observed in steady-state conditions without immune induction, suggesting it is a general function of stromules in epidermal cells.

     The study found that microtubules are required for stromule extension and movement, as depolymerization of MTs led to stromule retraction, and stabilization of MTs increased stromule frequency. Actin filaments, on the other hand, provide anchor points for stromules but are not required for their extension. The results suggest that MTs and AFs work together to facilitate perinuclear clustering of chloroplasts during an innate immune response.

     The researchers propose a model in which stromules extend along MTs and connect to AF anchor points surrounding the nucleus, facilitating stromule-directed movement of chloroplasts to the nucleus during innate immunity. This study provides new insights into the mechanism of stromule dynamics and the role of cytoskeletal elements in plant cells, which support various cellular functions, including cytoplasmic streaming, cell division, and nuclear positioning. The findings have implications for understanding the importance of perinuclear chloroplast clustering during innate immunity and the molecular components required for stromule dynamics and movement.
Scientists have developed a new method to predict how amino acids interact with each other across protein interfaces, which are areas where two or more proteins come together to form a complex. This method uses evolutionary information to identify pairs of amino acids that are likely to be in contact with each other. The researchers applied this method to 50S ribosomal units and 28 other bacterial protein complexes with known structures and found that the predicted pairs of amino acids were almost always in contact in the complex, as long as there were enough aligned sequences.

    The team then used this method to make predictions for 36 protein complexes with unknown structures and generated models for four of these complexes, including the tripartite ATP-independent periplasmic (TRAP) transporter, the tripartite efflux system, the pyruvate formate lyase-activating enzyme complex, and the methionine ABC transporter. The results suggest that this method can accurately predict interactions across protein interfaces given a sufficient number of aligned sequences.

    In a large set of complexes with known structures, the researchers found that pairs of amino acids that covary (i.e., change together over time) are almost always in contact in the three-dimensional structure, provided there are enough aligned sequences. They also found that significant residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting protein pairs but very rarely between non-interacting pairs, indicating that this method can be useful for predicting whether two proteins interact.

    This study builds on previous research that has used global statistical models to coevolution-based contact prediction across interfaces, but has been limited to specific cases such as the histidine-kinase/response-regulator two-component system. The new method developed in this study can be applied to a wide range of protein complexes and has the potential to contribute to the determination of the structures of these biologically important complexes. The predicted residue-residue interactions across protein interfaces of unknown structure are presented in Figure 3, which shows strongly co-evolving residue pairs for complexes without known structure that had at least one prediction with a GREMLIN score greater than or equal to 0.85.
Summary
     Researchers investigated how people categorize visual patterns by using a novel gaze-contingent paradigm in a visual categorization task. Participants' eye movements were tracked while they viewed patterns, and their scan paths were found to follow an active sensing strategy. This strategy incorporates information already acquired about the scene and knowledge of the statistical structure of patterns. The results showed that categorization performance improved when locations were revealed to participants by an optimal Bayesian active sensor algorithm.

     The study used a combination of a Bayesian ideal observer and the active sensor algorithm to estimate that a major portion of the apparent suboptimality of fixation locations arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. The central process of selecting fixation locations was found to be around 70% efficient in the task. The results suggest that participants select eye movements with the goal of maximizing information about abstract categories that require the integration of information from multiple locations.

     The study extended the range of active sensing to tasks with arbitrary, not necessarily spatial, latent features, such as categories. It provided the first fixation-by-fixation analysis of information gathering under active eye movements by matching the observer model to participants' performance. This allowed the researchers to dissociate the contributions of the eye-movement selection process from those of perceptual, motor, and decision processes.

     The study estimated the efficiency of eye movements in a high-level task on a fixation-by-fixation basis. The results showed that participants' eye movements were substantially more efficient than heuristic strategies that only employed a subset of the elements of a fully closed-loop active sensing strategy. They were about 70% as efficient as the optimal active sensor that operated with the participants' own prior bias, perception noise, and saccadic inaccuracies.

     The study used a gaze-contingent categorization task to track the eye and successively reveal small apertures of the image at each fixation location. This allowed for a mathematically tractable quantification of the information at any fixation location. The visual patterns used in the study were statistically well-controlled and relatively simple, ensuring that foveal vision would dominate.
summary
     Biomedical research has made significant progress in understanding the transport of molecules across the nuclear envelope in eukaryotic cells. The nuclear pore complex (NPC) plays a crucial role in this process, allowing thousands of molecules to pass through, including large cargoes such as pathogens, mRNAs, and pre-ribosomes. However, the requirements for nuclear import of these large cargoes are not well understood, particularly the role of multivalent binding to transport receptors via nuclear localization sequences (NLSs) and the effect of size on import efficiency.

     To address this knowledge gap, a recent study investigated the nuclear import kinetics of 30 large cargo models based on four capsid-like particles in the size range of 17-36 nanometers, with tunable numbers of up to 240 NLSs. The results showed that the requirements for nuclear transport can be recapitulated by a simple two-parameter biophysical model that correlates the import flux with the energetics of large cargo transport through the NPC.

     The study found that the requirements for nuclear transport scale non-linearly with size and can be influenced by the biochemical or physicochemical properties of the cargo surface. For small cargo transport, these properties have been shown to influence nuclear transport in previous studies. The theoretical model used in this study assumes that the capsids do not interact with each other during transport through the pore.

     The study also highlights the importance of nuclear localization sequences (NLSs) and nuclear transport receptors (NTRs) in facilitating the rapid entry of cargoes into the nucleus. The NTR-mediated transport process has been characterized in previous studies, typically focusing on cargoes with one to five NLSs, and their nuclear import kinetics have been shown to follow a mono-exponential behavior.

     The findings of this study have implications for understanding the export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and large RNA export complexes. The results suggest that basic principles of this work could help in the future to better understand these processes. Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular determinants of large cargo transport into the nucleus, shedding light on the complex process of nucleocytoplasmic transport in eukaryotic cells.
Summary
     The co-stimulatory molecule CD28 plays a crucial role in the activation of helper T cells, but its function after T cell priming was not well understood. To investigate this, researchers generated a strain of mice where CD28 was removed from CD4+ T cells after priming. The study found that continued CD28 expression is essential for the expansion of effector CD4+ T cells, particularly T helper type 1 (Th1) cells, and for the differentiation and maintenance of T follicular helper (Tfh) cells during viral infection. Additionally, maintained CD28 expression is required for the clearance of the bacterium Citrobacter rodentium from the gastrointestinal tract after oral infection. These findings demonstrate that CD28 persistence is necessary for helper T cell polarization in response to infection, revealing a novel function for CD28 distinct from its role in T cell priming. The study highlights the importance of CD28 in maintaining T cell responses following activation, which is critical for effective immunity to infection.

     The researchers used a mouse model where CD28 was removed from CD4+ T cells after priming to investigate the role of CD28 after T cell activation. They found that CD28 expression is required for the expansion of Th1 cells and for the differentiation and maintenance of Tfh cells. Furthermore, maintained CD28 expression is necessary for the clearance of Citrobacter rodentium from the gastrointestinal tract after oral infection. This demonstrates that CD28 expression is required after T cell priming for intact effector CD4+ T cell responses during infection.

     The study also showed that CD28 signaling is essential for multiple facets of CD4+ T cell activation, including proliferation, survival, glucose metabolism, and migration. CD28-deficient mice have reduced expansion of effector CD4+ T cells and do not form Th1 and Tfh cells after infection or immunization. Tfh differentiation requires CD28 ligation during interactions of primed T cells with B cells, and fully differentiated Tfh cells require CD28 expression for their survival. By contrast, Th1 cells do not require CD28 for their maintenance, but do for their expansion following T cell activation. The study highlights the importance of CD28 in maintaining T cell responses following activation, which is critical for effective immunity to infection.
summary
     Helicobacter pylori, a bacterium that colonizes the stomach, has a unique helical shape that allows it to efficiently colonize the stomach lining. However, the molecular mechanisms that generate this helical shape are not well understood. Researchers investigated how H. pylori maintains its helical shape and found that two cytoskeletal proteins, MreB and CcmA, play a crucial role in defining zones of enhanced cell wall synthesis.

     The study used quantitative 3D microscopy analysis to show that cell wall growth is enhanced at both the inner and outer curves of the helix. Immunofluorescence revealed that MreB is most abundant at the inner curve, while CcmA is most abundant at the outer curve. The researchers also found that strains of H. pylori expressing variants of CcmA with altered polymerization properties lost their helical shape and associated positive Gaussian curvatures.

     The study suggests that MreB promotes cell wall synthesis at the inner curve of the helix, while CcmA promotes synthesis at the outer curve. This patterning of cell wall synthesis is necessary for maintaining the helical shape of H. pylori. The researchers propose a model where MreB and CcmA work together to promote cell wall synthesis at different regions of the helix, allowing H. pylori to maintain its unique shape.

     The study also found that H. pylori achieves a broader distribution of sidewall Gaussian curvature than other curved-rod bacteria, such as Campylobacter crescentus and Vibrio cholerae. The researchers used computational tools to demonstrate that H. pylori uses distinct mechanisms to achieve these curvatures.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms that generate the helical shape of H. pylori and highlights the importance of MreB and CcmA in defining zones of enhanced cell wall synthesis. The findings have implications for our understanding of bacterial cell shape and the development of new treatments for H. pylori infections.
summary
     The protein Cdc48 plays a crucial role in regulating mitochondrial dynamics by controlling the activity of two deubiquitylases (DUBs), Ubp12 and Ubp2. These DUBs have opposing effects on mitochondrial fusion, with Ubp12 promoting fusion and Ubp2 inhibiting it. Cdc48 integrates the activities of Ubp12 and Ubp2 by promoting the proteolysis of Ubp12, which in turn stabilizes pro-fusion ubiquitylation on the mitofusin Fzo1. In contrast, the loss of Ubp12 stabilizes Ubp2, leading to the removal of ubiquitin chains on Fzo1 and inhibition of fusion.

     The study reveals a new cascade of ubiquitylation events involving Cdc48, Ubp12, and Ubp2, which fine-tunes the fusogenic activity of Fzo1. This cascade ensures a tight control of Fzo1 ubiquitylation, allowing for mitochondrial fusion while preventing unnecessary ubiquitylation that targets Fzo1 for proteasomal turnover. The research also revises recent findings linking Ubp2 and Mdm30, a protein that catalyzes the formation of pro-fusion ubiquitin forms on Fzo1.

     The study shows that Ubp12 and Ubp2 cleave different ubiquitylated forms of Fzo1, with Ubp12 stabilizing Fzo1 and promoting fusion, and Ubp2 targeting Fzo1 for degradation and inhibiting fusion. Cdc48 negatively regulates Ubp12, which in turn negatively regulates Ubp2, explaining why these two DUBs exert opposite effects on their targets and on ubiquitin homeostasis. The study proposes that Ubp12 recognizes ubiquitylated chains on Fzo1 composed of a small number of ubiquitin moieties, which are too short to target proteasomal turnover, and that Ubp2 recognizes longer ubiquitin chains that target Fzo1 for degradation.

     Overall, the research provides new insights into the regulation of mitochondrial dynamics and the role of Cdc48 in integrating the activities of Ubp12 and Ubp2 to control the balance between activation and repression of mitochondrial fusion. The study highlights the complexity of ubiquitin-dependent regulation of mitochondrial fusion and the importance of Cdc48 in fine-tuning this process.
summary
     The study investigated whether vision is necessary for the development of the categorical organization of the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOTC), a region of the brain responsible for processing visual information. Researchers used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to examine VOTC responses to sound categories in sighted and early blind individuals, and visual categories in a separate group of sighted individuals. The results showed that VOTC reliably encodes sound categories in both sighted and blind people, using a representational structure and connectivity similar to that found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group, using a representational format closer to the one found in vision.

The study found that VOTC in blind individuals represents the categorical membership of sounds rather than their acoustic features. This suggests that sounds trigger categorical responses in the VOTC of congenitally blind and sighted people that partially match the topography and functional profile of the visual response, despite qualitative nuances in the categorical organization of VOTC between modalities and groups. The results demonstrate that a categorical organization similar to the one found in vision can emerge in VOTC in the absence of visual experience, and that such categorical response to sounds is actually enhanced and more stable in congenitally blind people.

The researchers used representational similarity analysis to show that categorical membership is the main factor that predicts the representational structure of sounds in VOTC in blind people, rather than lower-level acoustic attributes of sounds. The study also found that the categorical representation of auditory stimuli in VOTC of both blind and sighted individuals exhibits different qualitative nuances compared to the categorical organization generated by visual stimuli in sighted people. The results suggest that the overlap in categorical responses between groups may be due to crossmodal plasticity, where the occipital cortex represents visual attributes in sighted individuals and acoustic attributes in blind individuals.

Overall, the study provides evidence that VOTC can develop a categorical organization in response to sound categories, even in the absence of visual experience, and that this organization is more robust in blind individuals. The findings have implications for our understanding of the neural basis of categorical perception and the role of experience in shaping brain function.
Scientists have discovered a strain of the fungal pathogen Entomophthora muscae that manipulates the behavior of Drosophila melanogaster, a common fruit fly species, in the laboratory. This fungus, known as E. muscae 'Berkeley', causes infected flies to exhibit moribund behaviors, such as climbing upward, extending their proboscides, and raising their wings, which allows the fungus to launch infectious spores from their abdomens. The researchers found that E. muscae invades the nervous system of the fly, suggesting a direct means by which the fungus induces behavioral changes.

To study the E. muscae-D. melanogaster system, the researchers isolated and characterized the E. muscae 'Berkeley' strain and its impact on D. melanogaster behavior in the laboratory. They found that the invasion of the nervous system by E. muscae 'Berkeley' grants the fungus direct access to host neurons, which may be mechanistically important for achieving behavioral manipulation of the host fly. However, the researchers note that there are other possible ways E. muscae 'Berkeley' could alter host behavior.

The researchers propose four general mechanisms by which E. muscae is able to achieve behavioral manipulation: (1) direct manipulation of host neurons, (2) alteration of host hormone levels, (3) disruption of host immune response, and (4) manipulation of host behavior through chemical signals. To test these mechanisms, the researchers exposed house flies to E. muscae 'Berkeley'-infected D. melanogaster and observed that the house flies died and sporulated, indicating that they had been killed by the fungus.

To gain a comprehensive understanding of how E. muscae 'Berkeley' infection progresses in D. melanogaster at the molecular level, the researchers measured transcription changes in both host and fungus at 24-hour time points over 5 days. They found that early on, infected flies are phenotypically indistinguishable from uninfected animals, making it challenging to study the molecular basis of behavioral manipulation. The researchers believe that the E. muscae-D. melanogaster system will enable rapid progress in understanding how E. muscae manipulates host behavior, given the vast molecular toolkit available for D. melanogaster.
Researchers have generated transcriptome-wide RNA-binding profiles of 30 general RNA degradation factors in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae to better understand RNA processing and degradation in eukaryotic cells. Using photoactivatable ribonucleoside-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation (PAR-CLIP), the team mapped the binding locations of these factors, which are involved in mRNA deadenylation, decapping, exosome-mediated degradation, and nuclear and cytoplasmic RNA surveillance pathways.

The analysis revealed how these degradation factors vary in their binding specificities for different classes of RNAs and their preferred locations on RNA transcripts. Global comparisons with previously published profiles of other RNA-binding factors showed clusters of factors that co-occupy RNAs or co-localize on RNAs. The study found that most degradation factors bind intact mRNAs, while decapping factors are recruited only for mRNA degradation, consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step.

The results also suggest that decapping factors preferentially bind mRNAs with non-optimal codons, which are rapidly degraded due to inefficient translation. Furthermore, the nuclear surveillance machinery, including the complexes Nrd1/Nab3 and TRAMP4, targets aberrant nuclear RNAs and processes small nucleolar RNAs (snoRNAs). The study provides a rich resource for future research on eukaryotic RNA degradation pathways, mechanisms, and the integration of mRNA metabolism.

The findings are consistent with the canonical degradation pathway for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation. Modeling based on mRNA half-lives suggests that most degradation factors bind intact mRNAs, whereas decapping factors are recruited only for mRNA degradation. This is consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step in the degradation process. The study's results have implications for our understanding of how RNA degradation pathways are selected and how the RNA sequence influences this selection.
Scientists have discovered a new type of chromatin, called bivalent chromatin, that plays a crucial role in the rapid induction of camalexin biosynthesis genes in response to a pathogen signal in Arabidopsis plants. Camalexin is a potent defense compound that helps protect plants from bacterial and fungal infections. The researchers found that two epigenetic modifications, trimethylation of histone 3 lysine 27 (H3K27me3) and acetylation of histone 3 lysine 18 (H3K18ac), work together to form this bivalent chromatin. These modifications have opposite effects on gene expression, with H3K27me3 repressing gene expression and H3K18ac activating it.

The study used publicly available epigenomic data and in vivo chromatin modification mapping to identify the presence of H3K27me3 and H3K18ac on camalexin biosynthesis genes. The researchers then used genetics, molecular biology, and biochemistry to examine the role of H3K27me3-H3K18ac bivalent chromatin in the induction of camalexin biosynthesis genes. They found that mutants with reduced H3K27me3 or H3K18ac had altered induction kinetics of camalexin biosynthesis genes and metabolite accumulation.

The study suggests that the H3K27me3-H3K18ac bivalent chromatin, which the researchers named "kairostat," is essential for controlling the timely induction of gene expression in response to stress stimuli in plants. This bivalent chromatin maintains the proper timing of camalexin gene induction upon a pathogen signal. The researchers also found that H3K27me3 and H3K18ac co-localize on camalexin biosynthesis genes, forming a previously uncharacterized form of bivalent chromatin.

The study provides new evidence for how chemical defense mediated by camalexin is regulated at the epigenetic level. However, the researchers note that other known mechanisms regulating camalexin genes may also affect transcriptional kinetics and metabolite accumulation. Future studies could investigate whether transcription factors that directly bind to promoters of camalexin biosynthesis genes interact with proteins that deposit or remove both epigenetic marks forming bivalent chromatin upon stress signals. The discovery of H3K27me3-H3K18ac bivalent chromatin and its role on camalexin biosynthesis genes provides a molecular handle for future investigations.
