     The study investigated the role of EZH2, a histone methyltransferase, in neurogenic astrocytes of the adult mouse subventricular zone (SVZ). Astrocytes in the SVZ serve as neural stem cells (NSCs) throughout adult life, and EZH2 expression was found to be a distinguishing feature of these cells. The research showed that EZH2 is required for neurogenesis, independent of its role in SVZ NSC proliferation. This was demonstrated by rescuing cell proliferation in Ezh2-deleted SVZ NSCs through Ink4a/Arf-deficiency, but neurogenesis remained defective.

     Further investigation revealed that Olig2, a bHLH transcription factor, is a direct target of EZH2, and its repression is critical for neuronal differentiation. Additionally, EZH2 prevents the inappropriate activation of genes associated with non-SVZ neuronal subtypes. In the human brain, SVZ cells, including local astroglia, also express EZH2, correlating with postnatal neurogenesis.

     The study identified distinct and separable roles for EZH2 in the production of new neurons from a multipotent stem cell population. EZH2 directly repressed the Ink4a/Arf locus to enable SVZ NSC self-renewal. In the human brain, EZH2 was also expressed in astroglia and neuronal precursors of the early infant SVZ, suggesting a key role in postnatal human neurogenesis.

     The results indicate that EZH2 is a critical epigenetic determinant for the maintenance of multipotent astroglia, enabling both proliferation and cell fate specification of an adult stem cell population. The study provides novel insights into the complex role of EZH2 in normal development and gliomagenesis. Future studies may address whether re-expression of EZH2 in non-SVZ astrocytes can enable neurogenesis or their directed transdifferentiation into neurons in vivo for potential therapeutic purposes. Acute deletion of Ezh2 in SVZ NSCs resulted in decreased neurogenesis, and this deficiency in part related to reduced cell proliferation. Re-expression of Ezh2 in Ezh2-deleted, Ink4a/Arf-null SVZ NSCs rescued neurogenesis, indicating that EZH2 has a role in SVZ neurogenesis distinct from its function in Ink4a/Arf repression.
Summary
     Scientists have long known that viruses play a crucial role in shaping microbial communities and influencing ecosystem functions. However, studying the interactions between viruses and their hosts has been challenging due to the difficulties in cultivating certain microorganisms. A recent study used single-cell genomics to investigate the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in a marine oxygen minimum zone (OMZ). The researchers isolated 127 single-cell amplified genomes (SAGs) from SUP05 bacteria and identified 69 viral contigs representing five new genera within dsDNA Caudovirales and ssDNA Microviridae.

     The study found that approximately one-third of SUP05 bacteria were infected with viruses, with higher infection frequencies observed in areas with more severe oxygen deficiency. The researchers also discovered that Microviridae viruses were clonal, suggesting that they were recovered from bloom-terminating viruses. Furthermore, the study revealed systematic co-infection between dsDNA and ssDNA viruses, indicating previously unrecognized cooperation modes between these viruses.

     The researchers analyzed 186 microbial and viral metagenomes from the OMZ and found that SUP05 viruses persisted for years but remained endemic to the OMZ. Notably, the study identified virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase, which suggests that SUP05 viruses can reprogram their host's energy metabolism. This finding has significant implications for understanding the role of viruses in modulating biogeochemical cycling in climate-critical and expanding OMZs.

     The study used single-cell genomics to directly link SUP05 viruses and their hosts across defined gradients of oxygen and sulfide over a 3-year time interval in a model OMZ ecosystem. The researchers obtained a SUP05 single-cell genomic dataset spanning defined redox gradients in the Saanich Inlet water column and identified SUP05-associated viruses infecting SAGs. The resulting virus-host pairs were used to estimate viral diversity, activity, dispersion, and potential impact on SUP05 population dynamics and metabolic capacity.

     The study found that SUP05 Microviridae contigs were inconsistently detected due to methodological biases, so the researchers focused on dsDNA viral contigs. All four SUP05 Caudovirales contigs were absent from surface waters but repeatedly detected within and below the oxycline, consistent with SUP05 water column disposition. The study also found that putative SUP05 phage contained genes encoding reverse dissimilatory sulfite reductase A and C, positing a role for viruses in modulating the marine sulfur cycle. Overall, the study provides new insights into the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP05 bacteria and highlights the importance of viruses in modulating biogeochemical cycling in OMZs.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a protein called TTC26/DYF13 as a crucial component of the intraflagellar transport (IFT) system, which is responsible for assembling and maintaining cilia and flagella in cells. IFT is a highly conserved mechanism involving over 20 proteins, but the functions of individual IFT proteins are not well understood. Using live imaging and biochemical approaches, the researchers found that TTC26/DYF13 is an IFT complex B protein in mammalian cells and the green algae Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. They also discovered that knockdown of TTC26/DYF13 in zebrafish embryos or mutation of TTC26/DYF13 in C. reinhardtii resulted in short cilia with abnormal motility.

     Notably, the IFT particle assembly and speed were normal in the dyf13 mutant flagella, unlike in other IFT complex B mutants. However, proteomic and biochemical analyses revealed that a specific set of proteins involved in motility was depleted in the dyf13 mutant. These findings suggest that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting different subsets of cargo, providing a possible explanation for the complexity of the IFT machinery.

     Further analysis showed that TTC26/DYF13 is not required for ciliogenesis, the process of forming cilia, nor for the assembly or movement of the IFT particle itself. Instead, it plays a specialized role in transporting a specific set of motility-related ciliary proteins into cilia/flagella. This is in contrast to other IFT proteins, which are necessary for ciliogenesis. The researchers also found that the dyf13 mutant cells could assemble cilia/flagella, but they were shorter than control cilia/flagella.

     The study provides new insights into the role of TTC26/DYF13 in the IFT system and highlights the complexity of the IFT machinery. The findings suggest that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting different subsets of cargo, and that TTC26/DYF13 plays a critical role in transporting motility-related proteins into cilia/flagella. Further research is needed to clarify the role of TTC26/DYF13 in determining the length of cilia and flagella.
Summary
     Biomedical research has shown that the interaction between melanoma cells and lymphatic endothelial cells (LECs) plays a crucial role in the development of metastasis in melanoma, a type of skin cancer. Metastasis is the process by which cancer cells spread from the primary tumor site to other parts of the body, and it is often associated with poor clinical outcomes. In this study, researchers investigated the molecular mechanisms underlying the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs in the development of metastasis.

The study used a combination of in vitro and in vivo models to examine the effects of LECs on the behavior of melanoma cells. The results showed that the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs led to the activation of matrix-metalloproteinase-14 (MMP14), Notch3, and Î²1-integrin in the melanoma cells. This activation was associated with the development of an invasive sprouting phenotype in the melanoma cells, which is characterized by the ability of the cells to invade and spread through three-dimensional matrices.

The researchers found that MMP14 was necessary for the activation of Notch3 and Î²1-integrin in the melanoma cells, and that this activation was required for the development of the invasive sprouting phenotype. Furthermore, the study showed that the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs led to an increase in metastasis of melanoma xenografts in mice, which was dependent on MMP14 and Notch3.

The study also used a zebrafish xenograft model to investigate the role of LECs in the development of metastasis. The results showed that the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs led to an increase in metastasis of melanoma cells in the zebrafish, which was dependent on MMP14 and Notch3.

Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the interaction between melanoma cells and LECs in the development of metastasis. The findings suggest that the activation of MMP14, Notch3, and Î²1-integrin in melanoma cells is a key event in the development of metastasis, and that LECs play a crucial role in promoting the invasive sprouting phenotype of melanoma cells. These findings have important implications for the development of new therapeutic strategies for the treatment of melanoma.
Summary
     MicroRNA-9 (miR-9) is a noncoding RNA that plays a crucial role in regulating gene expression in the brain. While its function in neural progenitors has been well characterized, its role in dendritic and synaptic development has remained largely unknown. To investigate this, researchers developed a transgenic mouse line that allows for the conditional inactivation of miR-9 in a spatio-temporal-controlled manner. Using this novel approach, the study found that miR-9 controls dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in vivo.

     The researchers used a double reporter mouse line to characterize the activity pattern of miR-9 in different cell types in the brain. They found that inhibition of miR-9 results in aberrant dendritic growth in vivo, accompanied by a concomitant impairment in synaptic transmission. Furthermore, they demonstrated that miR-9-mediated downregulation of the transcriptional repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth.

     The study focused on the postmitotic stages of neuronal differentiation and revealed a critical role for miR-9 in the control of dendritic development in vivo. Other miRNAs have been shown to influence dendritic growth, suggesting that the expression of genes orchestrating this process is under complex post-transcriptional regulation. The researchers also found that miR-9/REST regulation might play other non-developmental roles in some mature neuronal populations.

     The study describes the first transgenic sponge mouse line that allows for conditional inactivation of an miRNA family in a spatio-temporal-controlled manner. Analysis of this miR-9-SpNestin-Cre mouse line reveals a prominent role for miR-9 in postmitotic neuronal differentiation as a regulator of dendritic development through repression of REST expression. The results indicate that effects found in miR-9-SpNestin-Cre mice are essentially cell-intrinsic and that miR-9 exerts its proactive function in modeling dendritic arbors as a key player of neuronal genetic programs controlling dendrite development. Furthermore, the study found that basal synaptic transmission was impaired in miR-9 sponge-expressing mice. Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of miR-9 in dendritic development and synaptic transmission in vivo.
Summary
     Researchers investigated the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) using resting state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data from a large sample of ASD toddlers and non-ASD comparison groups. They found that ASD-related functional hypoconnectivity exists between the default mode network (DMN) and visual and attention networks. Specifically, a subtype of ASD characterized by pronounced early social visual engagement difficulties, known as GeoPref ASD, was found to have marked DMN-occipito-temporal cortex (OTC) hypoconnectivity. This hypoconnectivity was also linked to increased severity of social-communication difficulties in GeoPref ASD individuals. The study suggests that early and pronounced social-visual circuit hypoconnectivity is a key underlying neurobiological feature of GeoPref ASD and may be critical for future social-communicative development. This finding has implications for early intervention and treatment targets in individuals with GeoPref ASD. The researchers used a simple eye-tracking test to identify the GeoPref ASD subtype, which is characterized by high levels of social or non-social visual preferences in early development. The study highlights the importance of heterogeneity in early social engagement in understanding functional connectivity differences in ASD toddlers. The results support the idea that ASD is characterized by functional hypoconnectivity between the DMN and visual circuits in primary visual cortex (PVC) and OTC as well as attention circuitry. The study's findings provide new insights into the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in ASD and may inform the development of targeted interventions for early ASD diagnosis and treatment.
biomedical summary
     Biologists have long recognized the importance of hematopoietic organs in maintaining homeostasis in coelomate animals. In Drosophila melanogaster, the lymph gland produces two types of blood cells: plasmatocytes and crystal cells. While plasmatocytes are known to proliferate and increase in number during larval development, the origin of crystal cells has remained unclear. Recent research has shown that new crystal cells in Drosophila larvae originate through a process of plasmatocyte transdifferentiation, which occurs in sessile hemocyte clusters. This process is contingent upon the integrity of these structures and relies on Notch-dependent signaling events.

     The study of hematopoietic organs and their function is crucial for understanding how different mature blood cells arise and how their numbers are controlled. In Drosophila, embryonic hematopoiesis produces two types of mature hemocytes: plasmatocytes and crystal cells. Plasmatocytes are phagocytic cells that are often functionally compared to vertebrate macrophages. The increase in circulating and sessile crystal cell numbers during Drosophila larval development has been a paradox, as no mature crystal cell has been seen dividing during larval stages, and crystal cells in the lymph gland do not enter circulation in homeostatic conditions.

     Research has shown that the hemocyte population found in circulation and in sessile patches expands throughout larval development. Plasmatocytes are mitotically active cells that expand during larval development by self-renewal. However, the origin of crystal cells has remained unclear. The study reveals that new crystal cells in Drosophila larvae originate through a process of plasmatocyte transdifferentiation, which occurs in sessile hemocyte clusters. This process is contingent upon the integrity of these structures and relies on Notch-dependent signaling events.

     The existence of a hematopoietic tissue in Drosophila that relies on structure-dependent signaling events to promote blood homeostasis creates a new paradigm for addressing outstanding questions in Drosophila hematopoiesis and establishing further parallels with vertebrate systems. The study of hematopoietic organs and their function is essential for understanding how different mature blood cells arise and how their numbers are controlled. The discovery of a hematopoietic tissue in Drosophila that relies on structure-dependent signaling events to promote blood homeostasis brings us a step closer to addressing these outstanding fundamental questions of Drosophila hematopoiesis.
summary
     Biologists have long been puzzled by the phenomenon of aneuploidy, where cells have an abnormal number of chromosomes, which is linked to various diseases but also facilitates organismal evolution. While laboratory strains are highly sensitive to aneuploidy, researchers have found that aneuploidy is surprisingly common in wild yeast isolates. These wild yeast strains show lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes, suggesting that they have developed a mechanism to buffer the deleterious effects of aneuploidy.

     To investigate this phenomenon, scientists generated diploid strain panels in which cells carried two, three, or four copies of the affected chromosomes. They found that gene-dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes, which means that the expression of these genes is adjusted in proportion to the number of copies. Genes subject to dosage compensation are under higher expression constraint in wild populations, but they show elevated rates of gene amplification, indicating that copy-number variation is buffered at these genes.

     The researchers also discovered that aneuploidy provides a clear ecological advantage to certain wild yeast strains, such as oak strain YPS1009, by amplifying a causal gene that escapes dosage compensation. This suggests that aneuploidy can provide a natural, but likely transient, route to rapid phenotypic evolution.

     The study found that chromosomal amplification is common in non-laboratory yeast strains, which are inherently tolerant of aneuploidy and display an active mode of gene-dosage compensation at the transcript level for specific classes of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation are buffered against copy-number variation and show elevated rates of gene amplification in natural isolates.

     The results provide new insight into how cells can tolerate duplication of toxic genes long enough for new phenotypes to evolve. The study shows that up to 40% of amplified genes in naturally aneuploid strains show lower-than-expected expression, which is in part due to heritable polymorphisms that down-regulate expression from amplified genes. The findings raise the possibility of natural genetic variation in dosage compensation and suggest that while the expression of dosage-compensated genes is controlled in proportion to gene copy, many genes escape dosage compensation in response to aneuploidy and can contribute significantly to phenotypic variation.
Summary
     Biologists have discovered a new type of photoreceptor in a flatworm called Maritigrella crozieri. This photoreceptor, called xenopsin, was previously thought to belong to a different group of light-sensitive proteins called GÎ±i-coupled ciliary opsins. However, research has shown that xenopsin actually diverged from ciliary opsins a long time ago, before the evolution of bilateral animals.

     The study found that xenopsin is expressed in two types of cells in the flatworm: in the eyes of the larva and in extraocular cells around the brain of the adult. These extraocular cells are unique because they contain hundreds of cilia, which are long, thin extensions of the cell membrane, inside a specialized compartment called a phaosome. This is similar to the structure of rod cells in the eyes of jawed vertebrates, which also enclose their cilia inside their own plasma membrane.

     The researchers used human cells to test whether xenopsin can drive phototransduction, the process by which light is converted into a signal that can be interpreted by the brain. They found that xenopsin can indeed drive phototransduction, primarily by coupling to a type of G protein called GÎ±i. This is similar to how c-opsin, another type of light-sensitive protein, works.

     The study also found that xenopsin is expressed in the cilia of cells that form the eyes of the flatworm, suggesting that the VxPx motif, a sequence of amino acids, is involved in transporting xenopsin to the cilia. The discovery of xenopsin+ ciliary cells in the eyes of protostome larvae challenges our current understanding of opsin and photoreceptor evolution, as it deviates from the typical pattern of extraocular ciliary photoreceptors and ocular rhabdomeric/microvillar photoreceptors found in other invertebrates.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the evolution of photoreceptors and the diversity of ciliary photoreceptors in metazoans. It also highlights the similarities between xenopsin and c-opsin, and provides molecular and functional evidence for the presence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how spatial and temporal cues specify neuronal diversity in neural progenitors. Drosophila (fruit fly) neuroblasts, which are individually identifiable and have known spatial and temporal transcription factors, provide a suitable model system to investigate this process. A recent study aimed to determine whether spatial and temporal factors act independently or sequentially in neuroblasts by examining the genomic binding sites of the temporal transcription factor Hunchback (Hb) in two neuroblasts (NB5-6 and NB7-4) that produce different progeny.

     The researchers used Targeted DamID to identify the genomic binding sites of Hb in NB5-6 and NB7-4. The results showed that Hb targets were different in each neuroblast, ruling out the independent specification model. Furthermore, each neuroblast had distinct open chromatin domains, which correlated with differential Hb-bound loci in each neuroblast. The study also found that the spatial factor Gsb/Pax3, expressed in NB5-6 but not NB7-4, had genomic binding sites correlated with open chromatin in NB5-6, but not NB7-4.

     The data support a model in which early-acting spatial factors like Gsb establish neuroblast-specific open chromatin domains, leading to neuroblast-specific temporal factor binding and the production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage. The study identified over 100 loci that are differentially bound by Hb in NB5-6 or NB7-4, which are excellent candidates for lineage-specific neuronal specification. This research contributes to our understanding of how spatial and temporal factors are integrated during neurogenesis and has implications for understanding the development of neuronal diversity in both Drosophila and mammals. The findings suggest that early-acting spatial factors play a crucial role in establishing neuroblast-specific open chromatin domains, which in turn influence the binding of temporal transcription factors and the production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage.
Scientists have long been interested in understanding how P2X receptors, a type of ion channel, open and close in response to extracellular ATP. These receptors play a crucial role in various physiological processes, including pain sensation and neurotransmission. Recent studies have provided insights into the molecular motions of the extracellular domain of P2X receptors upon agonist binding, but the mechanism of pore opening remains unclear.

    To gain a better understanding of this process, researchers used photo-switchable cross-linkers, or "molecular tweezers," to monitor the distance between specific residues in the transmembrane domain of the P2X2 receptor during activation. This approach allowed the scientists to create high-resolution models of the channel in both open and closed states. The results showed that the outer pore expansion is significantly reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure, and that the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices come closer during opening, likely through a hinge-bending motion.

    The study used a novel chemical-based method to explore the gating motion of the rat P2X2 receptor by cross-linking engineered cysteine residues in the transmembrane domain with photo-switchable azobenzene tweezers. The end-to-end distance of these tweezers can be controlled by light, allowing the researchers to monitor the distance between specific residues during activation. The data revealed that the TM2 helices in the P2X2 receptor contain a critical "hinge" that facilitates their vertical bending, allowing the inner and outer ends of adjacent TM2 helices to come closer during activation.

    The findings of this study provide new insights into the gating mechanism of P2X receptors and establish a versatile strategy applicable to other membrane proteins. The use of photo-switchable cross-linkers and molecular dynamics simulations allowed the researchers to create high-resolution models of the channel in both open and closed states, shedding light on the complex process of pore opening. These results have significant implications for our understanding of P2X receptor function and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for diseases related to these receptors.
summary
     Biomedical research has long assumed that animal cells divide using a uniform molecular mechanism during cytokinesis, the physical division of one cell into two. However, recent evidence suggests that cytokinetic regulation in individual cell types is more varied than previously thought. A study on the four-cell Caenorhabditis elegans embryo has identified cell-type-specific variation in the cytokinetic requirement for a robust forminCYK-1-dependent filamentous-actin (F-actin) cytoskeleton.

Using fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants and acute drug treatment, researchers found that one cell (P2) regulates its cytokinetic variation cell-intrinsically, while another cell (EMS) regulates its variation cell-extrinsically, dependent on both SrcSRC-1 signaling and direct contact with its neighbor cell, P2. This suggests that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms control cytokinetic variation in individual cell types and can protect against division failure when the contractile ring is weakened.

The study used thermogenetics, drug treatment, and embryo microdissection to probe the mechanisms of cell-type-based variation in the regulation of cytokinesis among individual blastomeres within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. The researchers found cell-type-specific differences in both the functional levels and temporal window of activity required for forminCYK-1 activity, but not myosin-IINMY-2 activity, during cytokinesis.

Inactivation of forminCYK-1 by thermal upshift to restrictive temperature at any time before mid-ring constriction resulted in cytokinesis failure 100% of the time in the one-cell embryo. However, in the four-cell embryo, cell-type-specific variation was observed in the molecular requirement for forminCYK-1, but not myosin-IINMY-2.

The study demonstrates that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic regulatory mechanisms contribute to cytokinetic diversity and promote robust cytokinesis when the contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. Each cell in the four-cell embryo has a unique cell identity and can be individually scored for contractile ring constriction during cytokinesis. The findings suggest that the mechanisms of cytokinetic variation are complex and involve both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic factors.
biomedical summary
     Biologists have discovered a new role for the flagellar sheath of certain bacteria in triggering an immune response in the host. The flagellar sheath is a membranous structure that encases the flagellar shaft of some bacteria, including human pathogens such as Vibrio cholerae. While the function of the sheath has been unknown, researchers have hypothesized that it may allow these bacteria to evade the host's immune response to the immunogenic flagellin subunit.

     Using the model symbiosis between Vibrio fischeri and the Hawaiian bobtail squid, scientists have found that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum promotes the release of lipopolysaccharide (LPS), a potent bacteria-derived immunogen. LPS is found in the flagellar sheath and is recognized by the host's immune system. This discovery suggests that the flagellar sheath does not evade the host's immune response, but rather triggers it.

     The researchers used the model symbiosis to study the effect of the flagellar sheath on host recognition. They found that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum in both V. fischeri and V. cholerae leads to the release of LPS, which is then recognized by the host's immune system. This is a new role for the flagellar sheath, which was previously thought to be of unknown function.

     The discovery of this new role for the flagellar sheath has implications for the study of bacterial pathogens with sheathed flagella. It also raises important biophysical questions about the function of the sheathed flagellum. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms underlying the initiation of the squid-vibrio symbiosis, including the composition of LPS released by V. fischeri during flagellar rotation, the squid receptors that recognize V. fischeri LPS, and the role of flagellin in activating host responses.

     This study provides new insights into the complex interactions between bacteria and their hosts, and highlights the importance of the flagellar sheath in mediating these interactions. The discovery of a new role for the flagellar sheath in triggering an immune response has significant implications for our understanding of bacterial virulence and the development of new treatments for bacterial infections.
Summary
     Genome instability is a phenomenon associated with mitotic errors and cancer, leading to deleterious rearrangements and genetic novelty. However, the mechanisms and evolutionary role of genome instability remain poorly understood. This study investigates extreme chromosomal restructuring during genome elimination in plants, a process resulting from the hybridization of Arabidopsis plants expressing different centromere histones H3. The researchers found that shattered chromosomes are formed from the genome of the haploid inducer, consistent with genomic catastrophes affecting a single, laggard chromosome compartmentalized within a micronucleus. Analysis of breakpoint junctions revealed that breaks are followed by repair through non-homologous end joining (NHEJ) or stalled fork repair.

The study also found that mutation of the NHEJ factor DNA Ligase 4 results in enhanced haploid recovery, suggesting that NHEJ plays an important role in the repair of the haploid inducer chromosomes that contribute to diploid and aneuploid progeny. The results indicate that unrepaired double-stranded DNA breaks increase elimination of the haploid inducer genome, similar to observations in mouse-human hybrid genome elimination. The researchers propose that missegregated chromosomes enter a degradative pathway initiated by endonucleolytic breaks, and occasionally, such chromosomes are rescued.

The study provides evidence for the occurrence of chromosome restructuring when diverged individuals hybridize, identifying a centromere-based mechanism for genomic instability. This phenomenon depends on chimeric CENH3, but a similar effect was observed when the haploid inducer strain expresses CENH3 of a close species. The results suggest that NHEJ contributes to the formation or persistence of aneuploid and diploid progeny, and that unrepaired double-stranded DNA breaks increase elimination of the haploid inducer genome. The study provides a tractable, natural system for investigating the causes and mechanisms of complex genomic rearrangements similar to those associated with several human disorders.

Scientists have long been interested in understanding how plants produce diverse metabolites to cope with environmental challenges. These metabolites are specialized chemicals that help plants adapt to their surroundings. However, the genetic factors that control the production of these metabolites and how they vary across different plant populations are not yet fully understood. A recent study investigated the genetic and environmental factors that influence the production of specialized metabolites in Arabidopsis thaliana, a small flowering plant commonly used in scientific research.

The study analyzed 797 natural Arabidopsis accessions from across Europe and found that the production of specialized metabolites is influenced by a combination of genetic, environmental, and demographic factors. The researchers used the Arabidopsis glucosinolate (GSL) pathway as a model to study the genetic and environmental factors that shape the production of these metabolites. The GSL pathway is a well-studied system that produces a range of specialized metabolites, including aliphatic glucosinolates.

The study found that the production of aliphatic glucosinolates varies significantly across different Arabidopsis accessions, and that this variation is influenced by a combination of genetic, environmental, and demographic factors. The researchers identified multiple genetic loci that contribute to the variation in aliphatic glucosinolate production, and found that these loci contain frequent independently generated alleles. This suggests that the production of specialized metabolites is shaped by multiple genetic and environmental factors, and that these factors interact with each other in complex ways.

The study also found that the geographic distribution of aliphatic glucosinolate-producing Arabidopsis accessions is structured by ecological and demographic pressures. This suggests that the production of specialized metabolites is influenced by the local environment and the demographic history of the plant population. The researchers propose that the intersection of environmental pressure, demography, and genomic complexity gives rise to the pattern of metabolic variation across a plant species.

Overall, the study provides new insights into the genetic and environmental factors that shape the production of specialized metabolites in Arabidopsis thaliana. The findings suggest that the production of these metabolites is influenced by a complex interplay of genetic, environmental, and demographic factors, and that these factors interact with each other in ways that are not yet fully understood. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms underlying the production of specialized metabolites and how they contribute to plant adaptation and evolution.
summary
     Biomedical research has traditionally viewed apoptosis, a process of programmed cell death, and its molecular mediators, caspases, as tumor suppressors. However, recent studies have suggested that under certain conditions, apoptotic caspases can promote tumor growth and proliferation. A new study in Drosophila (fruit flies) has uncovered a mechanism by which caspases can switch from tumor suppressors to tumor promoters.

     In the study, researchers used a well-studied neoplastic tumor model in Drosophila to investigate the role of caspases in tumor growth. They found that oncogenic mutations of the proto-oncogene Ras (RasV12) maintain tumorous cells in an "undead" state, where caspases are activated but do not induce cell death. Instead, caspases promote the generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS), which are highly reactive molecules that can damage cells.

     The ROS generated by caspases recruit and activate macrophage-like immune cells, which in turn signal back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic JNK signaling. JNK further amplifies caspase activity, creating a feedback amplification loop that promotes tumor growth and invasive behavior. The researchers found that interfering with this amplification loop significantly reduces the neoplastic behavior of these cells and improves organismal survival.

     The study reveals that caspases can adopt a tumor-promoting role under certain conditions, such as in the presence of oncogenic RasV12 in scrib mutant cells. This is in contrast to their traditional role as tumor suppressors. The researchers also found that the tumor-promoting function of apoptotic caspases is dependent on the generation of intra- and extracellular ROS, which are required for neoplastic behavior of scribâ/â RasV12 clones.

     The study provides new insights into the complex relationship between apoptosis, caspases, and tumor growth, and highlights the importance of ROS in promoting tumor growth and invasive behavior. The findings have implications for our understanding of cancer biology and may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for cancer treatment.
Biomedical research has shown that the way we perceive social interactions can influence our subjective experience of time. Specifically, studies have found that people tend to perceive social interactions as shorter in duration compared to non-social interactions. This phenomenon, known as temporal compression, is more pronounced in individuals with higher social cognitive competency. However, individuals with autistic-like tendencies exhibit a weaker temporal compression effect, suggesting that social proficiency plays a role in this phenomenon.

    Further investigation revealed that intranasal oxytocin administration can restore the temporal compression effect in socially less proficient individuals, whereas the administration of atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, diminishes the effect in socially proficient individuals. These findings suggest that oxytocin is involved in mediating time perception of social interaction, supporting its role in human social cognition.

    The study also highlighted the idiosyncrasy of subjective time for social interactions, which is tied to a stable personality trait â social proficiency. Autistic traits have been associated with reduced levels of oxytocin, and intranasal oxytocin administration has been shown to ameliorate these traits. The link between autistic-like tendency and subjective duration of social interactions raises the question of whether oxytocin plays a role in this phenomenon.

    Experiments were conducted to investigate the relationship between oxytocin and the temporal compression effect. The results showed that socially less proficient individuals, who have lower levels of endogenous oxytocin, are less susceptible to the temporal compression effect compared to socially proficient individuals. The administration of oxytocin promoted the social interaction-induced temporal compression effect in socially less proficient individuals, while the administration of atosiban diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals.

    These findings indicate that perceived time is not a faithful representation of physical time, but rather is highly idiosyncratic and ingrained with oneâs personality trait. The study provides evidence for the role of oxytocin in mediating time perception of social interaction, further supporting its involvement in human social cognition. The results have implications for our understanding of social cognition and the neural mechanisms underlying time perception.
Summary
     The interaction between the SARS-CoV-2 virus Spike protein receptor binding domain (RBD) and the ACE2 cell surface protein is essential for viral infection of cells. Mutations in the RBD are present in SARS-CoV-2 variants of concern (VOCs) that have emerged independently worldwide. This study investigated the effects of five common RBD mutations (K417N, K417T, N501Y, E484K, and S477N) and two common ACE2 mutations (S19P and K26R) on the RBD/ACE2 interaction.

The researchers analyzed the effects of individual RBD mutations and combinations found in new SARS-CoV-2 Alpha (B.1.1.7), Beta (B.1.351), and Gamma (P1) variants. The results showed that most of these mutations increased the affinity of the RBD/ACE2 interaction, with the exceptions being mutations K417N/T, which decreased the affinity. The N501Y and S477N mutations were found to enhance transmission primarily by enhancing binding, while the K417N/T mutations facilitate immune escape, and the E484K mutation enhances binding and immune escape.

The study also examined the effect on ACE2 binding of combinations of RBD mutations, including combinations present in VOC-202102â02, a subset of the Alpha lineage (N501Y) with the E484K mutation, and the Beta and Gamma variants. The results suggest that the increased affinity for SARS-CoV-2 Spike RBD of the K26R and S19P ACE2 mutants is less clear, but carriers of these ACE2 variants may be at greater risk of infection and/or severe disease.

The study highlights the importance of understanding the effects of RBD and ACE2 mutations on the Spike/ACE2 interaction to assess the extent to which RBD mutations have been selected because they enhance ACE2 binding or facilitate immune evasion. The findings provide insights into the mechanisms of SARS-CoV-2 transmission and disease severity, which can inform public health strategies to mitigate the spread of the virus.
Scientists have developed a large-scale map of genetic interactions in Drosophila cells, which reveals the complex relationships between genes and their effects on cellular processes. This map, generated using a combination of RNA interference and automated single-cell phenotyping, includes 1367 genes involved in key biological processes such as signaling, chromatin biology, cell cycle regulation, and protein turnover control. The researchers used this map to identify directional genetic interactions, which reflect regulatory relationships between genes, and found that these interactions can be used to reconstruct the sequence of protein activities during mitosis.

The study also revealed an unexpected link between chromatin remodeling and RAS signaling, with the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodeling complex interacting with the RAS pathway. This interaction was found to be conserved in human cancer cells, suggesting that it may play a role in cancer development. The researchers used a method that combines genetic interactions on multiple phenotypes to reveal directional relationships, which allowed them to reconstruct a detailed circuit diagram of regulatory, temporal, and causal relationships of complexes that function during the M-phase of the cell cycle.

The study provides a powerful approach for reconstructing directional regulatory networks and offers a resource for interpreting the functional consequences of genetic alterations. The map of genetic interactions can be used to identify regulatory relationships between genes and to predict the effects of mutations on cellular processes. The findings of this study have important implications for our understanding of the complex relationships between genes and their effects on cellular processes, and may lead to new insights into the development of cancer and other diseases.

The researchers used a combination of high-throughput imaging of single-cell phenotypes and modeling of gene-gene interactions to generate the map of genetic interactions. They selected 1367 genes implicated in key biological processes and quantitatively scored 21 phenotypes in cultured cells using automated imaging. The study demonstrates that directional genetic interactions can be identified at a large scale and mapped to other genomic data sets to identify regulatory relationships. The findings of this study provide a new resource for understanding the complex relationships between genes and their effects on cellular processes, and may lead to new insights into the development of cancer and other diseases.
summary
     Biologists have long been interested in understanding the genetic basis of heterostyly, a widespread floral adaptation that promotes outbreeding in plants. Heterostyly is characterized by the reciprocal arrangement of reproductive organs and incompatibility between different morphs. In the genus Primula, also known as primroses, heterostyly is controlled by the S-locus supergene, which determines the style-length dimorphism. However, the identities of the component genes remained unknown until now.

     Researchers have identified the CYP734A50 gene as the G locus that determines the style-length dimorphism in Primula. This gene encodes a putative brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme and is only present on the short-styled S-morph haplotype. CYP734A50 is specifically expressed in S-morph styles, and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. The gene arose by a duplication specific to the Primulaceae lineage and shows an accelerated rate of molecular evolution.

     The presence or absence of CYP734A50 strictly cosegregates with the morph type in all heterostylous species sampled across the Primula phylogeny. The gene is absent or inactivated in all seven cases of derived long homostyly. This strongly supports the conclusion that CYP734A50 is the G locus of the Primula heterostyly supergene that determines the style-length polymorphism.

     The discovery of CYP734A50 provides a mechanistic explanation for the Primula style-length dimorphism and sheds light on the evolution of the S-locus as a prime model for a complex plant supergene. The CYP734A50 gene is a rare example of an identified causal locus in a supergene underlying an adaptive polymorphism. This finding opens the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-locus, which is an iconic case of a complex supergene.

     Heterostyly evolved only once in the genus Primula, and the same causal genes should co-segregate with morph phenotypes in all heterostylous Primula species. The researchers sequenced style transcriptomes of the distantly related Primula species and found that CYP734A50 is absent or inactivated in all seven cases of derived long homostyly. This supports the conclusion that CYP734A50 is the G locus of the Primula heterostyly supergene that determines the style-length polymorphism.
summary
     Biomedical research has identified the role of kisspeptin (Kiss1) and neurokinin B (NKB) neurocircuits in pubertal development and fertility. Kisspeptin neurons in the hypothalamic arcuate nucleus (Kiss1ARH) co-express Kiss1, NKB, dynorphin, and glutamate, and are thought to provide an episodic, excitatory drive to gonadotropin-releasing hormone 1 (GnRH) neurons. Using optogenetics, whole-cell electrophysiology, molecular pharmacology, and single-cell RT-PCR in mice, researchers characterized the cellular basis for synchronized Kiss1ARH neuronal activity.

     High-frequency photostimulation of Kiss1ARH neurons was found to evoke the local release of excitatory (NKB) and inhibitory (dynorphin) neuropeptides, which synchronized the firing of Kiss1ARH neurons. This synchronized activity caused robust excitation of GnRH neurons through a synaptic mechanism involving glutamatergic input to preoptic Kiss1 neurons from Kiss1ARH neurons. The researchers propose that Kiss1ARH neurons play a dual role in driving episodic secretion of GnRH through the differential release of peptide and amino acid neurotransmitters to coordinate reproductive function.

     The study found that high-frequency photoactivation of Kiss1ARH neurons induced a NKB-mediated slow excitatory postsynaptic potential (EPSP), which was modulated by co-released dynorphin, and caused synchronization of Kiss1ARH neurons. Additionally, activation of Kiss1ARH neurons co-released glutamate, which excited rostral Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. The combination of synchronization and unified excitation of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons resulted in robust excitation of GnRH neurons.

     Kiss1ARH neurons communicate with Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons via the fast neurotransmitter glutamate, which stimulates burst-firing of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. Activation of these rostral Kiss1 neurons releases kisspeptin to robustly excite GnRH neurons via activation of the GPR54 signaling cascade, thereby stimulating the release of GnRH at the time of the preovulatory surge. Kisspeptin, GPR54, NKB, Tacr3, and GnRH are all required for normal fertility. The study suggests that Kiss1ARH neurons play a dual role in driving pulsatile (episodic) secretion of GnRH through kisspeptin release into the external zone of the median eminence, but also excite Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons via glutamate release to help generate the preovulatory surge of GnRH through a robust di-synaptic pathway.

     Biomedical research has shown that bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signalling plays a crucial role in maintaining the pluripotency of cells, which are capable of differentiating into various cell types. However, the mechanisms by which BMP controls differentiation are not well understood. A recent study has shed light on this process, revealing that BMP signalling suppresses differentiation by maintaining the expression of E-Cadherin (Cdh1), a protein involved in cell adhesion.

     The study found that the loss of Cdh1 is a limiting requirement for differentiation of pluripotent cells, and that experimental suppression of Cdh1 activity rescues the BMP-imposed block to differentiation. This suggests that Cdh1 plays a critical role in the earliest stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. Furthermore, the study showed that BMP acts prior to and independently of Cdh1 to prime pluripotent cells for mesoderm differentiation, thereby reinforcing the block to neural differentiation.

     BMP signalling is known to contribute to the maintenance of pluripotency, but the underlying mechanisms have remained unclear. The study proposes that BMP suppresses differentiation by maintaining Cdh1 expression, which interferes with efficient neural and mesodermal differentiation. Higher levels of BMP also impose a posterior identity that primes cells for mesoderm and is incompatible with a neural fate. This finding helps to explain why BMP can simultaneously protect pluripotency and promote mesoderm differentiation.

     The study also identified the BMP target gene Id1, which imposes a proximal posterior identity on undifferentiated cells, priming them towards mesoderm. This suggests that the ability of Id1 to block neural differentiation is not due to inhibition of a neural-inducing transcription factor or direct activation of mesoderm differentiation, but rather to a shift in the positional identity of pluripotent cells.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which BMP signalling controls differentiation, highlighting the importance of Cdh1 in maintaining pluripotency and the role of Id1 in priming cells for mesoderm differentiation. These findings contribute to our understanding of the complex events that drive the transition from pluripotency to lineage commitment.
Biomedical researchers have identified a novel mechanism that regulates the maturation of centrioles in quiescent cells. Centrioles are crucial components of the centrosome, a structure that plays a key role in cell division and cilia formation. In quiescent cells, the two centrioles are inherently asymmetric, differing in age, morphology, and function. However, the underlying mechanisms that establish and maintain these asymmetric properties during quiescence were previously unknown.

The study found that a protein called Cep120, which is associated with the daughter centriole, plays a critical inhibitory role in regulating centriole maturation. Depletion of Cep120 in quiescent mouse and human cells led to an accumulation of pericentriolar material (PCM) components, including pericentrin, Cdk5Rap2, ninein, and Cep170. This accumulation resulted in increased microtubule-nucleation activity at the centrosome, leading to aberrant dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, dispersal of centriolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling.

The researchers demonstrated that Cep120 helps maintain centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting the untimely maturation of the daughter centriole. Loss of Cep120 initiated a series of changes in protein localization and abundance that ultimately resulted in defective ciliary assembly and function. The study also showed that Cep120 regulates the asymmetric distribution of PCM proteins at the centrosome by residing on the outer wall of the centriolar microtubules.

The findings suggest that loss of Cep120 during quiescence is detrimental to ciliary assembly and function, likely due to aberrant CS-dependent trafficking of proteins to the centrosome. The researchers propose that dispersal of centriolar satellites upon Cep120 loss disrupts the formation and/or function of cilia during quiescence. The study establishes the importance of Cep120 in maintaining centrosome homeostasis and its relationship to primary cilium assembly and signaling. The results also uncover a potentially new type of molecular defect that may underlie the pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune Asphyxiating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joubert syndrome.

     Biomedical research has shown that communication between neurons and glial cells, specifically MÃ¼ller glial cells (MCs), plays a crucial role in the maturation of neural circuits in the developing central nervous system (CNS). A recent study investigated neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina, where it was found that retinal waves induce calcium transients in MCs. The study used a combination of whole-cell recordings and two-photon imaging to examine the spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways of neuron-glia interactions in the developing retina.

The results showed that retinal waves were correlated with calcium transients in a large proportion of MCs, indicating that spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks at this age. The study also found that the mechanism of interaction between neurons and MCs during cholinergic retinal waves is similar to previous findings in the visual cortex and hippocampus, where cholinergic neuron stimulation leads to astrocyte calcium transients via activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors.

Furthermore, the study demonstrated that MCs express multiple neurotransmitter receptor types throughout this period, allowing neuronal control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal synchronization shifts from being dependent on cholinergic to glutamatergic signaling. The proportion of MCs that responded to waves with calcium transients decreased significantly from postnatal day 9 (P9) to P11, despite the presence of retinal waves.

The study used an optical glutamate sensor to show that the decline in MC calcium transients is caused by a reduction in the amount of glutamate reaching MCs. This suggests that neurons and MCs exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation that is enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses. The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of neuron-glia interactions in the developing retina and highlight the importance of neurotransmitter spillover in enabling communication between neurons and glial cells.
summary
     Establishing the postmortem interval (PMI), the time elapsed since death, is crucial in death investigations. However, current methods, such as forensic entomology, are prone to errors and biases. Researchers have explored the potential of microbes to estimate PMI, as they undergo significant changes during decomposition. A recent study used a mouse model system to investigate the feasibility of using microbial communities to estimate PMI.

     The study involved 40 mouse corpses, which were decomposed on soil under controlled conditions for 48 days. Microbial communities, including bacteria, archaea, and microbial eukaryotes, were analyzed using high-throughput DNA sequencing. The results showed that postmortem microbial community changes were dramatic, measurable, and repeatable, allowing PMI to be estimated within approximately 3 days over 48 days.

     This study expands on previous research by Pechal et al. (2013), which demonstrated that skin and mouth bacterial communities followed a consistent trajectory of change during decomposition of three swine corpses over 5 days. The current study characterizes microbial community change in three external sites and one internal site in mouse corpses, providing a more comprehensive understanding of microbial ecology during decomposition.

     The study's findings suggest that microbial community data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PMI. The results provide a detailed understanding of the underlying microbial ecology of corpse decomposition and the feasibility of using microbes as evidence. The study's approach differs from previous work, which evaluated regression models by their ability to explain variation in all data points. Instead, the current study provides a proof-of-principle demonstration that microbial community ecology of decomposing corpses has potential to be developed into a complementary forensics tool for estimating PMI.

     The study's results have significant implications for the field of forensic science, as they provide a novel method for estimating PMI that avoids many of the limitations of existing techniques. The use of microbial communities as a "clock" to estimate PMI has the potential to improve the accuracy and reliability of death investigations. Further research is needed to validate and refine this approach, but the current study provides a promising foundation for the development of a microbial-based forensic tool.
Despite the vast diversity of body forms in nature, bilaterian animals share common sets of developmental genes that display conserved expression patterns in the embryo. One such set of genes is the Hox genes, which define different identities along the anterior-posterior axis. Hox proteins interact with TALE transcription factors to exert their function. The presence of Hox and TALE members in some, but not all, non-bilaterian phyla raises the question of how Hox-TALE interactions evolved to provide positional information.

     Research has shown that Hox-TALE networks emerged from an ancestral generic motif present in Hox and other related protein families. This ancestral regulatory module was later exploited for patterning functions in bilaterians. The network was effective as soon as different interaction modes could exist with duplicated Hox family members, allowing for diversifying patterning functions along the body axis. This original molecular system was subsequently co-opted by the various contexts of embryogenesis in different eumetazoan phyla for axis or tissue patterning.

     Hox genes are expressed along the anteroposterior axis of all bilaterian animals, providing positional information during embryogenesis. Given their important patterning roles, Hox genes are thought to have strongly contributed to morphological diversification of bilaterian organisms during evolution. Although these networks rely on intricate molecular properties, they originated from an ancestral generic mode of interaction that was kept in other homeobox gene families.

     The study found that developmental genes can display highly dissimilar expression patterns or be absent in non-bilaterian lineages, showing that the genetic mechanisms underlying embryonic development are not universal. Interestingly, Cnidaria is the only non-bilaterian phylum with a bona fide Hox repertoire, whereas others lack Hox genes. This raises the question of how the Hox gene family acquired its crucial axial patterning functions during metazoan evolution.

     In bilaterians, Hox patterning functions rely on the presence of PBC and Meis proteins, which are also present in non-bilaterian phyla. The study suggests that Hox-TALE networks constituted an ancestral regulatory module that was later exploited for patterning functions in bilaterians. This network was effective as soon as different interaction modes could exist with duplicated Hox family members, allowing for diversifying patterning functions along the body axis. Overall, the study describes how the molecular cues underlying the Hox-TALE patterning system in bilaterians were established stepwise during eukaryote evolution.
Using super-resolution microscopy, researchers examined how the size of fluorescent probes affects the tracking of AMPA receptors (AMPARs) at synapses. AMPARs are a type of glutamate receptor that plays a crucial role in synaptic transmission. The study aimed to investigate the dynamics of AMPARs within and outside of post-synaptic densities (PSDs) in rat neurons.

The researchers used different-sized fluorescent probes, including organic dyes, small quantum dots (sQDs), and large quantum dots (bQDs), to label AMPARs. They found that when using sQDs, approximately 90% of AMPARs were diffusing in confined nanodomains within PSDs, which remained stable for 15 minutes or longer. In contrast, less than 10% of sQDs-labeled AMPARs were extrasynaptic and highly mobile.

In contrast, when using bQDs, 5-10% of AMPARs were found within PSDs, while 90-95% were extrasynaptic. This suggests that bQDs may be too large to allow AMPARs to enter the synaptic cleft. The study's findings contradict the hypothesis that AMPAR entry is limited by the occupancy of open PSD "slots." Instead, the researchers propose that AMPARs rapidly enter stable nanodomains within PSDs, which have a lifetime of over 15 minutes, and do not accumulate in extrasynaptic membranes.

The study used super-resolution microscopy to track the dynamics of AMPARs in synapses for extended periods, up to 60 minutes. The results were consistent across different labeling techniques, including sparse labeling with sQDs and organic dyes on live neurons. The researchers observed that the localization of AMPARs is predominantly at synaptic regions, with one nanodomain per synapse, although multiple nanodomains can exist.

The study's findings have implications for understanding the regulation of synaptic transmission and the role of AMPARs in synaptic plasticity. The results suggest that the size of the fluorescent probe used to label AMPARs can significantly impact the observed dynamics of these receptors, highlighting the importance of careful consideration of probe size when studying synaptic receptors.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand the earliest response of central neurons to hypoxia, an increase in voltage-gated sodium current (INa). A recent study has identified the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier (SUMO) pathway as a key mediator of this response. The study found that hypoxia activates the SUMO pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CGN) and that SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels increases INa. The time-course for SUMOylation of single NaV1.2 channels at the cell surface and changes in INa coincide, and both are prevented by mutation of NaV1.2-Lys38 or application of a deSUMOylating enzyme. Within 40 seconds, hypoxia-induced linkage of SUMO1 to the channels is complete, shifting the voltage-dependence of channel activation so that depolarizing steps evoke larger sodium currents. The study suggests that the SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 are potential targets for neuroprotective interventions, given the recognized role of INa in hypoxic brain damage.

The study used rat cerebellar granule neurons (CGN) and CHO cells to investigate the response of NaV1.2 channels to hypoxia. The researchers found that hypoxia-induced SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the plasma membrane underlies the immediate increase in INa in response to acute hypoxia. The time-course of SUMOylation of Lys38 matches the increase in NaV1.2 currents observed when CHO cells are subjected to hypoxia. The study also found that hypoxia-mediated SUMOylation of NaV1.2 is observed in the presence of TTX, showing that Na+ flux is not a prerequisite for channel SUMOylation.

Consistent with tonic control of INa by the SUMO pathway, SUMO1 and SENP1 increased and decreased the current, respectively, under normoxic conditions. The response of INa to hypoxia was ablated by Âµ-Conotoxin-TIIIA (CnTX), a potent blocker of NaV1.2 channels. Supporting the implied mechanism, hypoxia was directly shown to increase the interaction of native SUMO1 and NaV1.2 channels. The study suggests that NaV1.2 channels and SUMO pathway enzymes may be valuable therapeutic targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons.

summary
     Biomedical researchers investigated the relationship between beta oscillations in the motor cortex and movement onset in three macaques performing a novel neurofeedback task. The study aimed to understand how changes in population neural activity, beta oscillations, and behavior are linked. The researchers recorded multi-scale neural activity from the motor cortex while the macaques performed the task, which involved bringing their beta oscillatory power to an instructed state and subsequently executing an arm reach.

     The results showed that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power exhibited significantly faster movement onset times than reaches preceded by an increase in beta power. Additionally, population neural activity was found to shift farther from a movement onset state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback-induced or naturally occurring during reaching tasks. This finding establishes a population neural basis for slowed movement onset following periods of beta oscillatory activity.

     Notably, this shift in neural population was also seen in naturally occurring beta oscillations during reaching tasks, suggesting that beta oscillations reflect a common signature of spiking patterns even in different task contexts. The study ties together existing works on behavioral correlates of beta oscillations with hypotheses of how the motor cortex encodes movement onset through the lens of population-level neural activity.

     The study's results support the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to neural patterns that slow the onset of new movements. The behavioral effect from neurofeedback-induced beta oscillations matches well with hypotheses claiming a movement-slowing role of beta oscillations during natural movements. However, individual unit responses during beta activity were found to be different across tasks.

     The researchers analyzed simultaneously recorded units and showed that they exhibited different activity during neurofeedback-induced beta oscillations compared with natural beta oscillations occurring during typical reaching tasks. Despite differences at an individual unit level, population-level analyses show that beta oscillations promote a consistent movement-slowing state during both the CO and NR tasks, matching what is observed behaviorally. This study provides new insights into the relationship between beta oscillations and movement onset, shedding light on the neural mechanisms underlying motor control.
summary
     TRAF2 is a protein that plays a crucial role in the regulation and homeostasis of immune cells. It is a component of the TNF superfamily signalling complexes and is essential for the proper functioning of the immune system. However, the role of TRAF2 in adult tissues is not well understood due to the fact that TRAF2-deficient mice die shortly after birth.

     Researchers have investigated the function of TRAF2 in keratinocytes, a type of skin cell, and found that its absence leads to the disruption of TNF-mediated NF-ÎºB and MAP kinase signalling pathways. This disruption causes epidermal hyperplasia and psoriatic skin inflammation in mice. The inflammation is driven by TNF, cell death, non-canonical NF-ÎºB, and the adaptive immune system, making it a clinically relevant model of psoriasis.

     The study found that TRAF2-deficient keratinocytes are sensitive to killing by TNF and have a delayed response to TNF-induced activation of NF-ÎºB and MAPK. The absence of TRAF2 also leads to the production of inflammatory cytokines via the non-canonical NF-ÎºB pathway, which ultimately generates a psoriasis-like phenotype in mice. This phenotype is characterized by the recruitment of inflammatory immune cells, including neutrophils and IFNÎ³+ T cells, to the skin.

     The researchers were able to reverse the inflammatory phenotype by simultaneously deleting both TNF and NF-ÎºB2 genes. This suggests that TRAF2 plays a crucial role in protecting keratinocytes from cell death and down-regulating inflammatory responses. The study also found that TRAF2-deficient keratinocytes do not need to be stimulated by bacteria to produce TNF, and TNF-induced death sets up a potentially vicious cycle of inflammation.

     In contrast, the absence of sphingosine kinase 1 (Sphk1), an enzyme involved in the generation of sphingosine-1-phosphate, does not disrupt TNF-mediated signalling pathways in keratinocytes. This suggests that TRAF2 has essential tissue-specific functions that do not overlap with those of Sphk1. Overall, the study highlights the importance of TRAF2 in regulating inflammation and cell death in keratinocytes and provides new insights into the pathogenesis of psoriasis.
summary
     Biomedical research has long recognized the importance of synaptic vesicles (SVs) in storing and secreting neurotransmitters at synapses. Following exocytosis, SV membranes are rapidly recaptured by endocytosis and recycled for the generation of new fusion-competent SVs. Clathrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) has been proposed to play a key role in this endocytic traffic. However, a recent study has shed light on an alternative pathway of SV recycling that bypasses the requirement for clathrin and dynamin 1/3.

Using synapses of dynamin 1 and 3 double knock-out neurons, where CME is dramatically impaired, researchers found that massive formation of bulk endosomes was not defective, but rather enhanced, in the absence of dynamin 1 and 3. The subsequent conversion of bulk endosomes into SVs was not accompanied by the accumulation of clathrin-coated buds on their surface and proceeded even after further clathrin knock-down, suggesting its independence of clathrin.

These findings support the existence of a pathway for SV reformation that operates during intense synaptic activity and bypasses the requirement for clathrin and dynamin 1/3. This pathway involves bulk endocytosis of SV membranes leading to the formation of large endocytic vacuoles, which then disappear in parallel with the reappearance of new SVs. The study's results demonstrate that neither dynamin 1 nor dynamin 3 is required for bulk endocytosis and that their absence delays, but does not abolish, SV reformation from internal membranes.

Ultrastructural analysis of the time course of SV recovery from bulk endosomes in the presence or near depletion of clathrin revealed that the conversion of bulk endosomes into SVs proceeded independently of clathrin. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of the mechanisms underlying synaptic vesicle recycling and suggest that multiple pathways may exist for the reformation of SVs during intense synaptic activity. Further research is needed to fully elucidate the molecular mechanisms underlying this alternative pathway of SV recycling.
biomedical researchers conducted a clinical study to identify biomarkers of glucocorticoid (GC) action in humans. Glucocorticoids are commonly prescribed medications, but there is currently no reliable way to measure their effects on the body. The study involved 10 participants with primary adrenal insufficiency, a condition where the adrenal glands do not produce enough glucocorticoids. The participants were admitted to a clinic and underwent a randomized crossover trial, where they received either a normal dose of glucocorticoids or no glucocorticoids for a period of time. The researchers collected blood and tissue samples from the participants before, during, and after the trial to analyze the effects of glucocorticoids on their bodies.

     The researchers used a multi-omic approach, combining data from gene expression in blood cells, adipose tissue, circulating microRNAs, and metabolites to identify potential biomarkers of glucocorticoid action. They found that a specific transcriptomic profile in both blood cells and adipose tissue was predictive of glucocorticoid exposure. Additionally, they identified a microRNA called miR-122-5p that was correlated with genes and metabolites regulated by glucocorticoids. This microRNA was also found to be associated with glucocorticoid exposure in independent studies with varying levels of glucocorticoid exposure.

     The researchers validated their findings by comparing the results to previously published data from cellular assays and found that the transcriptomic profile and miR-122-5p were consistently associated with glucocorticoid exposure. Two participants did not show the expected response to glucocorticoids, which may be related to their individual characteristics, such as disease duration and body mass index. The study suggests that miR-122-5p may be a potential biomarker for glucocorticoid action and could be used to individualize and optimize glucocorticoid therapy. The findings also provide insights into the effects of unphysiological glucocorticoid exposure on disease etiology, such as cardiovascular disease and obesity. The study was funded by several organizations, including the Swedish Research Council and the Wellcome Trust.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids. Amino acids are essential activators of mTORC1, a complex containing RAG GTPases, RAGULATOR, and the vacuolar ATPase. The sensing of amino acids triggers the translocation of mTORC1 to lysosomes, a crucial step for its activation. Live imaging of the mTORC1 component RAPTOR and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of di-leucine methyl ester revealed that translocation to lysosomes is a transient event, occurring within 2 minutes of amino acid addition and peaking within 5 minutes. This translocation is temporally coupled with the appearance of fluorescent leucine in lysosomes and is sustained compared to amino acid stimulation. The study also found that Sestrin2 and the vacuolar ATPase are negative and positive regulators of mTORC1 activity, respectively. Notably, phosphorylation of canonical mTORC1 targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, suggesting a dynamic and transient passage of mTORC1 from the lysosomal surface before targeting its substrates elsewhere. The research used HAP-1 cells and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of leucine to image RAPTOR translocation in response to this compound. Leucine has a prominent role in mTORC1 activation, and a leucine methyl ester that enters cells and accumulates in lysosomes can activate mTORC1 on its own. The study suggests that the lysosome is a crucial locus for mTOR activation, but the dynamics of this process and how mTORC1 activity is connected to lysosomal residence may be more complicated than current models. The research provides a dynamic view of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids, supporting the idea that amino acids ultimately activate mTORC1 in the cell interior, and that more than one type of sensing (and amino acid) is involved. Sestrin1/2 proteins sense leucine, while levels of arginine entering the lysosomes via transporter SLC38A9 are also important for mTORC1 activation.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered that modifying the glycan structure of an antigen, such as Ovalbumin (OVA), can alter its intracellular routing in dendritic cells and promote the priming of T cells. Specifically, the addition of the LewisX (LeX) glycan structure to OVA re-directs it to the C-type lectin receptor MGL1. This modification enhances the cross-presentation of OVA to CD8+ T cells and favors the differentiation of naive CD4+ T cells into Th1 cells, which produce interferon-gamma (IFN-Î³) and tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-Î±).

     The study found that the LeX-modified OVA (OVA-LeX) is taken up by dendritic cells and stored in Rab11+LAMP1+ compartments, where it is presented to CD8+ T cells without the need for TAP-transporters or Cathepsin-S. This cross-presentation pathway is independent of Toll-like receptor (TLR) signaling and can occur at low antigen doses. In contrast, the cross-presentation of native OVA requires high antigen doses and TLR stimuli.

     The researchers used MGL1-deficient mice to demonstrate that MGL1 is the primary lectin involved in the cross-presentation of OVA-LeX. Immunization of MGL1-deficient mice with OVA-LeX did not enhance the frequency of OVA-specific CD8+ effector T cells, whereas wild-type mice immunized with OVA-LeX showed increased CD8+ T cell responses.

     The study suggests that controlled neo-glycosylation of antigens can influence the intracellular routing of antigens, the nature, and strength of immune responses. The findings have implications for optimizing current vaccination strategies, as the conjugation of LeX glycans to antigens may enhance the efficacy of vaccines by promoting CD8+ T cell priming and Th1 skewing of CD4+ T cells.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have discovered a novel mechanism by which certain bacteria, Burkholderia thailandensis, control the growth of mutants that are unable to communicate with each other through a process called quorum sensing. Quorum sensing is a system used by many bacteria to regulate gene expression based on the density of their population. In B. thailandensis, quorum sensing is mediated by acyl-homoserine lactone (AHL) signals, which are produced by the LuxI family of signal synthases. These signals can diffuse between cells and trigger the expression of hundreds of genes.

     The researchers found that one of the genes regulated by quorum sensing in B. thailandensis is involved in the production of type VI secretion system (T6SS) toxins and immunity proteins. T6SS is a cell-contact-dependent system that allows bacteria to kill or inhibit the growth of neighboring cells. The study showed that when B. thailandensis cells are able to communicate with each other through quorum sensing, they produce T6SS toxins and immunity proteins that inhibit the growth of mutants that are unable to communicate.

     The researchers also found that when B. thailandensis cells are grown in liquid broth, the quorum-sensing mutants have a significant growth advantage over the wild-type cells. However, when the cells are grown on an agar surface, the wild-type cells are able to outcompete the quorum-sensing mutants. This is because the T6SS toxins and immunity proteins are only effective when the cells are in close contact with each other, which is more likely to occur on an agar surface.

     The study suggests that quorum sensing plays a crucial role in controlling the growth of quorum-sensing mutants in B. thailandensis populations. By producing T6SS toxins and immunity proteins, quorum-sensing competent cells are able to "police" the population and prevent the growth of mutants that are unable to communicate. This mechanism is thought to be important for maintaining the health and stability of bacterial populations. The study provides new insights into the complex interactions between bacteria and their environment, and highlights the importance of quorum sensing in regulating bacterial behavior.
