summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a new type of post-translational modification of RNA polymerase II (RNAPII) that plays a crucial role in regulating gene expression in mammalian cells. Specifically, they found that certain lysine residues in the C-terminal domain (CTD) of RNAPII can be mono- or di-methylated (K7me1 and K7me2). These modifications occur during the earliest stages of transcription, preceding or coinciding with the phosphorylation of serine residues at positions 5 and 7 in the CTD.

     The study used novel antibodies to detect K7me1 and K7me2 and found that these modifications are present at the promoters of genes that are actively transcribed into mature RNA. However, the presence of K7me1 and K7me2 is associated with less productive transcription, whereas acetylation of the same lysine residues (K7ac) is linked to higher levels of gene expression. The researchers also observed that the extent of productive transcription at active genes is modulated by the balance between K7 methylation and acetylation.

     The study used biochemical and genome-wide approaches to investigate the role of K7 methylation in transcription. They found that K7me1 and K7me2 are detected primarily in the hypophosphorylated forms of RNAPII, suggesting that these modifications are associated with early stages of the transcription cycle. In contrast, K7ac is enriched at the highest expressed genes.

     The researchers also explored the functional significance of K7 methylation by mutating the lysine residues in the CTD of RNAPII. They found that these mutations are compatible with the viability of mouse fibroblasts, suggesting that global serine phosphorylation and RNAPII localization are independent of the presence or absence of K7 residues.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the regulation of gene expression in mammalian cells and highlights the importance of post-translational modifications in modulating the activity of RNAPII. The findings suggest that the balance between K7 methylation and acetylation plays a critical role in regulating the extent of productive transcription at active genes.
summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the mechanisms governing the quiescence of skeletal muscle satellite cells (SCs), which are crucial for muscle repair and regeneration. Satellite cells reside in a quiescent state in their niche, but upon muscle injury, they exit quiescence, proliferate, and self-renew to replenish the SC population. To understand the regulatory mechanisms involved in maintaining SC quiescence, researchers identified gene transcripts that were differentially expressed during SC activation following muscle injury. The study found that transcripts encoding RNA binding proteins, including the mRNA decay factor Tristetraprolin (TTP), were among the most significantly changed.

TTP promotes the decay of MyoD mRNA, which encodes a transcriptional regulator of myogenic commitment. Upon SC activation, p38Î±/Î² MAPK phosphorylates MAPKAP2 and inactivates TTP, stabilizing MyoD mRNA. This leads to the accumulation of MyoD, differentiation, and cell fusion into myofibers. Satellite cell-specific knockdown of TTP precociously activates SCs in vivo, enabling MyoD accumulation, differentiation, and cell fusion into myofibers. The regulation of mRNAs by TTP appears to function as one of several critical post-transcriptional regulatory mechanisms controlling SC homeostasis.

The study proposes that TTP may be involved in down-regulating MyoD mRNA and other TTP-target transcripts expressed by myoblasts, during the initial formation of a quiescent SC population or in reacquisition of quiescence following an injury. The rapid p38Î±/Î² MAPK-dependent phosphorylation (inactivation) of TTP upon SC activation suggests that TTP plays a role in maintaining SC quiescence. Furthermore, TTP inactivation preceding MyoD up-regulation is consistent with the hypothesis that TTP actively suppresses pro-myogenic mRNA targets.

The research highlights the importance of post-transcriptional regulation of RNA in skeletal muscle homeostasis, with transcripts encoding proteins that directly regulate mRNAs, including mRNA binding proteins involved in skeletal muscle diseases, being highly represented. The study suggests that TTP may be required for SC quiescence, as it is present in quiescent SCs, rapidly inhibited by p38Î±/Î² MAPK signaling within minutes of SC activation, and reduces the half-life of MyoD mRNA. Loss of TTP from skeletal muscle tissue should precociously activate SCs, promoting inappropriate MyoD expression and aberrant cell cycle entry.
summary
     Infants with defects in the interleukin 10 receptor (IL10R) are prone to developing very early onset inflammatory bowel disease. Researchers investigated whether IL10R regulates lamina propria macrophage function during infant development in mice and whether macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling is required to prevent colitis in infancy. The study found that signs of colitis were absent in IL10R-deficient mice during the first two weeks of life, but intestinal inflammation and macrophage dysfunction began during the third week of life, concomitant with weaning and changes in the intestinal microbiota. Macrophage depletion with clodronate inhibited the development of colitis, while the absence of IL10R specifically on macrophages sensitized infant mice to the development of colitis. These results indicate that IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function during the early postnatal period is crucial for preventing the development of murine colitis.

The study used detailed time-series experiments to evaluate histology, inflammatory gene expression, cellular composition of the lamina propria, and luminal bacterial communities from birth to 14 weeks of age in IL10R-deficient infant mice and their littermate controls. The results suggest that IL10R does not directly regulate the microbial ecology during infant development. Instead, the study found that macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling is required to prevent colitis in infancy. The requirement for macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling develops during a critical developmental transition, which has important implications for understanding factors that initiate colitis in infants with genetic deficiencies in IL10R signaling.

Previous studies have shown that the IL10/IL10R pathway is essential for intestinal mucosal homeostasis and prevention of intestinal inflammation in infancy. Modeling infantile-onset colitis in mice that lack functional IL10R signaling could lead to significant advances in our understanding of the etiopathogenesis of these diseases. The study's findings highlight the importance of IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function during the early postnatal period in preventing the development of murine colitis.
Summary
     Biologists have identified a protein called SIMR-1 in the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans that plays a crucial role in the regulation of transposons and other germline genes. Transposons are mobile genetic elements that can jump from one location to another in the genome, and their regulation is essential for maintaining genome stability. SIMR-1 is a Tudor domain protein that acts downstream of the piRNA (piwi-interacting RNA) pathway and upstream of the mutator complex, which is responsible for synthesizing secondary siRNAs (small interfering RNAs) from piRNA-targeted mRNAs.

The researchers found that SIMR-1 localizes to distinct subcellular foci adjacent to P granules and Mutator foci, which are phase-separated condensates that are the sites of piRNA-dependent mRNA recognition and mutator complex-dependent siRNA amplification, respectively. This suggests that multiple perinuclear condensates are involved in organizing the piRNA pathway and promoting mRNA regulation by the mutator complex.

SIMR-1 is thought to function similarly to Krimper, a protein that coordinates Ago3 and Aubergine during ping-pong piRNA biogenesis in Drosophila. The researchers propose that SIMR-1 bridges the gap between the primary and secondary phases of the C. elegans piRNA silencing pathway.

The study also identifies SIMR-1 as a factor that acts downstream of PRG-1 to mediate the production of secondary siRNAs by the mutator complex. Furthermore, the researchers suggest that multiple perinuclear condensates, including P granules, Z granules, and Mutator foci, are involved in the regulation of mRNAs by the piRNA pathway and mutator complex.

In C. elegans, a small RNA amplification pathway dependent on the mutator complex synthesizes secondary downstream siRNAs from piRNA-targeted mRNAs to trigger robust and heritable silencing. Several proteins, including GLH-1, DDX-19, LAF-1, MUT-14, and VBH-1, localize at or near P granules and could potentially serve a function similar to RDE-12 in the shuttling of piRNA-targeted mRNAs into SIMR-1 foci and ultimately to Mutator foci. Overall, the study provides new insights into the regulation of transposons and other germline genes in C. elegans and highlights the importance of multiple perinuclear condensates in organizing the piRNA pathway and promoting mRNA regulation.

     Bi
Biomedical researchers have investigated the process of clathrin-mediated endocytosis, where cells internalize substances by forming vesicles from the cell membrane. A key component of this process is the actin cytoskeleton, a dynamic network of protein filaments that generates forces to shape the membrane. Using a combination of experiments and computer simulations, the study reveals how the actin cytoskeleton self-organizes and adapts to internalize endocytic pits against the tension of the cell membrane.

The researchers developed a multiscale model that integrates membrane mechanics and actin polymerization to simulate the process of endocytosis. They found that a minimal branched actin network, composed of actin, the Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein, is sufficient to generate sufficient force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. The actin network self-organizes into a radial branched array with growing ends oriented towards the base of the pit, and long actin filaments bend between attachment sites in the coat and the base of the pit.

Elastic energy stored in bent filaments contributes to endocytic internalization, and elevated membrane tension directs more growing filaments towards the base of the pit, increasing actin nucleation and bending for increased force production. The study used a newly developed molecule-counting method to determine that approximately 200 Arp2/3 complexes assemble at sites of clathrin-mediated endocytosis in human cells.

The researchers also used cryo-electron tomography to confirm the presence of bent filaments in the actin network. They found that spatially constrained actin filament assembly utilizes an adaptive mechanism enabling endocytosis under varying physical constraints. In cells with low membrane tension, endocytosis can occur even with low Arp2/3 complex activity, whereas in cells with high membrane tension, more Arp2/3 complexes are required for robust internalization.

Overall, the study provides new insights into the principles of self-organization and load adaptation by the actin cytoskeleton during clathrin-mediated endocytosis. The findings suggest that the actin network can adapt to changing loads by self-organizing into a radial branched array and utilizing elastic energy stored in bent filaments to generate sufficient force for endocytic internalization.
Summary
     Macropinocytosis is a cellular process that allows cells to take up large amounts of extracellular fluid into large vesicles called macropinosomes. The formation of macropinosomes depends on the creation of a ring of protrusive actin beneath the plasma membrane, which develops into the macropinocytic cup. Research has shown that in Dictyostelium cells, macropinocytic cups are formed around patches of active Ras and phosphatidylinositol 3-phosphate ( PIP3 ). These patches are associated with a ring of active SCAR/WAVE at their periphery, which drives the formation of a hollow ring of actin to extend the walls of the macropinocytic cup.

     The study used lattice light sheet microscopy to examine the spatial and temporal control of actin regulators and signalling molecules during macropinocytosis. The results suggest that macropinocytic cups form from self-organizing patches of active Ras/PIP3, which recruit a ring of actin nucleators to their periphery. This process does not depend on the enclosing F-actin ring, and the patches become enlarged when the RasGAP NF1 is mutated, indicating that Ras plays an instructive role in the formation of macropinocytic cups.

     Macropinosomes develop from cup-shaped projections of the plasma membrane, whose walls are driven outwards by actin polymerization. The central patch of activated Ras and PIP3 is associated with a ring of active SCAR at its edge, which drives the formation of a hollow ring of F-actin to extend the walls of the macropinocytic cup. New macropinocytic cups predominantly form by splitting from existing ones, and the F-actin in de novo macropinosome cups is closely associated with signalling molecules, such as active Ras.

     The study also found that circular ruffles can persist on the cell surface for prolonged periods before either closing successfully or regressing back into the cell body. The closure of the cup can be abrupt and often involves the inward collapse of the rim. The research suggests that Ras activity stimulates macropinocytosis in both mammalian and Dictyostelium cells, and macropinocytic cups are associated with an intense domain of PIP3, which is essential for their function. The study proposes a new hypothesis for the formation of cups from the plasma membrane, where self-organizing patches of active Ras/PIP3 recruit a ring of actin nucleators to their periphery.
summary
     The cohesin ring is a crucial protein complex that holds together newly replicated sister chromatids in eukaryotic cells until their separation at anaphase. A separate complex, Scc2NIPBL/Scc4Mau2 (Scc2/4), is responsible for loading cohesin onto DNA and determining its localization across the genome. Proper cohesin loading is essential for cell division, and defects in this process can lead to chromosome missegregation and aberrant transcriptional regulation, resulting in severe developmental defects in multicellular organisms.

     Researchers have determined the crystal structure of the interaction between Scc2 and Scc4, revealing that Scc4 is a TPR array that envelops an extended Scc2 peptide. Using budding yeast, they demonstrated that a conserved patch on the surface of Scc4 is required to recruit Scc2/4 to centromeres and build pericentromeric cohesion. This finding shows that Scc4 plays a key role in determining the localization of cohesin loading and establishes a molecular basis for Scc2/4 recruitment to centromeres.

     The study also shows that Scc4 targets cohesin loading to a specific genomic locus and that this function is separable from its essential role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome. The researchers used separation of function alleles to uncouple cohesin loading from cohesin targeting to centromeres, demonstrating that cohesin targeting in yeast depends on Scc4. This finding is consistent with unpublished genetic evidence for an Scc4-dependent cohesin localization pathway.

     Further experiments revealed that perturbation of the conserved patch on Scc4 eliminates centromeric localization of Scc2 in mitotic cells and reduces association of the cohesin subunit, Scc1, with the centromere and pericentromere, but not with chromosome arms. This suggests that the Scc4-dependent cohesin targeting pathway is a general feature of the control of cohesin loading, conserved across diverse eukaryotes. The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying cohesin loading and localization, which is essential for accurate chromosome segregation during cell division.

Summary
     Scientists have long known that ADAR proteins play a crucial role in altering gene expression by converting adenosine to inosine in RNA. This process, known as A-to-I editing, is essential for proper neuronal function in all animals studied to date. In the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans, the loss of ADARs leads to reduced chemotaxis, or the ability to move towards chemical stimuli. However, the specific targets of ADARs responsible for this phenotype have remained unknown.

     To identify critical neural ADAR targets in C. elegans, researchers performed an unbiased assessment of the effects of ADR-2, the only A-to-I editing enzyme in C. elegans, on the neural transcriptome. Using publicly available software, SAILOR, they identified 7361 A-to-I editing events across the neural transcriptome. By intersecting the neural editome with gene expression changes associated with ADR-2, the researchers revealed an edited mRNA, clec-41, whose neural expression is dependent on deamination.

     Further investigation showed that restoring clec-41 expression in ADR-2 deficient neural cells rescued the chemotaxis defect, providing the first evidence that neuronal phenotypes of ADAR mutants can be caused by altered gene expression. This study is the first to link a phenotype associated with loss of C. elegans ADAR enzymes to editing-dependent regulation of a specific gene.

     The study also provided the first analysis of the tissue-specific role of ADR-2, both in A-to-I editing and in regulating neural gene expression in C. elegans. Combining neural cell isolation with RNA-sequencing and editing site detection with SAILOR, the researchers identified over 7300 A-to-I editing sites in the C. elegans neural transcriptome, including 104 novel edited genes. These findings suggest that ADR-2 plays a critical role in regulating neural gene expression in C. elegans, and that altered gene expression, rather than recoding events, is responsible for the aberrant behavioral phenotypes caused by lack of C. elegans ADAR enzymes.
biomedical summary
     The Influenza A virus (IAV) employs a common strategy called host shutoff to repress cellular mRNA translation and promote the efficient translation of viral mRNAs. Using RNA-sequencing and ribosome profiling, researchers investigated the mechanisms behind IAV-induced host shutoff. The results show that viral transcripts are not preferentially translated, and instead, the decline in cellular protein synthesis is primarily due to the viral takeover of the mRNA pool.

The analysis reveals that the translation efficiency of viral genes falls within the general range of host gene translation, indicating that direct co-opting of the translation machinery does not play a dominant role in cellular gene shutoff. Instead, host shutoff stems from the viral dominance over the mRNA pool. At 8 hours post-infection (hpi), viral mRNAs take over 53.8% of the translation activity in the cells, while 57.3% of the mRNAs in the cells are viral.

Interestingly, the study found that short transcripts are less affected by IAV infection, and these mRNAs are enriched in cell maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Furthermore, the continuous oxidative phosphorylation activity is essential for viral propagation. The results suggest that IAV-induced shutoff facilitates the translation of genes with important housekeeping functions.

The findings contradict previous suggestions that IAV employs mechanisms such as cap-snatching, inhibition of cellular pre-mRNAs polyadenylation, and PA-X endonuclease activity to induce host shutoff. Instead, the study's systematic and unbiased analysis reveals that viral mRNAs are not preferentially translated compared to their host counterparts, and the extensive translation of viral proteins is the result of viral takeover of the mRNA pool in the cell. These results are consistent with the notion that NS1 is not required for the shutoff of host cell protein production. Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of IAV-induced host shutoff and highlights the importance of oxidative phosphorylation in viral propagation.
Biomedical researchers have long understood the importance of signaling protein gradients in guiding tissue development, but the mechanisms behind their formation have remained unclear. A recent study in Drosophila (fruit flies) has shed light on how these gradients are generated and maintained. The study focused on the Branchless (Bnl) protein, a member of the fibroblast growth factor (FGF) family, which plays a crucial role in the development of tracheal epithelial tubes in Drosophila.

Traditionally, it was thought that paracrine signals like Bnl diffuse through the extracellular space to form a gradient, guiding the directional migration of tracheal branches. However, this study found that Bnl is transported through specialized structures called cytonemes, which extend from the air-sac-primordium (ASP) to the wing disc, where Bnl is produced. The number of cytonemes decreases with increasing distance from the source, generating a gradient of Bnl that is specific to the recipient tissue.

The study used fluorescently-tagged Bnl and its receptor, FGFR, expressed at physiological levels in Drosophila larvae. The results showed that Bnl produced in the wing disc moves through FGFR-containing cytonemes to the ASP, where it activates concentration-dependent gene expression. The transcription factors Pointed-P1 and Cut, which antagonize each other, differentially regulate the formation of FGFR-containing cytonemes, creating regions with higher-to-lower numbers of Bnl-receiving cytonemes.

This feedback regulation of cytoneme-mediated transport allows the Bnl gradient to self-generate precise tissue-specific contours. The study demonstrates that morphogens like Bnl can dynamically adapt to diverse tissue morphologies, providing precision and adaptability in shaping long-range positional gradients and tissue architectures in space and time. These findings have significant implications for our understanding of tissue development and morphogenesis, and highlight the importance of cytoneme-mediated signaling in generating complex tissue patterns.

Summary
     Epsin is a protein that plays a crucial role in the process of endocytosis, which is the uptake of molecules into cells through the formation of vesicles. Endocytosis is essential for various cellular functions, including nutrient uptake, cell signaling, and waste removal. However, the exact function of epsin in endocytosis has remained elusive.

     To investigate the role of epsin in endocytosis, researchers generated embryonic fibroblasts from mice lacking all three epsin proteins (epsin triple knockout, or TKO). These cells displayed a significant defect in cell division and a robust impairment in clathrin-mediated endocytosis. Clathrin-coated pits, which are the precursors to endocytic vesicles, accumulated and failed to invaginate properly in epsin TKO cells.

     The researchers found that epsin helps to couple the clathrin coat to the actin cytoskeleton, which is essential for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits. In a cell-free assay, they confirmed that epsin is required for the proper coupling between the clathrin coat and actin. This suggests that epsin generates the force necessary for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits.

     The study also revealed a weak interaction between epsin's ENTH domain and the SNARE protein synaptobrevin 2/VAMP2, which is involved in vesicle fusion. However, the researchers found that epsin's most critical function is at the transition between shallow and deep invaginated pits, rather than in the fusion process.

     The findings of this study provide conclusive evidence for the role of epsin in clathrin-mediated endocytosis and highlight the importance of epsin in generating the force necessary for the invagination and fission of clathrin-coated pits. The study also suggests that epsin's function is distinct from other endocytic proteins, such as clathrin, which have been implicated in the organization of mitotic scaffolds. Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms of endocytosis and the role of epsin in this process.

summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the molecular mechanisms underlying the formation of the striped pattern in adult zebrafish (Danio rerio). The striped pattern is a result of interactions between three types of pigment cells: melanophores, xanthophores, and iridophores. Mutations in the leopard (leo) gene, which encodes Connexin 41.8 (Cx41.8), a gap junction subunit, cause a series of spotted patterns in zebrafish. A new dominant allele, leotK3, leads to a complete loss of the pattern, suggesting a dominant negative impact on another component of gap junctions.

     Further investigation identified Connexin 39.4 (Cx39.4), encoded by the luchs gene, as the additional component. Loss-of-function alleles of luchs demonstrate that it is required for stripe formation in zebrafish, but the fins are almost unaffected. Double mutants and chimeras show that leo and luchs are only required in xanthophores and melanophores, but not in iridophores. This suggests that both connexins form heteromeric gap junctions, which promote homotypic interactions between melanophores and xanthophores, respectively, and instruct the patterning of iridophores.

     Fish homozygous for leotK3 or trans-heterozygous for leotK3 over leot1 show an identical phenotype of a completely dissolved pattern. Individual melanophores that hardly cluster together are still present, mostly associated with blue iridophores. This indicates that the dramatic expansion of dense iridophores is an indirect effect, possibly caused by mutant xanthophores covering the iridophores.

     The study suggests that Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 interact to form heteromeric gap junctions, which are responsible for some of the interactions that take place among chromatophores while the pigment pattern is established. The findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the formation of the striped pattern in zebrafish and highlight the importance of gap junctions in pigment cell interactions.
summary
     The evolution of insect olfactory receptors has been a topic of interest in understanding the development of the insect sense of smell. Insects use various types of receptors to detect volatile molecules, including olfactory receptors (ORs), ionotropic receptors (IRs), and gustatory receptors (GRs). While IRs are found in olfactory organs across Protostomia, ORs have been hypothesized to be an adaptation to a terrestrial insect lifestyle. However, a recent study investigated the olfactory system of three ancient insects, Lepismachilis y-signata (Archaeognatha), Thermobia domestica (Zygentoma), and Phyllium siccifolium (Phasmatodea), to shed light on the evolution of ORs.

     The study found that ORs and the olfactory coreceptor (Orco) are likely lacking in Lepismachilis, while Thermobia has three Orco candidates and Phyllium has a fully developed OR/Orco-based system. This suggests that ORs did not arise as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle, but evolved later in insect evolution, with Orco being present before the appearance of ORs. The study also suggests that the evolution of the coreceptor Orco occurred after the bristletails split from their last common ancestor with the remaining insects.

     Insect olfactory receptors function as heteromultimers composed of at least one ligand-specific OR and the coreceptor Orco. The study used a combination of approaches to investigate which receptors are involved in odor detection in these insects and in basal insects in general. In neopteran insects, most volatile stimuli are recognized by members of the olfactory receptor family (ORs), which are multitransmembrane domain proteins unrelated to nematode or vertebrate olfactory receptors. The study's findings provide new insights into the evolution of the insect olfactory system and suggest that ORs evolved later in insect evolution, with Orco being present before the appearance of ORs. This research contributes to our understanding of the development of the insect sense of smell and its evolution over time.
summary
     Biomedical researchers conducted a study in Myanmar to investigate the effectiveness of mass antimalarial drug administration (MDA) in reducing the spread of Plasmodium falciparum malaria. The study took place in four villages along the Myanmar-Thailand border, where MDA was implemented to interrupt malaria transmission. The researchers analyzed data on malaria episodes and asymptomatic infections in the villages before and after MDA.

     The results showed that individuals living in neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had a 2.85 times higher risk of experiencing a malaria episode after MDA compared to those living in neighborhoods with high adherence. This suggests a "herd effect," where the protection provided by MDA extends beyond individual participants to the broader community. The study also found that high mosquito biting rates, living with someone who has malaria, or having an asymptomatic malaria infection increased the risk of clinical episodes.

     The researchers observed a decrease in clinical episodes among all groups for a longer period than the prophylactic effect of the administered antimalarials, indicating a group-level effect from MDA adherence. This is the first documentation of a herd effect conferred by MDA for P. falciparum malaria. The increase in clinical P. falciparum episodes post-MDA also corresponded to an increase in village mosquito biting rates.

     The study highlights the importance of adequate population participation in MDA to achieve herd protection. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high overall participation, may hinder elimination efforts. The findings provide an evidence base for further research, intervention, and policy work to support malaria elimination efforts. The study's results suggest that MDA can have a broader impact on reducing malaria transmission than previously thought, making it a valuable tool in the fight against the disease.
Summary
     Biologists have long sought to understand how the genetic differences between species contribute to their distinct physical characteristics. One key factor is the expression of proteins in specific tissues, which can be influenced by the binding of transcription factors (TFs) to DNA. However, it has been challenging to determine the importance of TF binding specificity in this process, as previous studies used different methods in different species.

     To address this, researchers analyzed the binding specificities of 242 transcription factors in Drosophila (fruit flies) and compared them to data from humans and mice. The study found that TF binding specificities are highly conserved between Drosophila and mammals, with similarities extending to subtle dinucleotide binding preferences. This suggests that TF binding specificities are not subject to significant evolutionary drift.

     The researchers used a technique called HT-SELEX to determine TF binding specificities in Drosophila melanogaster. They found that TFs that belong to the same structural family generally bind to similar sites, and that orthologous TFs have almost identical core DNA binding profiles, flanking sequences, and secondary modes of binding. This level of conservation is striking, with fruit flies having almost as complex a repertoire of TF binding motifs as humans.

     The study also identified TFs with divergent specificities that function in cell types not found in fruit flies. This suggests that the evolution of TF specificities contributes to the emergence of novel types of differentiated cells. The researchers conclude that TF binding specificities are highly conserved across 600 million years of bilateria evolution, and that this conservation is not limited to core DNA recognition motifs, but also extends to secondary modes of binding and subtle dinucleotide preferences.

     The study provides new insights into the evolution of TF DNA-binding characteristics and sheds light on the role of divergence and subtle drift in TF DNA binding specificity in animal diversity. The dataset also extends previous models by providing 122 novel high-resolution motifs for Drosophila TF binding specificities. Overall, the study highlights the importance of TF binding specificity in the evolution of species and its contribution to the emergence of novel cell types.
Scientists have developed a new method to study how the heart and lungs develop together in the human body. This process, called cardio-pulmonary co-development, is critical for the proper formation and maturation of these organs. However, there has been a lack of models to investigate this complex interaction during human embryogenesis.

     Researchers used human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs), which can be directed to become any cell type in the body, to create a model of cardio-pulmonary co-development. They used a stepwise strategy to induce the simultaneous differentiation of cardiac and lung epithelial lineages from hiPSCs. This was achieved by tuning the WNT and nodal signaling pathways in the absence of external growth factors.

     The resulting cells were then cultured in a 3D suspension system, where they formed concentric cardio-pulmonary micro-tissues (Î¼Ts). These Î¼Ts effectively segregated into separate cardiac and pulmonary components, which was partially inhibited by WNT activation. The cardiac component promoted the maturation of alveoli, the air sacs in the lungs, within 3 days.

     When the WNT agonist was withdrawn, the cardiac and pulmonary components within each Î¼T segregated from each other, and cardiac contraction was initiated. This model offers an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis.

     The study demonstrates that the simultaneous induction of cardiac and pulmonary lineages from hiPSCs can be achieved using a temporal-specific tuning of WNT and nodal signaling. This model can be used to study the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying human cardio-pulmonary co-development and tissue boundary formation. Further investigation is needed to explore the morphological and functional maturation of both cardiac and pulmonary lineages and their crosstalk during this process.
summary
     Biomedical research has long considered the mutation rate of an organism to be a slowly-evolving parameter, strictly regulated to avoid negatively impacting fitness. However, a recent study on Escherichia coli (E. coli) has revealed an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates as a response to changes in selective pressure. The study found that when E. coli cultures adapt to high ethanol stress, hypermutation independently evolves, allowing the cells to rapidly adapt to the stressful environment.

     The research demonstrated that hypermutator states are transitory and repeatedly alternate with decreases in mutation rate. Specifically, population mutation rates rise when cells experience higher stress and decline again once cells are adapted. The study identified cellular mortality as the major force driving the quick evolution of mutation rates. This finding helps explain the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, including the emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy.

     The study used E. coli exposed to high ethanol stress as a model system to investigate the dynamics of hypermutation under near-lethal, complex stress. The researchers found that multiple mutations epistatically interact and diverse evolutionary trajectories can lead to adaptation to high ethanol concentrations. However, in addition to the increase in mutation rate, the study revealed that hypermutator states are transitory and mutation rates decrease again once cells are adapted to the stressful environment.

     The study's findings shed new light on the dynamics of mutation rate evolution and help explain why maintaining high mutation rates is limited in time. The research also identified cellular mortality as a major modulator of the population mutation rate. The study's results have implications for our understanding of how organisms balance robustness and evolvability, and provide new insights into the mechanisms underlying the evolution of mutation rates in response to stressful environments.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a key regulator of the low-energy response in plants, known as the transcription factor bZIP63. This protein is activated by the SnRK1 kinase, which is a metabolic master regulator that helps plants adjust to changing environmental conditions. The SnRK1 kinase phosphorylates bZIP63, causing it to change its dimerization preference, which in turn affects the expression of target genes involved in primary metabolism.

     The study found that bZIP63 is a direct target of the SnRK1 kinase and plays a crucial role in the starvation response in Arabidopsis plants. Phosphorylation of bZIP63 by SnRK1 is necessary for its function, as a mutant version of bZIP63 that cannot be phosphorylated by SnRK1 was unable to complement the metabolic and senescence phenotypes of a bzip63 knockout mutant.

     The researchers also identified the SnRK1 kinase AKIN10 as the major kinase responsible for the starvation-induced hyper-phosphorylation of bZIP63. They found that bZIP63 is phosphorylated at multiple sites in vivo in a sugar- and energy-dependent manner, and that this phosphorylation is crucial for its dimerization and activity in plants.

     The study proposes a molecular model for a phosphorylation-triggered switch of bZIP63 dimerization partners, which ultimately regulates metabolic reprogramming. The findings suggest that bZIP63 is an important metabolic regulator, especially under stress/starvation conditions, and that its phosphorylation by SnRK1 is essential for its function.

     The researchers used an unbiased approach to identify SnRK1 as one of the kinases responsible for bZIP63 phosphorylation, and found that it targets three highly conserved serine residues in the N- and C-terminus of bZIP63. They also demonstrated that the phosphorylation of these sites is crucial for bZIP63's dimerization and activity in planta.

     Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the low-energy response in plants and highlights the importance of bZIP63 as a key regulator of metabolic reprogramming in response to changing environmental conditions. The findings have implications for our understanding of plant metabolism and stress responses, and may lead to the development of new strategies for improving crop yields and stress tolerance in plants.
Summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a novel protein called ring finger protein 10 (RNF10) that acts as a messenger between synapses and the nucleus in the hippocampus, a region of the brain involved in learning and memory. RNF10 is activated by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment, where it associates with the GluN2A subunit of NMDA receptors (NMDARs). When NMDARs are activated and long-term potentiation (LTP) is induced, RNF10 is translocated from dendritic segments and dendritic spines to the nucleus. This process is mediated by importin-dependent long-distance transport from synapto-dendritic compartments to the nucleus.

     RNF10 plays a crucial role in the maintenance of LTP and structural modifications of dendritic spines, which are essential for learning and memory. Silencing RNF10 prevents the maintenance of LTP and structural modifications of dendritic spines, indicating that RNF10 is necessary for these processes. The researchers found that RNF10 dissociates from the NMDAR complex in an activity-dependent manner and is transported to the nucleus through importin-dependent long-distance transport.

     The study suggests that RNF10 is a novel synaptonuclear protein messenger that encodes activation of NMDA receptors in the hippocampus. RNF10 is highly expressed in the nucleus as well as at synapses, where it directly interacts with the cytoplasmic tail of the GluN2A subunit of NMDARs. Activation of synaptic GluN2A-containing NMDARs and induction of LTP lead to the translocation of RNF10 from dendritic segments and dendritic spines to the nucleus, where it can regulate gene expression.

     The researchers propose that RNF10-mediated synaptonuclear signaling is essential for the long-lasting re-shaping of dendritic spines and the induction of LTP. The study highlights the importance of NMDAR complex as a source of protein messengers that can traffic to the nucleus and regulate gene expression in response to synaptic activity. The findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying synaptic plasticity and learning and memory.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand the mechanisms by which Swi2/Snf2 ATPases remodel protein-DNA complexes to regulate chromatin structure and epigenetic states during transcription, DNA replication, and DNA repair. The single-subunit remodeler Mot1, which dissociates TATA box-binding protein (TBP):DNA complexes, has emerged as a useful model system for studying Swi2/Snf2 enzymes. To elucidate the structural basis for recognition and remodeling of the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex by Mot1, researchers have determined the crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of Mot1 in complex with TBP, DNA, and the transcription regulator negative cofactor 2 (NC2) at 3.8 Ã resolution.

     The study reveals that Mot1 reduces DNA:NC2 interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP:DNA:NC2 state, suggesting that Mot1 primes TBP:NC2 displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking data suggest that the Swi2/Snf2 domain of Mot1 associates with the upstream DNA and the histone fold of NC2, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers. This structural framework provides insights into how a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase interacts with its substrate DNA:protein complex.

     Swi2/Snf2-type ATPases, including Mot1, remodel protein-DNA complexes by disrupting the interface between the nucleic and protein partners of the target complex. The association of Mot1 with the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex results in some unexpected conformational changes, which may have implications for understanding the ATP-dependent dissociation mechanism. The study provides the first pseudoatomic view of a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase in complex with a DNA:protein substrate complex, offering a detailed structural framework for the interaction of Mot1 with the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex.

     The researchers used biochemical, electron microscopy, and cross-linking studies of the full-length Mot1 complex to complement the crystal structure of the Mot1 N-terminal domain in complex with TBP, NC2, and a TATA promoter DNA fragment. The study provides a structural basis for understanding the recognition and remodeling of the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex by Mot1, shedding light on the mechanisms of Swi2/Snf2 ATPases and their role in regulating chromatin structure and epigenetic states.
Scientists have developed synthetic variants of a natural compound called ternatin, which has been shown to be toxic to cancer cells. Ternatin was previously known to inhibit the growth of fat cells, but its mechanism of action was unknown. The researchers created new versions of ternatin with improved potency, up to 500 times more effective than the original compound. They used a technique called photo-affinity labeling to identify the target of ternatin, which is a complex of a protein called elongation factor-1A (eEF1A), GTP, and aminoacyl-tRNA. This complex is essential for protein synthesis in cells.

The researchers found that mutations in a specific region of the eEF1A protein prevented ternatin from binding and made cells resistant to its toxic effects. They also discovered that two other natural compounds, didemnin and cytotrienin, compete with ternatin for binding to the eEF1A ternary complex. This suggests that the eEF1A ternary complex is a common target for natural products that inhibit protein synthesis.

The study shows that ternatin and its variants inhibit protein synthesis, which correlates with their ability to block cell proliferation. The researchers identified the eEF1A ternary complex as a direct target of ternatin-related cyclic peptides. This is a significant finding, as specific binding of a small molecule to the eEF1A ternary complex has not been reported before.

The discovery of the eEF1A ternary complex as a target for ternatin and its variants has implications for the development of new cancer therapies. The researchers suggest that ternatins may be attractive leads for future medicinal chemistry efforts due to their relative structural simplicity and accessibility by total synthesis. Further studies are needed to understand the mechanism of action of ternatin and its variants and to explore their potential as cancer treatments.
Summary
     Biomedical research has shed light on the role of OCRL in clathrin-coated pit dynamics and uncoating in Lowe syndrome cells. Lowe syndrome is a genetic disorder caused by mutations in the OCRL gene, which encodes an inositol 5-phosphatase that interacts directly with clathrin. Previous studies have primarily attributed the clinical manifestations of Lowe syndrome to intracellular sorting defects. However, recent studies have revealed that OCRL plays a crucial role in clathrin-mediated endocytosis, a process by which cells internalize molecules and proteins from the cell surface.

The research found that the absence of OCRL in Lowe syndrome patient fibroblasts impairs endocytic clathrin coat dynamics, resulting in an endocytic defect. These cells accumulate clathrin-coated vesicles that fail to lose their coat, leading to the formation of U-shaped clathrin-coated pits. Additionally, the study discovered that SNX9, a clathrin-binding protein associated with late-stage endocytic clathrin-coated pits, directly binds to OCRL. This interaction suggests that OCRL acts as an uncoating factor, facilitating the recycling of endocytic factors required for the normal progression of endocytosis.

The study used time-lapse microscopy to analyze clathrin-coated pit dynamics in patient and control cells. The results showed that Lowe syndrome fibroblasts exhibit an accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and an increase in U-shaped clathrin-coated pits, which may result from the sequestration of coat components on uncoated vesicles. Furthermore, the study found that endocytic vesicles that fail to lose their coat nucleate the majority of the numerous actin comets present in patient cells.

These findings indicate that defects in clathrin-mediated endocytosis likely contribute to the pathology in patients with OCRL mutations. The study provides new insights into the role of OCRL in clathrin-coated pit dynamics and uncoating, highlighting the importance of this process in maintaining normal cellular function. The results suggest that the lack of OCRL impairs the completion of the endocytic reaction, leading to the accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and the formation of actin comets. Overall, this research contributes to our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying Lowe syndrome and may have implications for the development of therapeutic strategies for this disorder.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have identified a new pathway that contributes to the development of colorectal cancer. The Wnt/Î²-catenin signaling pathway is known to play a significant role in colorectal cancer, but finding effective inhibitors for this pathway has been a challenge. A recent study has shed light on the regulation of this pathway by revealing a novel FAK/PYK2/GSK3Î²Y216/Î²-catenin regulatory circuit.

     The study found that FAK (focal adhesion kinase) and PYK2 (proline-rich tyrosine kinase 2) are elevated in adenomas in mice and human colorectal cancer tissues. These proteins function redundantly to promote the Wnt/Î²-catenin pathway by phosphorylating GSK3Î² (glycogen synthase kinase 3 beta) at the Y216 site. This phosphorylation event reinforces the pathway output, leading to the accumulation of Î²-catenin and intestinal tumorigenesis.

     The study also showed that the phosphorylation of GSK3Î²Y216 is a molecular determinant of GSK3Î² recruitment of Î²-TrCP (Î²-transducin repeat-containing protein), which is necessary for Wnt-induced Î²-catenin accumulation. Pharmacological inhibition of FAK/PYK2 using the inhibitor PF-562271 suppressed adenoma formation in APCmin/+ mice, accompanied by reduced levels of phospho-GSK3Î²Y216 and Î²-catenin. These results indicate that the FAK/PYK2/GSK3Î²Y216 axis is critical for the activation of Wnt/Î²-catenin signaling in APC-driven intestinal tumorigenesis.

     Furthermore, the study found that the intestinal level of p-GSK3Î²Y216 was higher in APCmin/+ mice than in control mice, and that PF-562271 treatment suppressed GSK3Î²Y216 phosphorylation and GSK3Î² ubiquitination. These results strongly support the role of FAK/PYK2 kinase in APC-driven intestinal tumorigenesis through the phosphorylation of GSK3Î²Y216 to promote Wnt/Î²-catenin signaling.

     The study's findings suggest that the loss of functional APC induces the expression of FAK and PYK2, which in turn phosphorylate GSK3Î²Y216 to stabilize Î²-catenin, initiating and supporting intestinal tumorigenesis. The phosphorylation of GSK3Î²Y216 has been known for about two decades, but the underlying mechanisms remained unclear. The study's results provide new insights into the regulation of the Wnt/Î²-catenin pathway and offer a potential therapeutic target for the treatment of colorectal cancer.
summary
     Biomedical research has investigated the neural control of transitions between posture and movement, focusing on the regulation of reflex-stabilizing mechanisms to enable motion. A key aspect of this process is the disengagement of one motor control policy before the implementation of another, as suggested by optimal feedback theory. To test this possibility, researchers examined the continuity of vestibular control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion, as well as between two standing postures.

The study involved healthy subjects who initiated and terminated locomotion or shifted the distribution of their weight between their feet while exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli (EVS). The relationship between EVS and ground reaction forces was quantified using time-frequency analyses. The results showed that discontinuities corresponding to null coherence periods were observed preceding the onset of movement initiation and during the step preceding locomotion termination.

These findings indicate that humans interrupt the vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states, suggesting a discrete change between motor control policies. The vestibular system, which encodes motion of the head in space, plays a vital role in maintaining stability during both quiet standing and locomotion. Vestibular signals influence the activation of muscles engaged in the control of posture.

To transition between two motor states, balance correcting responses are disrupted, and the vestibular influence on whole-body balance responses is suspended. This suspension occurs while subjects are still maintaining quiet upright stance, well before the transition onset. The suppression of vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms is not specific to one type of transition, suggesting it represents a general control process that occurs during transitions between standing balance and other motor states.

The period of suspension of vestibular-evoked responses appears related to the different relative sensory feedback gains needed to control whole-body movements by the quiet standing and locomotor control policies. The vestibular control of balance during locomotor or posture-to-posture transitions can be monitored by continuously quantifying the magnitude of vestibular-evoked balance responses over the transition. This approach has been used previously to monitor the time course of engagement and disengagement of the vestibular control of balance between self- and externally-driven states of balance control. The results demonstrate that humans "stop balancing" before they start moving and "stop moving" before they start balancing again.
summary
     In cyanobacteria, the circadian output pathway plays a crucial role in regulating metabolic programs in response to light availability. The transcription factor RpaA is the master regulator of circadian transcription, driving genome-wide oscillations in mRNA abundance. Research has shown that deletion of rpaA has no effect on viability in constant light conditions, but renders cells inviable in cycling conditions when light and dark periods alternate. This study investigated the mechanisms underlying this viability defect and found that the rpaA- strain cannot maintain appropriate energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes crucial for utilization of carbohydrate stores at night.

     The study demonstrated that both accumulation and utilization of carbon reserves required for energy production during periods of darkness are defective in the rpaA- strain, and that correction of these defects restores viability. The results provide insight into the role of the circadian output pathway in enhancing fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central carbon metabolism with the metabolic demands imposed by periodic changes in the external environment.

     The research suggests that functional circadian output mediated by the activity of RpaA is required for the anticipatory accumulation of carbon reserves during the day, as well as metabolic switching to carbon catabolism at dusk. These metabolic adaptations allow cells to stimulate energy production and sustain cell viability during periods of darkness that challenge and constrain resources for cyanobacterial growth.

     Exposure to darkness restricts energy availability, creating unique metabolic demands in cyanobacteria that depend on sunlight for energy production through photosynthesis. The rpaA- strain exhibits defects in cell growth and viability in cyclic, but not in constant light environments, suggesting an important role for circadian regulation of gene expression in alternating light/dark cycles.

     The study found that the phosphorylated form of the transcription factor RpaA, regulated by the circadian clock, is critical for the coordination of carbon anabolism and catabolism and thus cell viability in light/dark cycles. Deletion of rpaA prevents glycogen accumulation in light and renders cells unable to utilize existing glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in cellular energy charge and a severe reduction in fitness.

     The research supports a model in which anticipation of periodic dark-induced resource limitation is one essential role of the cyanobacterial circadian system. The output of the core oscillator, through regulation of RpaA activity, temporally coordinates metabolism to prepare cells for the metabolic demands of periods of darkness. The study highlights the importance of the circadian output pathway in controlling and temporally coordinating essential pathways in carbon metabolism to maximize fitness of cells facing periodic energy limitations.

Biomedical researchers have identified a chromatin signature that accurately distinguishes genes subject to monoallelic expression (MAE) in human cells. MAE is a phenomenon where one allele of a gene is expressed while the other allele remains silent, and it plays a critical role in various human diseases. However, the regulation of MAE has been challenging to study due to its clonal nature.

     The researchers developed an approach to identify MAE genes using chromatin marks associated with active transcription (H3K36me3) and silencing (H3K27me3) simultaneously occurring in the gene body. This chromatin signature was found to be present in approximately 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and over 30% of tissue-specific genes across various cell types.

     Notably, the MAE signature was enriched among key developmental genes that have bivalent chromatin structure in pluripotent cells. The study suggests that MAE is linked to cell differentiation and opens a new approach to studying MAE that is independent of polymorphisms.

     The researchers applied this approach to various cell types and estimated that up to 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and more than one-third of tissue-specific genes showed the chromatin signature of MAE. The detection of the MAE signature does not rely on single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) or sample clonality, making it feasible to analyze MAE in primary samples, including systematic comparisons of normal and diseased tissue.

     The study also found that genes with the MAE chromatin signature share some pronounced features as a group. Importantly, the MAE identified in clones is reflected by the chromatin signature in a polyclonal sample. The researchers identified novel genes with the MAE chromatin pattern and assessed their allelic bias in monoclonal cell lines.

     The study revealed that a remarkably large fraction of known bivalent genes acquire the MAE chromatin signature in at least one of the differentiated cell types, much higher than other genes that are also silent in embryonic stem cells. This suggests that upon activation, these master regulator genes preferentially resolve into a state with the characteristic MAE chromatin signature. The findings of this study provide new insights into the regulation of MAE and its link to cell differentiation.
summary
     Biomedical research has revealed a novel mechanism of activity control for the AAA+ chaperone ClpC, a protein essential for bacterial virulence and stress resistance. ClpC forms a protease complex with the peptidase ClpP, and its activity is regulated by the adaptor protein MecA, which targets substrates and stimulates ClpC's ATPase activity. In its ground state, ClpC is repressed by forming large two-helical assemblies through head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil middle domains (MDs). These MDs sequester ClpC molecules in an inactive resting state, restricting substrate binding and ATP hydrolysis.

     The coiled-coil MDs play a crucial role in controlling ClpC activity, and their loss leads to constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity. This regulatory mechanism is unique compared to other AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpB/Hsp104, which use a repressing belt formed by a long coiled-coil MD to reduce ATPase activity. In contrast, ClpC MDs form intermolecular head-to-head contacts, allowing docking of two layers of ClpC molecules in a helical conformation.

     The formation of this structure involves ATP binding to AAA domains and restricts substrate binding, ClpP, and adaptor protein binding, preventing efficient ATP hydrolysis. Mutating a key regulatory MD residue of ClpE, another bacterial AAA+ chaperone, also causes cellular toxicity, suggesting that the repressing mode of MDs is a general mechanism for controlling bacterial AAA+ chaperone systems. The severe toxicity of ClpC and ClpE MD mutants qualifies these AAA+ chaperones as potential targets for antimicrobial therapies.

     This study provides new insights into the regulation of AAA+ chaperones and highlights the importance of coiled-coil MDs in controlling protein activity. The findings have implications for understanding the mechanisms of bacterial virulence and stress resistance and may lead to the development of novel antimicrobial strategies.
summary
     Biomedical researchers have long sought to understand how stem cells remain in a dormant state and how they are reactivated. To address this question, a team of scientists used the fruit fly Drosophila as a model organism to study the behavior of adult muscle precursors (AMPs), the stem cells responsible for muscle growth and regeneration in adult flies. The researchers found that AMPs send out thin filopodia that make contact with neighboring muscles, suggesting that muscles may play a role in AMP reactivation.

     Further investigation revealed that muscles send signals to AMPs through the Insulin pathway, which activates the Notch signaling pathway. Notch, in turn, regulates the activity of dMyc, a protein that promotes cell proliferation. The researchers found that dMyc is essential for AMP reactivation and proliferation, and that Notch acts downstream of the Insulin pathway and dMyc functions downstream of Notch.

     These findings suggest that muscles serve as a niche for AMPs, providing the necessary signals for their reactivation. The Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade plays a key role in setting the activated state of AMPs, allowing them to proliferate and contribute to muscle growth and regeneration. This research provides new insights into the mechanisms governing stem cell behavior and has implications for our understanding of muscle development and regeneration in both Drosophila and vertebrates.

     The study also highlights the conserved nature of the Insulin-Notch-dMyc pathway across species, with dMyc acting as a positive regulator of AMP reactivation in Drosophila, similar to its role in vertebrates. The researchers propose a scheme illustrating the muscle niche-induced Insulin/Notch/dMyc cascade governing the reactivation of dormant AMPs, providing a framework for further investigation into the complex interactions between stem cells and their niche.
summary
     Biomedical research has identified the sodium leak channel NALCN as a crucial component in the functioning of substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr) neurons. SNr neurons are a type of GABAergic neuron that fire spontaneously at high rates, providing tonic inhibition to downstream brain areas. A depolarizing "leak" current supports this firing pattern, but the molecular basis of this current remained poorly understood until now. Using single-cell RNA sequencing, researchers determined the transcriptome of individual mouse SNr neurons and found that NALCN is the highest expressed nonselective cation channel (NSCC) in these cells.

     The study found that SNr neurons lacking NALCN have impaired spontaneous firing, suggesting that NALCN is essential for maintaining tonic activity in these neurons. Furthermore, NALCN is involved in the modulation of excitability by changes in glycolysis and by activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. The researchers also discovered that NALCN interacts with other proteins, including Unc80, Unc79, and Fam155a, which are known to interact with NALCN.

     The findings of this study suggest that disruption of NALCN could impair the basal ganglia circuit, which may underlie the severe motor deficits in humans carrying mutations in NALCN. The basal ganglia circuit plays a critical role in movement control, and dysfunction of this circuit has been implicated in various neurological disorders, including Parkinson's disease. The identification of NALCN as a key component of SNr neurons provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying basal ganglia function and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for treating movement disorders.

     The study used single-cell RNA sequencing to determine the gene expression profile of SNr neurons and identified several NSCCs that could potentially generate the tonic depolarizing current. NALCN stood out as a prime candidate for the channel carrying the depolarizing leak current in these cells. The researchers found that SNr neurons require NALCN for appropriate tonic firing, suggesting that NALCN mutations could contribute to basal ganglia-related motor disorders. Disruption of the normal tonic firing of SNr neurons by injection of GABA receptor agonists leads to excessive muscle tension and dystonia, while hyperactivity of the SNr is observed in Parkinsonian animals.
Scientists have developed a new method to cultivate and identify microorganisms that produce antibiotics, which are essential for treating bacterial infections but are becoming increasingly ineffective due to antibiotic resistance. Conventional methods for cultivating microorganisms are limited by their low throughput and sensitivity, making it difficult to access the vast majority of microbial diversity. To address this issue, researchers created a droplet-based microscale cultivation system that allows for high-throughput screening of antimicrobial activity prior to strain isolation.

The system consists of tiny droplets, each containing a single microorganism, which are cultivated in a growth substrate designed to mimic environmental conditions. This approach enables the cultivation of previously uncultured species and increases bacterial diversity compared to traditional agar plate cultivation. The system also allows for the detection of antimicrobial compounds produced by the microorganisms, which can be isolated and characterized.

The researchers verified their method by culturing a complex bacterial community from a soil sample and comparing the results to classical agar plate cultivation. They identified and characterized the secreted metabolites of one isolate that showed antimicrobial activity in the droplet screening. The microfluidic droplets provide a genotype-phenotype linkage, meaning that the compounds produced by the microorganisms remain confined to the cell from which they originated.

The combination of bacterial strain discovery during in-droplet cultivation with a high-throughput screening for antimicrobial products using bacterial reporter strains in droplets streamlines the process of discovering new antibiotics. The researchers devised an innovative microscale cultivation strategy using a modular droplet-microfluidic platform, which allows for high-density long-term cultivation of millions of single cells in a small volume. This approach increases the practical relevance of the cultivation method by combining it with a screening step to detect and select droplets containing environmental cells producing compounds with antibiotic activity.

Even though axenic cultures of putative new species could not be maintained with standard microbiological cultivation after isolation from droplets, the cells could be used to obtain high-quality genome information. This breakthrough method has the potential to revolutionize the discovery of new antibiotics and provide a solution to the growing problem of antibiotic resistance.
Tuberculous meningitis (TBM) is a severe form of tuberculosis that affects the membranes surrounding the brain and spinal cord, resulting in high mortality rates. Despite the use of adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids, which reduce mortality by only 30%, the underlying inflammatory pathophysiology of TBM remains poorly understood. Research has suggested that the leukotriene A4 hydrolase (LTA4H) gene, which regulates inflammatory eicosanoids, may play a critical role in determining the response to corticosteroid treatment.

A study in Vietnam found that patients with the LTA4H TT genotype, which is associated with increased expression of the LTA4H enzyme, had improved survival rates when treated with corticosteroids. In contrast, a study in Indonesia did not find a significant association between the LTA4H TT genotype and survival. Using Bayesian analysis, researchers re-examined both studies and found that the LTA4H TT genotype confers a survival benefit that begins early and continues long-term in both populations. However, this benefit is nullified in the most severe cases with high early mortality.

The researchers propose that LTA4H genotyping, combined with disease severity assessment, may help target glucocorticoid therapy to patients most likely to benefit from it. They suggest that personalized genotype-directed adjunctive glucocorticoid treatment may be warranted, with the drug given only to patients with the LTA4H TT genotype. The study's findings have implications for the treatment of TBM and highlight the need for further research into the role of genetic variants in determining the response to corticosteroid treatment.

One possible explanation for the limited effect of the LTA4H TT genotype in the Indonesia study is that the underlying association is overridden by severe disease, which is a strong independent correlate of mortality. Another possibility is that Indonesia patients presented with a greater degree of dysregulated inflammation, which may not be revealed by standard metrics of disease severity. The researchers suggest that the beneficial effects of dexamethasone for the LTA4H TT genotype may be nullified in more severe disease, which has precedent in other medical conditions. Overall, the study's findings provide new insights into the complex relationship between LTA4H genotype, inflammation, and response to corticosteroid treatment in TBM.
summary
     Biomedical research has long sought to understand how growth cones navigate axonal projection in response to guidance cues. A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating the role of the protein shootin1 in axon guidance. The study found that knockout mice lacking shootin1 display abnormal projection of forebrain commissural axons, a phenotype similar to that of mice lacking the axon guidance molecule netrin-1.

     Further investigation revealed that shallow gradients of netrin-1 elicit highly polarized phosphorylation of shootin1 within growth cones. This phosphorylation increases the interaction between shootin1 and the cell adhesion molecule L1-CAM, promoting F-actin-adhesion coupling and the generation of forces necessary for growth cone migration. The study demonstrated that the spatially regulated phosphorylation of shootin1 within growth cones is required for axon turning induced by netrin-1 gradients.

     The research also showed that shootin1a is expressed at high levels in developing forebrain commissural axons and that its knockout leads to dysgenesis and misprojection of these axons. Moreover, the study found that netrin-1-elicited shootin1a phosphorylation promotes direct interaction between shootin1a and L1-CAM, generating traction force for growth cone motility. Disturbing the spatially regulated shootin1a phosphorylation within growth cones inhibited axon turning, but not axon outgrowth, induced by netrin-1 gradients.

     Additionally, the study demonstrated that uncoupling of F-actin-adhesion coupling by shootin1a (1-125) inhibited netrin-1-induced axon outgrowth and turning on laminin. These findings indicate that netrin-1-induced axon guidance, mediated by the shootin1a-L1-CAM interaction, is not limited to growth cones on L1-CAM. Overall, the study defines a mechano-effector for netrin-1 signaling and demonstrates that shootin1 phosphorylation is a critical readout for netrin-1 gradients that results in a directional mechanoresponse for axon guidance.
Scientists have long been fascinated by the unique humming sound produced by hummingbirds in flight, but the exact mechanisms behind this phenomenon have remained unclear. A recent study aimed to shed light on this mystery by investigating the relationship between the aerodynamic forces generated by a hummingbird's flapping wings and the acoustic properties of its hum. The researchers used a combination of advanced measurement techniques, including a 2176-microphone array and a new aerodynamic force platform, to collect data from six freely hovering Anna's hummingbirds.

     The study found that the hummingbird's hum is primarily generated by the oscillating lift and drag forces on its wings, which produce higher harmonics that contribute to the characteristic timbre of the sound. The researchers developed an idealized acoustic model that integrated the aerodynamic forces with wing kinematics, which accurately predicted the acoustic properties of the hummingbird's hum. This model was corroborated by direct measurements of the 3D aerodynamic forces using the aerodynamic force platform.

     The study also compared the acoustic properties of hummingbirds with those of insects, such as mosquitoes, and found that the characteristic humming timbre and radiated power of their flapping wings originate from the higher harmonics in the aerodynamic forces that support their body weight. The researchers observed that larger birds tend to be quieter, while elongated flies tend to be louder, due to allometric deviations in their wing shapes and sizes.

     The study's findings have significant implications for our understanding of the complex bioacoustic behavior of animals in flight. The researchers' use of advanced measurement techniques and modeling approaches has provided new insights into the relationship between aerodynamic forces and acoustic properties, which can inform the development of more accurate models of animal flight and sound production. The study's results also highlight the importance of considering the interplay between aerodynamics and acoustics in understanding the unique sounds produced by animals in flight.
Scientists have long debated the roles of chance, contingency, and necessity in the process of evolution. To address this question, researchers used a combination of ancestral protein reconstruction and a new continuous evolution technology to study the evolution of proteins in the B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) family. This family of proteins plays a crucial role in regulating cell death and survival in animals.

The study found that over long periods of time, contingency, or the unpredictable nature of historical events, played a significant role in shaping the evolution of BCL-2 family proteins. This is in contrast to the idea that necessity, or the physical and chemical properties of the proteins, and chance, or random mutations, were the primary drivers of evolution. The researchers found that even under strong and identical selection pressures, trajectories of evolution launched from different ancestral proteins yielded few common mutations.

The study suggests that the sequences of present-day BCL-2 family proteins are idiosyncratic products of their particular and unpredictable histories. This means that the sequences of these proteins reflect the interaction of accumulated chance events during their descent from common ancestors with the necessity imposed by physics, chemistry, and natural selection.

The researchers used a laboratory setting to simulate the evolution of BCL-2 family proteins under selection for new functions. They found that contingency generated by sequence change over phylogenetic timescales played a significant role in shaping the evolution of these proteins. The study also highlights the importance of considering contingency as a primary factor shaping the outcomes of evolution, alongside selection, drift, and mutation.

The results of the study indicate that the paths through sequence space leading to historical protein-protein interaction (PPI) specificities changed repeatedly during the BCL-2 family's history, even when the proteins' PPI binding profiles did not evolve. This suggests that multiple paths to a selected phenotype must be accessible with similar probabilities of being taken for chance to strongly influence the outcomes of adaptive evolution. The study's findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of other proteins and the role of contingency in shaping the diversity of life on Earth.
A recent genome-wide analysis of 48 sub-Saharan African groups has provided new insights into the structure of genetic diversity and gene-flow within the continent. By examining the combination of genetic markers along chromosomes, researchers were able to identify historical connections between different population groups due to admixture. The study found that coastal populations experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 7,000 years, while Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo speaking groups share ancestry with Central West Africans as a result of recent population expansions.

The analysis revealed that most sub-Saharan populations share ancestry with groups from outside of their current geographic region as a result of gene-flow within the last 4,000 years. This movement of alleles into new environments is relevant to studies of genetic epidemiology. The study also identified clear evidence for the movement of Eurasian ancestry back into the continent as a result of admixture over various timescales.

Previous studies, such as the African Genome Variation Project, have described the influence of Eurasian and hunter-gatherer ancestry on the population genetic history of sub-Saharan Africa. However, this new analysis provides a more detailed understanding of the extent and timing of key events and their impact on contemporary populations. The study used a haplotype-based approach to measure gene-flow between populations and relate it to potential historical movements of people into and within sub-Saharan Africa.

The results show that admixture is a common feature of human populations across the globe, and genome-wide analyses of African populations are refining previous models of the continent's history and its impact on genetic diversity. The study also highlights the importance of considering the movement of alleles into new environments when studying genotype-phenotype associations in Africa. Overall, the findings provide new insights into the complex history of human migration and admixture in sub-Saharan Africa.
