     summary
Scientists have discovered a new type of genetic control system in bacteria that uses two types of RNA molecules to regulate the production of purine nucleotides. This system, called a tandem riboswitch, consists of two separate RNA molecules that bind to different chemical signals, or ligands, to control the expression of genes involved in purine biosynthesis. The two ligands are phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate (PRPP) and guanine, which are both involved in the production of purine nucleotides.

     The researchers found that when both ligands are present, the tandem riboswitch activates the expression of genes involved in purine biosynthesis. However, when only one of the ligands is present, the riboswitch does not activate gene expression. This behavior is consistent with the function of an IMPLY Boolean logic gate, which is a type of digital logic gate that performs a specific logical operation based on the presence or absence of two inputs.

     The discovery of this tandem riboswitch is significant because it demonstrates that RNA molecules can form complex regulatory systems that can sense multiple chemical signals and control gene expression. This finding supports the hypothesis that RNA World organisms, which are thought to have existed billions of years ago, could have managed complex metabolic states without the assistance of protein regulatory factors.

     The researchers used a technique called in-line probing to study the structure and function of the tandem riboswitch. They found that the two aptamers, which are the regions of the RNA molecule that bind to the ligands, interact with each other and with the expression platform, which is the region of the RNA molecule that controls gene expression. The stability of the terminator and antiterminator stems, which are regions of the RNA molecule that regulate gene expression, depends on the relative concentrations of the two ligands.

     The discovery of the tandem guanine-PRPP riboswitch is also significant because it is the first example of two tandem aptamers with different ligand specificities that share the same expression platform. This allows for a new type of logic function to be implemented, which is not possible with single riboswitches. The researchers suggest that additional variations of two-input Boolean logic gates may be represented by tandem riboswitches that have yet to be discovered in modern cells.

     The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of the evolution of genetic control systems and the origins of life on Earth. The discovery of complex RNA regulatory systems in modern cells suggests that these systems may have evolved from simpler systems in ancient RNA World organisms. The researchers propose that RNA aptamers that selectively bound PRPP in ancient times may have persisted to serve as riboswitch aptamers in modern cells.
Summary
     The number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential is a critical determinant of synaptic transmission reliability. However, the source of variability in this fundamental property has been unclear. Researchers have long believed that the number of vesicles released is determined by the probability of vesicle release, which is influenced by the presence of calcium ions. However, a new study using mouse brain slices has found that the number of vesicles released is actually regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool (RRP) of vesicles, which is controlled by protein kinase A (PKA) and its target, synapsin.

The study demonstrates that the RRP is a functional measure of the number of release-competent vesicles per active zone, and that it constrains the number of vesicles released. This means that the number of vesicles released is determined by the size of the RRP, rather than the probability of vesicle release. The researchers found that PKA and synapsin dynamically control release site occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles released, without altering the probability of release.

The study provides new insights into the mechanisms that control multivesicular release (MVR), a process in which multiple vesicles are released with each action potential. MVR is a widespread phenomenon in the rodent central nervous system and is thought to be the dominant release mode in the human cortex. However, some synapses are limited to the release of a single vesicle per active zone, known as univesicular release (UVR).

The study's findings have important implications for our understanding of synaptic diversity and the development of unifying models of presynaptic function. The results suggest that the size of the RRP is a critical determinant of synaptic function, and that PKA and synapsin play a key role in regulating the number of vesicles released. The study's findings also highlight the importance of considering the RRP as a fundamental synaptic parameter, rather than solely focusing on the probability of vesicle release.
